Description of Impacts




10 Description of Impacts
This chapter presents the impacts assessed on the VECs due to setting up of the Project covering three phases, namely
Pre-construction, Construction and Operation & Maintenance.


10.1Introduction
Potential points of interaction between project related activities and the Valued Ecosystem Components (VECs) of the
receiving environment were initially identified using the interaction matrix described in Chapter 6 and included as
Appendix 10-A to this chapter. The baseline condition of impacted VECs is described in Chapters 7 and 8. All the
direct, indirect and cumulative impacts of the Project and its associated facilities are identified and presented in
Chapter 10 and Chapter 13 (Cumulative Impact Assessment), based on review of the Feasibility Report 2014 (Prepared
by ENEX), EIA Report 2014 (Prepared by SNC Lavalin) and Feasibility Report 2025 (Prepared by AFRY); Environmental
and Social baseline conditions in 2014 & 2024 -25 and outcome of various stakeholder consultations for the Project.

Following the identification of significant impacts associated with the Project, appropriate mitigation, management, or
compensation measures have been developed in Chapter 11.

Residual risk to the receiving environment posed by significant impacts following the application of appropriate
mitigation, management or compensation measures is then assessed by the Environmental Risk Assessment (ERA)
described in Chapter 12.

The Residual Risk is considered to be the actual risk posed by the Project to the biophysical and social components of
the receiving environment. It is only by consideration of residual risks that the authorities can make informed
decisions as to whether or not a project should be approved for construction.

Note on Environmental Flows:
Environmental flow considerations for the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project are addressed in a dedicated appendix to
this ESIA, Appendix 10-C, rather than within Chapter 10. This is because the downstream reach between Kambarata-1
and Kambarata-2 is already fully impounded by the Kambarata-2 reservoir and does not contain any free-flowing
riverine habitat or aquatic receptors requiring ecological flow releases. The Environmental Flow Impact Assessment
(EFIA) instead focuses on specific non-routine operational scenarios—such as river diversion, initial impoundment,
seasonal refilling (if reservoir levels fall below the Normal Minimum Operating Level), and emergency or planned
drawdowns—where managed releases from Kambarata-1 may be required to maintain stable water levels and
hydrodynamic conditions in the downstream Kambarata-2 reservoir. These issues are operational in nature and do not
overlap with the biophysical VEC impact assessments presented in this chapter.


10.2Method to Determine Impact Significance
Determination of whether or not an impact has the potential to be significant is not a straightforward exercise.
Significance must be assessed in the context of a number of parameters, such as the sensitivity of VECs, cultural
requirements and social acceptability.
In conducting the significance assessment for the ESIA of the K-1 HPP, a structured and transparent methodology has
been employed, adhering to the principles established by the International Association for Impact Assessment (IAIA,
2016). The assessment has defined clear, objective criteria to evaluate the significance of potential impacts, which are
based on both the physical characteristics of each impact and the context-specific values of the receiving environment.
The physical characteristics examined include magnitude, spatial extent, duration, frequency, likelihood, and
reversibility of the impacts. These characteristics are assessed in conjunction with value-based criteria, which
encompass ecological sensitivity, cultural significance, social vulnerability, implications for public health, and economic
dependence, all of which are tailored to the environmental and social components within the area of influence of the
K-1 HPP.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                     Page 1
Description of Impacts



Regulatory thresholds, alongside national and international standards, as well as insights gained from stakeholder
engagement, have played a pivotal role in forming these value judgments. Furthermore, the assessment has delineated
context-specific boundaries for each VEC, acknowledging that an impact deemed significant at a local scale, such as the
alteration of riverine habitats, may lack the same significance at a regional level, where analogous resources or
ecosystems may be more prevalent and resilient.
This nuanced, criteria-driven approach ensures that determinations of significance are both technically sound and
socially informed, thereby providing a robust foundation for decision-making and the planning of impact mitigation
strategies.
As outlined in Chapter 6, the classification system used in this assessment is based on three degrees of significance of
impact, that is:
    1.     High
    2.     Moderate
    3.     Low
Impacts which are of moderate or high significance have been identified as key issues requiring further attention in
terms of developing comprehensive mitigation, management or compensatory measures. The effectiveness of these
measures in reducing the risks associated with a potentially significant impact is assessed in the Environmental Risk in
Chapter 12.

Significance Scoring
Determining the significance of impacts is typically one of the most challenging aspects of an ESIA. Quantification of
impact magnitude is typically a scientifically objective process, based on hard data collected from primary or
secondary sources. However, once the magnitude of an impact has been assessed, the significance of an impact must
be evaluated. This requires consideration of the extent and duration of the impact, and the sensitivity and cultural
value of the VEC, this can be a somewhat more subjective process.

Assigning significance to impacts is a critical step in the ESIA process, as typically it is this information that decision
makers will rely upon when determining the fate of the Project or when considering alternatives. Significant impacts
are the major focus of mitigation measures, resource allocation, and possible modifications to the ultimate Project
design.

Established significance scoring criteria (
Table 10-1) have been used to identify potentially significant impacts. These were established in Chapter 6 using
methods described in the Nepalese National Environmental Impact Assessment Guidelines (Government of Nepal,
1994) which provide a straightforward impact scoring and ranking methodology which has been applied to HPPs by
other practitioners (Khadka, et.al. 2011).

In order to compare the relative potential significance of impacts an assessment framework has been established
which scores the magnitude, extent and duration of the impacts identified by the ESIA studies.

Table 10-1 and Table 10-2 shows the following numerical scale that has been developed to score the major
components of significance (that is, magnitude, extent and duration).

Based on the total numerical scores for magnitude, extent and duration of an impact on a VEC the relative unweighted
significance of the impacts has been categorised and colour coded as defined below.

Significance score ranges for Low, Moderate and High impacts are as follows:
     •     Low: score of 20-60
     •     Moderate: score of 70-100
     •     High: score of 110 to 140
The higher the value, the greater the effects associated with it. The impacts were then categorised and prioritised to
invest greater efforts and resources for mitigating the most significant detrimental effects.
The types of potential impacts to VECs during different phases of the project are identified in this section. For the
significance scoring purpose, the project phases have been defined as follows:
Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
In the following sections the major anticipated impacts are discussed, identified and assessed. Appendix 10-A has a
series of significance scoring tables for each section. These tables have been used identify significant impacts to VECs
associated with various phases of the Project. In these tables the significance of the potential impacts of works and
activities associated with each phase of the Project are quantified according to the perceived ‘magnitude’ ‘extent’ and
‘duration’ of an impact on each VEC, in accordance with the guidance in
Table 10-1.
Potentially significant impacts are summarised in a table at the end of each section. For the purpose of this assessment
‘significant impacts’ have been distinguished from ‘highly significant impacts.’ The former designation has been applied
to impacts of high magnitude that either occur at a local scale or can be readily mitigated. The second designation has
been reserved for impacts of high magnitude that occur at a regional scale or for which either specialised management
and/or mitigation measured need to be developed or no practical mitigation measures exist.

Specific avoidance, mitigation or compensation measures have been discussed in Mitigation Measures (Chapter 11)
and ESMP (Chapter 14).

Impacts and Interactions
One of the important issues when conducting an ESIA for an HPP that includes the formation of a reservoir is the
determination of the temporal and spatial scales of the study, considering the Area of Direct Influence of the
Reservoir, the Area of the Indirect Influence and the Area to be covered under Cumulative Impact Assessment.
Evaluation of social processes associated with reservoir construction, sites of the migrating population, economic
impacts in urban and rural areas, and the health situation both in the existing towns, rural and peri-urban regions, as
well as in the construction sites is of fundamental importance in the overall impact assessment. Land cover, studies and
its changes will provide insights into the new patterns of occupation of the landscape and its future perspectives.
The schematic in Figure 10-1 provides an overview of the potential impact pathways that are created when a storage
HPP is constructed. The ESIA must identify and evaluate the positive and negative impacts that these various
pathways generate.




                            Figure 10-1: Impact pathways of reservoir construction (Tundisi et al, 2015)




10.3Positive Impacts and Benefits
The expected benefits of constructing and operating the K-1 HPP are described in Chapter 4.1.2.2. Together with these
benefits, the proposed K-1 HPP is expected to generate a range of positive impacts across energy generation,
hydrological regulation, sediment management, groundwater, flood risk mitigation, job creation, and local economic
development, public service and infrastructure development, subject to appropriate implementation and operational
safeguards.
A key benefit of the project is its contribution to increased hydropower generation capacity within the Naryn cascade.
By enhancing seasonal flow regulation, the Kambarata-1 HPP is expected to improve the reliability of winter energy
supply, potentially reducing the volume of water required for electricity production during the low-flow season. This
could in turn, free up water at downstream reservoirs, such as Toktogul, for other beneficial uses, including irrigation
and environmental flows, supporting regional water-energy-food security objectives.


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                  Page 6
Description of Impacts



Reservoir regulation may also improve flow reliability downstream, particularly during dry periods, benefiting aquatic
ecosystems and human water users. The project introduces the opportunity to establish long-term surface water and
groundwater monitoring networks, enabling improved basin-scale water resource management.
If successfully implemented, the Catchment Area Treatment Plan (CATP) may reduce sediment loads from degraded
upstream areas. Sediment trapping within the reservoir may temporarily reduce turbidity and downstream contaminant
transport, although long-term sediment starvation risks remain and require ongoing monitoring. The bottom outlet is
not designed for sediment flushing but only for inspection purposes. A more refined sedimentation analysis will be
completed during the feasibility stage, supported by new topographical surveys of the reservoir area.
Reservoir impoundment may promote local groundwater recharge, potentially supporting spring flows and dry season
baseflows.
Kambarata‑1 will function as a near‑total sediment trap, retaining essentially all of the ~16.5 Mt/year of material
entering the reservoir. As a result, sediment delivery to the downstream Kambarata‑2 reservoir will decline sharply,
easing sedimentation pressure, removing the need for flushing operations, and extending its effective storage life. While
not a primary design objective, this cascade‑level outcome represents a clear system benefit, reducing maintenance
demands and strengthening long‑term sediment management across the Naryn cascade.
The project’s large live storage volume (3.6 km³) gives Kambarata‑1 significant capacity to attenuate flood peaks,
including the Probable Maximum Flood (PMF) and 1‑in‑100‑year design floods. With appropriate operating protocols,
this regulation will reduce downstream flood risk. Looking ahead, the reservoir is also expected to increase resilience
to extreme climate‑related events such as glacial lake outburst floods (GLOFs) and rain‑on‑snow floods. To fully realise
these benefits, basin‑wide arrangements for early warning, real‑time forecasting, and coordinated flood response will
need to be formalised with downstream operators and riparian states.
On the social front, the Project is anticipated to deliver socio-economic benefits across local, regional, and national
levels throughout both its construction and operational phases.
The construction phase will generate a substantial number of direct and indirect employment opportunities, stimulating
the local economy and service sectors. Through targeted training and skills development programs, the project will
enhance the employability of local residents, reduce labor influx, and help build a more resilient, skilled local workforce
with long-term livelihood potential.
Local economies will experience increased demand for goods and services, prompting the growth of small businesses,
expanded trade, and enhanced local markets. This economic momentum will benefit not only directly affected villages,
but also surrounding and downstream communities involved in supplying goods, labor, and services to project areas.
During operation, downstream communities may also benefit from more regulated river flows, which can support
agriculture, reduce flood risks, and improve water reliability.
The project’s benefit-sharing and community development programs will address key local priorities by improving
access to clean water, reliable electricity, and road infrastructure. These investments are critical for improving access
to health care, education, markets, and overall community well-being.
At the national level, the project is poised to contribute to energy security, support the transition to clean energy, and
bolster economic growth through expanded hydropower capacity. These outcomes will not only advance national
development goals but also help position Kyrgyz Republic as a regional leader in sustainable energy.




10.4Biophysical Impacts
Air Quality
This section presents a qualitative assessment of potential impacts on ambient air quality during all the phases of the
Kambarata-1 HPP.

The impact assessment has been conducted to cover the following phases of the project:

Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                     Page 7
Description of Impacts



Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
The screening & scoping study conducted for the project has identified the Valued Ecosystem Components (VECs) and
the Impact Area for Air Quality Impact Assessment and the same is presented below:

      • Local air quality – Impact area is envisaged to be the construction site for the dam including material stockpile
      area, batching plant, HPP and associated facilities including the quarries and borrow pits and crushing plant, upto
      2 km downwind from the sources. Workshop and the Workers’ camp(s) and 2 km downwind from the sources have
      also been considered. Access Road (From Highway to the Kambarata-1 HPP site) and the distance upto 500 m of
      the road centre line has been included within the impact area.

    •      Regional air quality - Impact area beyond 2 km from the outer periphery of the construction zones of the HPP
           and the Dam, including the associated facilities outside direct Impact Area (i.e 2 km from the sources)

Air quality impact assessment across the three phases for the identified VECs is presented in the following section.
However, prior to undertaking the impact assessment on air quality, a summary of baseline air quality is also provided.

Air quality monitoring undertaken during October 2024 (Autumn season) was used to establish the existing
characteristics of ambient air and assess the impact of the Project activities during the construction and operational
phases of the Project. The analysis of PM concentration showed values varying from 0.12±0.03 mg/m³ to 0.24±0.06
mg/m³, which is significantly lower than the established Maximum Permissible Concentrations (MPCs) (0.5 mg/m³).
Minimum dust concentrations were recorded at the sampling point between the K-1 HPP & the K-2 HPP sites (0.12±0.03
mg/m³), while the maximum was recorded at a sampling point adjacent to the K-2 HPP site (0.24±0.06 mg/m³). These
low concentrations of suspended particulates are reflective of the absence of major industrial activities in the
surrounding environment of the project site. Concentrations of sulphur dioxide (<0.2 mg/m³) and carbon monoxide
(<1.0 mg/m³) were reported to be below the laboratory detection limits and the criteria (Please refer to Table 8-3).

10.4.1.1               Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase
The Pre-construction phase involves the following major activities that have the potential to impact air quality:


      •    Access Road / Bridge Construction
      •    Install Power Supply System
      •    Install Water Supply and Sewerage System
      •    Install Heating and Air Supply System
      •    Establish Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing System

The assessment of air quality impacts during the pre-construction phase is outlined and detailed in the following
sections:

10.4.1.1.1 Local Air Quality
The major activities planned for the pre-construction phase are expected to generate air pollutants, resulting in higher
levels of dust and gaseous pollutants in the surrounding atmosphere. The impact of each specific activity mentioned
above is outlined below:

Access Road / Bridge Construction- Construction of access road and bridge can significantly contribute to air pollution
from dust generation due to construction activities like site clearance, earthmoving, material handling etc, gaseous
emissions from machinery, and increased traffic carrying material, equipment or manpower. There would be generation
of windborne particulate matter as well as gaseous pollutants like NOx, SOx and CO from these activities, which would
be eventually dispersed and would impact the nearby receptors as identified below:
     • Working personnel (Access Road),
     • Working Personnel (The Kambarata-2 HPP),
     • Commuters (Vehicle drivers, co-passengers) using approach road to the Kambarata-2 HPP
     • Small scale industrial establishments near the Highway and the HPP Access Road

The dispersion of air pollutants from the source would depend on local meteorological conditions, local characteristics
and flue gas flow rate; and the dispersion pattern would be the key factor impacting the nearby receptors.


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                   Page 8
Description of Impacts



Installation of Power Supply System, Water Supply and Sewerage System, Heating and Air Supply System: Installation
of these facilities includes site/area clearance, grading, excavation, laying of lines, etc, which would lead to the
generation of particulates as well as gaseous pollutants to some extent. This, in turn, may impact the health of working
personnel and lead to a rise in the concentration of suspended matter in river water. However, these are short term
activities and would be largely localised.
Establish Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing System: Establishing aggregate production and concrete mixing
system involves site clearance, procuring raw materials, setting up processing equipment (crushers, screens, mixers),
implementing efficient storage and transportation methods, which in turn has the potential to cause emission of
particulates and gaseous emission. The generation of air pollutants would be along the access road and at the
Construction site ; however, these are limited to a short duration only.

10.4.1.1.2 Regional Air Quality:
Regional air quality typically refers to the overall state of air quality within a larger geographical area, beyond the project
site and its direct impact area. For the proposed project, regional air quality has been considered 2 km outside the
boundary of the K-1 HPP (including the Dam) and beyond 500 m of both sides of the access road.

During the major activities to be undertaken at the pre-construction phase, the following sub-activities have the
potential to impact regional air quality:
     • Transport of raw materials and equipment from various sources to the site
     • Commutation of working personnel to and from Workers’ camp (located outside the site)

All these would generate air pollutants like fugitive dust, NOx, SOx, CO etc, which may have adverse impact on local
commuters, adjacent local community, small scale industrial establishments as well as flora and fauna of the region.
However, this phase is of short duration and the impacts on regional air quality would be largely guided by the pattern
of dispersion of air pollutants from the source, based on local meteorology and emission rate.

10.4.1.2               Phase 2 – Construction Phase
The construction phase involves the following major activities that have the potential to impact air quality:


      •    Secondary Access Roads/site roads construction
      •    Construction Site Operation (Crushing plant, workshops, quarries and batching plant)
      •    Main RCC Dam (including Spillway and Outlets) construction
      •    River Diversion Tunnel (Excavation and Installation)
      •    Coffer Dam Construction (Upstream and Downstream)
      •    HPP Water Intake (Excavation and Installation)
      •    Headrace Tunnels and Shafts (Excavation and Installation)
      •    Powerhouse Building (Excavation and Installation)
      •    Tailrace Channel (Excavation and Installation)
      •    Substation Construction
      •    Reservoir Filling (“First Fill”)

The assessment of air quality impacts during the construction phase is outlined and detailed in the following sections.


10.4.1.2.1             Local Air Quality
The major activities indicated during construction phase will lead to generation of particulate matter and gaseous
pollutants in ambient air. Impact due to individual activities presented above is described below:

Secondary Access Roads/site roads: Construction of secondary access roads/site roads can lead to air pollution from
dust generation due to construction activities like site clearance, earthmoving, etc, gaseous emissions from machinery.
There will be generation of windborne particulate matter, NOx, SOx and CO from these activities, which will be dispersed
and may cause occupational health hazards for working personnel. However, considering that the working personnel
will be using Personal Protective Equipment (PPE), and this is a short-term activity prior to the commencement of full-
fledged construction activities, the impact is envisaged to be of low significance.

 Release of air pollutants from the above operations has the potential to cause health effects on human receptors,

impact on aquatic flora and fauna.

Construction of River Diversion Tunnel and Coffer Dam, Installation of HPP Water Intake, Headrace Tunnels and Shafts,

activities like excavation of rock and earth, tunnel boring, drilling and blasting, material handling, including loading,

construction for erection of powerhouse and substation buildings, placing turbines, generators, transformers, and other
equipment and setting up electrical systems and connections. All these activities can generate both coarse & fine
particulates and gaseous pollutants like NOx, SOx and CO, causing health issues in nearby human receptors (working
personnel and workers’ camp). The rise in concentration of particulates in ambient air may impact surrounding
terrestrial and riparian flora and fauna and may deposit on river water, impacting river and sediment water quality and
aquatic ecosystem.

Main RCC Dam (including Spillway and Outlets): Construction of dam, including spillways and outlets, may contribute to
air pollution through the generation of dust and gaseous emissions from construction activities and equipment. These
would impact the health of nearby human receptors (mainly working personnel and Workers’ camp), both terrestrial

Reservoir filling (first filling) is not envisaged to impact local or regional ambient air quality in terms of air pollutants.

10.4.1.2.2 Regional Air Quality
During the construction phase, the following sub-activities3 has the potential to impact regional air quality:
     • Transport of raw materials and equipment from various sources to the site

Major raw materials which are likely to be transported 4 over a long distance are as follows:

         •    Fly Ash – Preferably from Bishkek (about 330 km from site)
         •    Cement – Preferably from Osh (about 300 km from site)
Movement of the heavy vehicles carrying raw materials would generate air pollutants like particulates, NOx, SOx, CO
etc, from tailpipe emission, dust emission from combustion of diesel(includes soot ), wear and tear of brake pad,
abrasion of tyres on road surfaces, dust lifted by tyres from roads and fugitive emission from materials conveyed. These
air pollutants may cause adverse health impacts on commuters, adjacent local community, receptors within the local
construction or industrial units as well as flora and fauna of the region.
From the construction schedule presented in Dam Options Report5, critical construction path includes site installations,
construction of river diversion, RCC dam construction and the impounding time, considering which the peak
construction period will span over a period of almost 8-8.5 months every year for about 8.5 years. Hence the number
of additional vehicles would also vary, with an average of 74 trucks/day and about 256 trucks/day during the peak
period, carrying cement and fly ash6 for construction of Dam and HPP. The route of transportation would be along Osh-
Bishkek Highway (Noth-South highway), which is presently conveying a total load of about 5000 vehicles (Please refer
to Section 10.4.13) and passes along settlements like Toktogul, Karakul, Uch Terek, KaraJygach, Uchkurgon, Torkent and
Sosnovka. There are no densely placed settlements along this ~600 km highway, which is already used by heavy vehicles,
including trucks & dumpers. Emissions from tailpipe of vehicles on roads, however dilute and disperse rapidly, with
pollutant concentrations gradually decrease to background concentration within a few meters span (Deng et al., 2020)
from the source of emission. Fugitive emission from resuspension of dust accumulated on the road surface is not
expected, as the highway is paved. Also, considering that the baseline vehicle count is quite significant, additional load
of 256 trucks is not expected to cause substantial alteration to the regional air quality with respect to the Project. Hence,
highly significant impact on regional ambient air quality is not envisaged even though the extent of impact would be
along the entire route of material & equipment transport .
Also, it should be noted that, if natural pozzolans are used instead of fly ash, the number of additional vehicles on the
highway estimated for fly ash procurement would not be required and hence the additional truck load would be for
carrying cement, other raw materials and waste only, which would eventually reduce the impact on regional air quality
to be generated from the Project.

10.4.1.3                  Phase 3 – Operation Phase

10.4.1.3.1 Local Air Quality
Operation phase involves activities like Flow Regulation (for Dam),     Infrastructure Maintenance and Power
Production. These activities are not envisaged to cause air pollution, except the maintenance of infrastructure which
involve sub-activities like regular inspections (structural, mechanical and electrical inspections), cleaning and debris
removal from water intake and outlet structures, repairs and replacements including road repairs. These sub-activities
may entail use of equipment operated by hydrocarbons, thereby causing emission of gaseous pollutants as well as

10.4.1.3.2 Regional Air Quality
Operation of the K-1 HPP is anticipated to have a beneficial impact on regional air quality as there would no significant
emission of air pollutants such as particulate matter, oxides of Nitrogen, Sulphur and Carbon.

Noise and Vibration
Hydropower projects can produce considerable noise and vibration throughout both the pre-construction, construction
and operational phases. The entire project activities of three phases would have an impact on the receptors of following
locations:

                asphalt mixing plants would contribute continuous noise and vibrations.
         •      Construction of Access Road: May require for site clearance to prepare access road and bridges, which
                would generate high levels of noise and ground vibrations.
         •      Traffic Noise: Transportation of equipment to the site
         •      Site surveys, preparation, environmental assessments

     •       Specific Activities: Site Clearing, Site surveys, preparation, environmental assessments

             Jygach; The Kambarata-2 HPP).
Sub-VECs 2: Aquatic Ecosystems
     •       Specific Activities: Site surveys (e.g., water quality, fish habitat assessments on the Naryn River)
     •       Impact: Minimal noise and vibration from surveying activities in the Naryn River; could disturb aquatic species,
             especially fish.
Sub-VECs 3: Terrestrial Wildlife
     •       Specific Activities: Site Clearing, Preliminary site assessments, environmental studies in forested and riverbank
             areas)
     •       Impact: Minor noise and vibration from site inspections; limited impact but may temporarily disturb wildlife
             of the project area.
Sub-VECs 4: Cultural and Heritage Sites
     •       Specific Activities: None, as there is no known Cultural and Heritage Sites in and around the project site
     •       Impact: None.

10.4.1.6               Phase 2 – Construction Phase

         •      Blasting and Drilling: Would be used for creating tunnels and foundations, these activities generate high

         •      Heavy Machinery Operation: Excavators, bulldozers, and cranes contribute to continuous noise and

         •      Concrete Pouring and Piling: These activities can produce intermittent but intense noise and vibrations.

Sub-VECs 1: Human Health and Well-being
    •      Specific Activities: Heavy machinery operation, movement, excavation, blasting, construction of dam, traffic

    •      Impact: High noise and vibration from construction activities (e.g., blasting, excavation, dam construction) lead
           to hearing loss, stress, sleep disturbances, and fatigue for local workers.
Sub-VECs 2: Aquatic Habitats
    •      Specific Activities: Removal of vegetation/ boulders, levelling of land, construction activities (including
           excavation, tunnelling and drilling) in the river channel or on the riverbanks
    •      Impact: Significant noise and vibration from underwater drilling, blasting, and construction activities disturb
           aquatic life, especially fish in the Naryn River, potentially affecting fish spawning.
Sub-VECs 3: Terrestrial Habitats
    •      Specific Activities: Construction equipment mobilization, land clearing, excavation, grading of the project site
    •      Impact: Noise and vibration from heavy machinery, blasting, and land clearing disturb wildlife habitats and
           cause temporary displacement of species.

    •      Impact: None.
    Sub-VECs 5: Residential and Commercial Areas
    •      Specific Activities: Blasting, heavy machinery operation, transportation of materials, vehicle movement
    •      Impact: High noise levels from blasting, machinery operation, and heavy vehicle traffic lead to hearing damage,
           stress, and sleep disturbances for nearby residents of Highways and surrounding communities, resettlement
           area and worker camps. Vibration may cause structural damage to office buildings, worker camps.
    Sub-VECs 6: Occupational Health
    –      Specific Activities: Blasting, excavation, heavy machinery operation, concrete pouring and piling

10.4.1.7               Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance
In this phase there are following activities would be involved.
    •      Turbine Operation: The rotation of turbines generates continuous noise and vibrations.
    •      Water Flow: High-speed water flow through penstocks and spillways can create significant noise.
    •      Maintenance Activities: Regular maintenance involving machinery can also contribute to noise and vibrations.
There are six sub VECs have been identified and impact of above activities on all individuals sub-VECs are as follows:
Sub-VECs 1: Human Health and Well-being
    •      Specific Activities: Operation of turbines, generators, and machinery at hydropower plant
    •      Impact: Continuous noise from turbines, generators, and water flow regulation causes long-term hearing
           issues, stress, and sleep disturbances for both workers and resident of worker camp. Vibration from equipment
           may lead to discomfort and health issues.
Sub-VECs 2: Aquatic Ecosystems
    •      Specific Activities: Operation of hydropower plant, including water flow regulation
    •      Impact: Operational noise from turbines, water flow, and mechanical activities may disturb aquatic life in the
           Naryn River, affecting behaviours such as feeding, and breeding.
Sub-VECs 3: Terrestrial Wildlife
    •      Specific Activities: Operation of turbines, machinery, and traffic for transport of materials
    •      Impact: Continuous noise from turbines and machinery disturbs wildlife in nearby habitats, affecting
           communication, mating, and migration. Vibration may disrupt sensitive species in the area.
Sub-VECs 4: Cultural and Heritage Sites
    •      Specific Activities: None, as there is no known Cultural and Heritage Sites in and around the project site
    •      Impact: None.
Sub-VECs 5: Residential and Commercial Areas
    •      Specific Activities: Noise from turbines, generators, dam operations, and transport activities
    •      Impact: Ongoing noise from turbines and generators may cause chronic stress, sleep disturbances, and
           hearing loss for residents in nearby communities (e.g., Staff Quarters).
Sub-VECs 6: Occupational Health
    •      Specific Activities: Continuous operation of turbines, machinery, and maintenance activities
    •      Impact: Workers exposed to high noise levels may suffer from hearing loss, stress, and musculoskeletal
           disorders due to vibration.

Terrestrial Biodiversity
This section presents a qualitative assessment of potential impacts of the project on terrestrial biodiversity during the
following phases of the Project:

                 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase, including site clearance
                 Phase 2 – Construction Phase, including initial reservoir filling
                 Phase 3 – Operation Phase, including maintenance




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3            Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                              SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                           11 August 2025                 Page 16
Description of Impacts



The Project-related activities during the said phases are likely to lead to the following general impacts on terrestrial
species, habitats and ecosystem services:

Loss of terrestrial habitats, including natural habitats and potential critical habitats, owing to removal or
submergence/inundation of vegetation.
Degradation of terrestrial habitats, including natural habitats and potential critical habitats, owing to excavation,
dumping, erosion, deposition or compaction of soil.
Fragmentation of terrestrial habitats owing to construction of Project-related linear structures.
Unintentional introduction or spread of invasive alien species owing to movement of project-related personnel,
vehicles and machinery.
Disturbance, injury or mortality of flora and fauna owing to Project-related structures and activities or noise, vibration,
artificial illumination, emissions, effluents, dust or muck generated therefrom.
Reduction in local populations of flora and fauna species owing to incidental utilization by Project personnel for
personal use, such as food or fuel.
Loss or loss of access to terrestrial ecosystem services, including highly valued provisioning services, owing to removal,
submergence or degradation of terrestrial vegetation.


Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
Activities expected to occur during Phase 1 – Preconstruction include:
        Preparatory Engineering and Geological Surveys
        Laying of Access Roads
        Preparation of Construction Sites
        Installation of Power, Water Supply and Sewerage Systems
        Establishment of Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing Systems

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on terrestrial biodiversity during the pre-construction
phase.

10.4.1.8.1 Loss or Degradation of Habitat
Clearing, levelling and grading of land towards the various pre-construction activities is expected to result in removal
of the largely natural vegetation and alteration of the existing largely natural topography and drainage in and around
the footprint areas of the concerned structures and features. This is expected to lead to loss or degradation of
terrestrial habitat - predominantly grassland, cold desert and rocky areas, interspersed with some shrubland and
riparian forest patches, including potential habitat of IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as CR
Ammopiptanthus nanus (Dwarf Sandlip), CR Crataegus knorringiana (Knorring’s Hawthorn) and EN Tulipa toktogulica
(Toktogul Tulip) - thereby reducing availability of potential feeding, roosting and/or breeding habitats for the
associated terrestrial fauna, including IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as EN Otis tarda (Great
Bustard), VU Columba eversmanni (Yellow-eyed Pigeon) and VU Streptopelia turtur (European Turtle-dove).

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat loss or degradation is classified as local in extent, high in
magnitude and long-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is of moderate
significance.

10.4.1.8.2 Fragmentation of Terrestrial and Aerial Habitat
Construction or installation of linear structures, such as access roads, pipelines and power transmission lines, as part
of the pre-construction phase is expected to result in disruption of terrestrial habitats, including aerial habitat
traversed by aerially moving terrestrial fauna. This is expected to result in reducing availability of, or impeding access
to, feeding, roosting or breeding habitats for the terrestrial fauna of the impacted areas. Overhead power
transmission lines, besides fragmenting aerial habitats, would also pose risk of collision and/or electrocution mortality
of avian species, particularly IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened soaring birds, such as EN Aquila nipalensis
(Steppe Eagle), EN Falco cherrug (Saker Falcon) and EN Neophron percnopterus (Egyptian Vulture) and migratory
waterbirds, such as CR Vanellus gregarius (Sociable Lapwing), EN Oxyura leucocephala (White-headed Duck) and VU
Aythya ferina (Common Pochard).




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                    Page 17
Description of Impacts



Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat fragmentation is expected to be relatively local in extent,
high in magnitude and largely short-term in duration, except for roads required beyond the construction phase. As
per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is of moderate significance.


10.4.1.8.3 Disturbance to Terrestrial Fauna
Blasting, drilling and deployment of heavy machinery towards the various pre-construction activities is expected to
generate high levels of noise, vibration, artificial illumination, dust and emissions, as also, involve movement of
Project-related personnel in the Study Area. This is expected to result in disturbance to terrestrial flora and fauna
species therein, including disruption of biological rhythms and cycles. This may also lead to displacement of terrestrial
fauna, thereby increasing pressure on feeding, roosting or breeding habitats beyond the directly impacted area. Flora
and fauna species likely to be impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as CR
Crataegus knorringiana (Knorring’s Hawthorn) and CR Vanellus gregarius (Sociable Lapwing).
Based on the impact significance matrix, the said disturbance or displacement of terrestrial flora and fauna is expected
to be relatively local in extent, high in magnitude and short-term in duration. As per the impact significance
evaluation scale, the said impact is of moderate significance.


Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
Activities expected to occur during Phase 2 – Construction include:
              Establishment and operation of Labour Camps
              Establishment and operation of Construction Sites
              Tunnelling for the HRT and TRT
              Diversion of the River
              Construction of the Dam
              Installation of the Powerhouse and Substation
              Initial Filling of the Reservoir

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on terrestrial biodiversity during the construction
phase.

10.4.1.8.4 Incidental Mortality of Terrestrial Species
The prolonged presence of Project-related personnel in and around the labour camps and construction sites is likely to
result in unplanned harvest of wild species for food or fuel purposes. Similarly, the frequent movement of Project-
related vehicles between the various Project-related sites is likely to result in Animal Vehicle Collision (AVC)-related
injury or mortality of terrestrial fauna. Terrestrial fauna may also be susceptible to injury or mortality from accidental
falls into Project-related excavations or collision/electrocution from Project-related infrastructure. This is expected to
lead to reduction in the local populations of the concerned species, potentially impeding their health and survival.
Flora and fauna species likely to be impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as CR
Ammopiptanthus nanus (Dwarf Sandlip), CR Crataegus knorringiana (Knorring’s Hawthorn), VU Panthera uncia (Snow
Leopard) and VU Vormela peregusna (Marbled Polecat).

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said species population reduction is expected to be local in extent, high in
magnitude and short to medium-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is
deemed of moderate significance.

10.4.1.8.5 Disturbance and/or Displacement of Terrestrial Fauna
Blasting, drilling and deployment of heavy machinery towards the various construction activities is expected to
generate high levels of noise, vibration, artificial illumination, dust and emissions, as also, involve movement of
Project-related personnel in the Study Area. This is expected to result in disturbance to terrestrial flora and fauna
species therein, including disruption of biological rhythms and cycles. This may also lead to displacement of terrestrial
fauna, thereby increasing pressure on feeding, roosting or breeding habitats beyond the directly impacted area. Flora
and fauna species likely to be impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as CR
Crataegus knorringiana (Knorring’s Hawthorn) and CR Vanellus gregarius (Sociable Lapwing).



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                       Page 18
Description of Impacts



Based on the impact significance matrix, the said disturbance or displacement of terrestrial flora and fauna is expected
to be relatively local in extent, moderate in magnitude and short-term in duration. As per the impact significance
evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of moderate significance.

10.4.1.8.6 Submergence or Inundation of Terrestrial Habitats
The initial reservoir filling is expected to result in submergence or inundation of terrestrial habitats, comprising
approximately 0.06 sq km of riparian forest, 0.07 sq km of shrubland, 0.5 sq km of cold desert, 2.5 sq km of rocky area
habitat types, all of which constitute largely natural habitats, as also, 49 sq km of grassland habitat type, which is
partially utilized as pasture by the local community. This is expected to lead to loss of the concerned natural terrestrial
habitats, thereby reducing availability of feeding, roosting or breeding habitats for the associated terrestrial fauna.
This would also lead to displacement of the said fauna into adjacent comparable habitats, thereby increasing pressure
on feeding, roosting or breeding habitats beyond the directly impacted area. Flora and fauna species likely to be
impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Crataegus knorringiana (Knorring’s
Hawthorn) and CR Vanellus gregarius (Sociable Lapwing).

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat loss or degradation is expected to be regional in extent, high
in magnitude and long-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of
major significance.

10.4.1.8.7 Loss or Loss of Access to Ecosystem Services

The initial reservoir filling is expected to result in submergence or inundation of approximately 49 sq km of grassland
habitat type, part of which is utilized as pasture by the local community. The resulting provisioning ecosystem service,
in terms of fodder, is reportedly highly valued by the said community. The reservoir filling is also expected to result in
submergence of traditional access routes connecting communities with the said fodder resource. This is expected to
lead to loss or loss of access to the concerned highly valued provisioning ecosystem service, which in turn, may
increase grazing pressure on grassland areas beyond the submergence zone.

The inundation is also expected to impact significant populations of 13 non-CHQ flora species that are nationally
valued by the Kyrgyz people as native plants of Kyrgyzstan, amounting to loss of a cultural ecosystem service.

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said losses are expected to be local in extent, moderate in magnitude and
long-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is of moderate significance.

Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
Activities expected to occur during Phase 3 – Operation include:
              Power Generation
              Infrastructure Maintenance
              Flow Regulation

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on terrestrial biodiversity during the operation phase.


Alteration of Terrestrial Habitat Profile
The presence of the reservoir is expected to create an alternative hyporheic zone that is likely to inundate terrestrial
habitats adjacent to the reservoir edge. This is likely to lead to alteration of the natural habitat profile of the impacted
area from dryland to marshland habitats. Flora and fauna species likely to be impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed
globally threatened species of dry grassland and pastureland habitats, such as EN Tulipa toktogulica (Toktogul Tulip),
EN Otis tarda (Great Bustard) and VU Chlamydotis macqueenii (Asian Houbara).

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat alteration is expected to be regional in extent, moderate in
magnitude and long-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of
major significance.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                     Page 19
Description of Impacts



Alteration of Terrestrial Faunal Movement
Owing to its substantial depth and flow velocity throughout the year, the river currently hinders the movement of
terrestrial fauna across the river stretch within the Study Area. As per inputs received from national terrestrial fauna
expert working on this ESIA, the creation of the reservoir may allow some faunal species to swim across the river
section upstream of the dam, while any reduced flow in the downstream reach, along with the corresponding
decrease in water depth and flow velocity, may allow some faunal species to cross the river section immediately
downstream of the dam. Considering the higher presence of human settlements on the right bank of the river, and the
potential for predatory species to get increased access to the said communities and their livestock, this could
temporarily lead to human-wildlife conflict situations. Species that may be impacted include IUCN Red List-assessed
globally threatened species, such as VU Panthera uncia (Snow Leopard) and VU Vormela peregusna (Marbled Polecat).


Based on the impact significance matrix, the said alteration in faunal movement is expected to be local in extent, high
in magnitude and short term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of
moderate significance.


Fragmentation of Aerial Habitat
The 2 options under consideration for the associated power transmission facility expected to evacuate power from the
Project comprise (a) a pre-existing 500 kV transmission line (TL) of 0.6 km length and (b) an under-construction 110 kV
TL of 23.6 km length. Either TL would pose some risk of collision and electrocution mortality to aerially moving fauna.
Given that the TL is situated within the Central Asian Flyway - an avian migratory flyway, species likely to be impacted
include globally threatened migratory waterbirds, such as CR Vanellus gregarius (Sociable Lapwing), EN Oxyura
leucocephala (White-headed Duck) and VU Aythya ferina (Common Pochard). The TLs are also likely to impact globally
threatened and/or legally protected species of soaring birds recorded within or potentially occurring in the Study Area,
such as EN Aquila nipalensis (Steppe Eagle), EN Falco cherrug (Saker Falcon) and EN Neophron percnopterus (Egyptian
Vulture)

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said impact is classified as local in extent, high in magnitude and medium-
term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of moderate significance.
In case of Option (a), considering the pre-existence and significantly short length of the TL, the risk is deemed to be
relatively lower than that in case of Option (b), which involves introduction of a new, relatively longer TL into the area.

Degradation of Provisioning Ecosystem Service
The presence of the reservoir is expected to cause continued loss of access for local communities to grasslands currently
utilized as pasture. The continuing loss of a highly valued provisioning ecosystem service is, in turn, likely to result in
increased grazing pressure on grassland areas beyond the submergence zone and therefore, degradation of the quality
of the said provisioning ecosystem service.


Based on the impact significance matrix, he said impact is classified as local in extent, moderate in magnitude and
medium-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of moderate
significance.

Aquatic Biodiversity
This section presents a qualitative assessment of potential impacts of the project on aquatic biodiversity during the
following phases of the project:

                 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase, including site clearance
                 Phase 2 – Construction Phase, including initial reservoir filling
                 Phase 3 – Operation Phase, including maintenance

The Project-related activities during the said phases are likely to lead to the following general impacts on aquatic
species, habitats and ecosystem services:

    Loss or degradation of aquatic habitats, including natural habitats and precautionarily assumed critical habitat,
        owing to conversion of lotic habitat to lentic habitat.
    Degradation of aquatic habitats, including natural habitats and precautionarily assumed critical habitat, owing to
        Project-related pollution or contamination.
    Fragmentation of aquatic habitat owing to construction of the dam.
    Reduction in local populations of fauna species owing to incidental utilization by project personnel for food or sport.


Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance


Activities expected to occur during Phase 1 – Preconstruction include:
        Preparatory Engineering and Geological Surveys
        Laying of Access Roads
        Preparation of Construction Sites
        Installation of Power, Water Supply and Sewerage Systems
        Establishment of Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing Systems

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on aquatic biodiversity during the pre-construction
phase.


10.4.1.8.8 Degradation of Aquatic Habitat

Introduction of sediment, generated by the pre-construction activities, into the river through would result in increased
turbidity and reduced light penetration. This may lead to degradation of the concerned natural aquatic habitats,
thereby reducing availability of food or impeding predation success for the associated aquatic fauna. Species that can
potentially be impacted include IUCN Red List assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Pseudoscaphirhynchus
fedtschenkoi (Syr-Darya Shovelnose Sturgeon) and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp), of which the latter also qualifies
as a restricted range species with respect to the Study Area.

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat degradation is expected to be relatively local in extent, high
in magnitude and short-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed
of moderate significance.



Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
Activities expected to occur during Phase 2 – Construction include:
              Establishment and operation of Labour Camps
              Establishment and operation of Construction Sites
              Tunnelling for the HRT and TRT
              Diversion of the River
              Construction of the Dam
              Installation of the Powerhouse and Substation
              Initial Filling of the Reservoir

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on aquatic biodiversity during the construction phase.

10.4.1.8.9 Incidental Mortality of Aquatic Fauna
The prolonged presence of Project-related personnel in and around the labour camps and construction sites may
result in unplanned capture of aquatic fauna, mainly fishes, for food purposes. This is expected to lead to reduction in
the local populations of the concerned species, potentially impeding their viability. Species that can potentially be
impacted include IUCN Red List assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Pseudoscaphirhynchus fedtschenkoi
(Syr-Darya Shovelnose Sturgeon) and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp), of which the latter also qualifies as a
restricted range species with respect to the Study Area.

Based on the impact significance matrix, the impact is classified as local in extent, high in magnitude and short-term in
duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of moderate significance.

10.4.1.8.10 Degradation of Aquatic Habitat
The initial reservoir filling is expected to result in conversion of aquatic habitat, comprising approximately 9 sq km of
permanent river type of inland wetland habitat, which constitutes a near-natural habitat. This is expected to lead to
loss or degradation of the concerned natural aquatic habitat, thereby reducing availability of feeding or spawning
habitats for the associated aquatic fauna. This would also lead to displacement of the said fauna into adjacent
comparable habitats, thereby increasing pressure on feeding or spawning habitats beyond the directly impacted area.
Species that can potentially be impacted include IUCN Red List assessed globally threatened species, such as CR
Pseudoscaphirhynchus fedtschenkoi (Syr-Darya Shovelnose Sturgeon) and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp), the latter
having a documented preference for fast-flowing rivers.

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said habitat loss or degradation is classified as regional in extent, high in
magnitude and long-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of
major significance.

10.4.1.8.11 Fragmentation of Aquatic Habitat
The initial reservoir filling is expected to result in conversion of aquatic habitat from lotic to lentic. Species that can
potentially be impacted include IUCN Red List assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Pseudoscaphirhynchus
fedtschenkoi (Syr-Darya Shovelnose Sturgeon) and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp), the latter having a documented
preference for fast-flowing rivers.
Based on the impact significance matrix, the said impact is classified as local in extent, high in magnitude and long-
term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale the impact is deemed of major significance.

10.4.1.8.12 Loss of Cultural Ecosystem Service
The construction of the Project dam, followed by the initial reservoir filling, are expected to result in degradation and
fragmentation of aquatic habitat. This wouldresult in disruption of movement of fishes between the river sections
downstream and upstream of the dam, thereby reducing availability of feeding or spawning habitats for the
associated aquatic fauna. Species that would be impacted include LC Glyptosternon oschanini (Turkestan Catfish), a
species nationally valued and legally protected as native to Kyrgyzstan, thus amounting to loss of a cultural ecosystem
service.
Based on the impact significance matrix, the said impact is classified as regional in extent, moderate in magnitude and
short-term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the impact is deemed of moderate significance




Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance
Activities expected to occur during Phase 3 – Operation include:
              Power Generation
              Infrastructure Maintenance
              Flow Regulation

The following sections outline the impacts of the said activities on aquatic biodiversity during the operation phase.

10.4.1.8.13 Trapping of Aquatic Fauna
Owing to the presence of the dam, any fishes pre-existing in the downstream reach or introduced therein through the
dam spillway, would be trapped in the downstream reach. This would lead to small, isolated, inbreeding fish populations
lacking the genetic diversity necessary for their viability. Species that can potentially be impacted include IUCN Red List
assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Pseudoscaphirhynchus fedtschenkoi (Syr-Darya Shovelnose Sturgeon)
and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp).

Based on the impact significance matrix, the said impact is classified as local in extent, high in magnitude and medium
term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of moderate significance.

10.4.1.8.14 Introduction of Alien Species
The likely use of the reservoir for culture fisheries, a long-standing practice in comparable reservoirs of the region, could
result in introduction of alien and potentially invasive fish species into the river. This could create unnatural competition
for resources, leading to reduction in populations of native fish populations. Species that can potentially be impacted
include IUCN Red List assessed globally threatened species, such as CR Pseudoscaphirhynchus fedtschenkoi (Syr-Darya
Shovelnose Sturgeon) and EN Aspiolicius esocinus (Pike Asp).

Based on the impact significance matrix , the said impact is classified as regional in extent, high in magnitude and long-
term in duration. As per the impact significance evaluation scale, the said impact is deemed of major significance.
