Surface Water Resources and Quality
Water Resources, being one of the primary components of the Kambarata-1 HPP, a qualitative assessment was
undertaken to envisage the potential impacts on water resources and quality which may arise during the following
phases of the project:

    •      Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance

    •      Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling

    •      Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.

During the screening and scoping study for the project, the Valued Ecosystem Components (VECs) were identified
relevant to the Water Resources and Quality for the Kambarata-1 HPP which have been discussed below:
    •      Surface Water Quality: The Naryn River being the primary surface water resource may undergo changes in its
           water quality parameters such as TSS, DO, BOD due to activities such as excavations and material handling and
           disposal of waste and wastewater during the Phase-1 and Phase-2 of the project. Qualities such as water
           temperature may also be significantly impacted during the Phase-3 of the project due to the upcoming
           reservoir. Also, during the FS 2014, radioactivity was found in the form of alpha activity in multiple sampling
           locations (11 locations) which exceeded the MPC of 0.1 Bq/L. However, during the Detailed Engineering if it is
           found that radioactivity persists consistently in the Naryn river water near dam site, then water from the Naryn
           River may not be possible to be used as a construction water and potable water for the construction workforce.
           At the same time, Zhorobekova (2024) suggests that the process of clearing of dumped radioactive wastes have
           already been initiated by the Kyrgyz Authority. Minor karst-like features identified in the Kambarata-1 Project
           area may locally affect groundwater and surface water flow and slope permeability, leading to increased
           turbidity, alkalinity and hardness in water sources, though no major karst systems are present from initial
           screening. In case, major proportions of Karst materials are found in water sources during water quality testing
           before the construction work begins, then EPC Contractor would ensure and finalize the water treatment
           requirements before use for drinking and other domestic purposes.
    •      Surface Water Hydrology: Impacts on the present hydrological regime is envisaged during the Phase- 1 and
           Phase-2 of the project on account of land clearance for setting up of construction zones, although these
           changes in the runoff and infiltration pattern due to changes in landcover is expected to be localized and short
           termed. In addition, changes in the flow regime of the Naryn River during river diversion and during operation
           of the dam is anticipated to alter the hydraulic regime of the river.
    •      Thermal Regime and Reservoir Water Quality: Stratification and associated thermal effects are expected
           during impoundment of the Kambarata-1 HPP reservoir, with potential implications for both temperature and
           oxygen distribution. The intake sill is located at EL 1090.6 masl—well below the thermocline and the extended
           Minimum Operating Level (EL 1116 masl)—which ensures consistent submergence and withdrawal from the
           hypolimnion. While this design supports operational reliability, it may also result in the release of cold, low-
           oxygen water downstream, particularly during periods of strong stratification. These effects are anticipated to
           be confined to the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir, where no sensitive aquatic habitats exist. However, further
           hydrodynamic and water quality modelling is recommended to assess the onset, extent, and persistence of
           stratification and its downstream implications. Related VECs such as sediment transport, flood risk,
           hydrogeology, and groundwater hydrology are addressed in their respective sections—Sediment Transport,
           Groundwater, and Natural Hazards (Floods). Note that the Impact Assessment for Environmental Flows is
           provided in a separate appendix.
The baseline monitoring of drinking water and surface water quality during October 2024 (Autumn) was used to assess
the impact of the project activities during the construction and operational phases of the project on the surface water



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                            Page 24
Description of Impacts



resources and quality. During the monitoring, it was found that the drinking water samples had pH in the range 7.03-
8.03 which although a bit alkaline was suitable for drinking. The total hardness (2.45 mol/m 3 - 9.2 mol/m3) and the water
mineralization was also found to be within acceptable standards. However, there were detections of excess mercury,
lead and arsenic in multiple drinking water samples. In the surface water quality samples, although most parameters
were found to be within permissible limits, the values of Mercury (highest: 0.056 mg/dm 3) and Arsenic (highest: 0.02
mg/dm3) were found to be quite higher than the permissible values.
During Spring Season baseline monitoring (April 2025), with regard to drinking water quality, most parameters such as
pH, turbidity, BOD5, Iron were within acceptable standards. In few drinking water samples, the concentration of Calcium
(highest: 78.2 mg/l) and Magnesium (highest: 68 mg/l) were relatively high. In addition, heavy metal concentrations
were below detection or significantly below KR maximum allowable concentrations. In surface water quality samples,
the turbidity (highest: 362.5 mg/dm3) was found higher in multiple samples. Heavy metal concentrations were within
permissible limits, other than Lead which was found to be above MPC in one location.
As discussed in details in Chapter 8: Biophysical Baseline Monitoring, in the Autumn 2024 monitoring it has been found
that heavy metal concentrations, especially Mercury, in surface water, groundwater and bottom sediments were
fluctuating along the river length from upstream of the proposed K-1 HPP reservoir to downstream of the the K-2 HPP
dam face with a few abrupt peaks which is not justifiable due to absence of anthropogenic sources of contamination in
the adjoining. However, corroborating these results with the Spring 2025 monitoring, the concentration of heavy metals
were well within permissible limits in surface water, ground water and bottom sediments, which appears to be more
plausible.
The following sections discusses the impacts on surface water resources and quality that may arise in each phase of the
Kambarata-1 HPP and their sources with relation to the VECs identified above.

10.4.1.9 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
The major activities during the pre-construction phase that may have impacts on the surface water resources and
quality are as follows:
•        Access Road / Bridge Construction
•        Install Power Supply System
•        Install Water Supply and Sewerage System
•        Install Heating and Air Supply System
•        Land Clearance and Preparation
•        Establish Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing System
The activities listed above during Phase-1 of the project are expected to generate suspended matter and sediments
which may result in higher levels of total suspended solids (TSS) and turbidity in the nearby surface water bodies
which includes the Naryn River. Impacts which may arise from each activity listed above and their sources are outlined
below:


10.4.1.9.1 Surface Water Quality
       •      Access Road/Bridge Construction: The construction of access road and bridges will involve activities such as
              site clearance, earth moving and excavation, handling of fine aggregates and subgrade materials which will
              cause localized sediment mobilization. These mobilized sediments and construction materials may be carried
              by run-off streams into the Naryn River and has the potential to increase the turbidity and TSS of the main
              channel of the Naryn River. This may impact potential water resources users of the Naryn River including
              aquatic species. However, these impacts are localized and is of low significance.
       •      Installation of Power Supply System, Water Supply and Sewerage System, Heating and Air Supply System,
              Land Clearance and Preparation, Establishment of Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing System:
              Establishment of these facilities as well as land clearance involves grading, excavation and earth moving,
              generating mobilized sediments which may have the potential to be carried by surface water runoff into the
              main Naryn River increasing its turbidity and TSS levels which may consequently impact the health of
              dependant surface water users of the Naryn River and associated aquatic life. The primary risk of sediment



    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                 Page 25
Description of Impacts



              laden runoff into the Naryn River will be where vegetation clearance will expose erodible soils on steep terrain
              near the river. Additionally, fuel, oil and grease spills from machinery and equipment may also contaminate
              the surface water bodies. However, considering the short duration of the Phase-1 activities and their localized
              nature, these impacts may be considered to be of low significance and reversible by implementing proper
              erosion and pollution control measures.

10.4.1.9.2 Hydrology
       •      Access Road/Bridge Construction: In absence of information on horizontal and vertical alignment of access
              roads and assuming that the access roads will have culverts and embankments, minor localised changes to
              surface drainage patterns may occur where culverts or embankments alter runoff paths, although these effects
              will be spatially limited and are reversible through mitigation measures.
       •      Install Water Supply and Sewerage System, Land Clearance and Preparation, Establishment of Aggregate
              Production and Concrete Mixing System: Land clearance and preparation will involve activities such as removal
              of vegetation which may induce increased surface runoff and minor alteration of drainage pattern at site scale.
              Also, site grading, surface sealing and localized groundworks during installation of water supply and sewerage
              system, aggregate production and concrete mixing system may change the overland flow which however will
              be localized having low significance. These impacts can be readily mitigated through artificial drainage
              arrangement for diverting water from the construction area.

10.4.1.10 Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
The major activities during the construction phase that may have impacts on the surface water resources and quality
are as follows:

       •      Secondary Access Roads/site roads construction
       •      Coffer Dam Construction (Upstream and Downstream)
       •      Construction Site Operation (Crushing plant, workshops, quarries and batching plant)
       •      Main RCC Dam (including Spillway and Outlets) construction
       •      River Diversion Tunnel (Excavation and Installation)
       •      HPP Water Intake (Excavation and Installation)
       •      Headrace Tunnels and Shafts (Excavation and Installation)
       •      Powerhouse Building (Excavation and Installation)
       •      Tailrace Channel (Excavation and Installation)
       •      Substation Construction
       •      Reservoir Filling (“First Fill”)
The Phase-2 of the project, which includes the above activities will involve large quantities of excavation and material
handling. It also includes generation of muck and large quantities of wastewater generated from construction
activities and preparation and placing of concrete. These activities may increase surface water quality parameters
such as TSS and turbidity. Impacts envisaged on the hydrological regime as a result of diversion of river and first filling
of the reservoir have also been discussed in detail in the subsequent sections.

Impacts which may arise from each activity listed above and their sources are outlined below:

10.4.1.10.1 Surface Water Quality
•      Secondary Access Roads/site roads construction:

Large-scale earthworks, excavation, and stockpile placement on steep slopes can heighten erosion risk and may lead
to the addition of fine sediments to surface runoff, especially during periods of heavy rainfall or snowmelt which may
lead to increased levels of turbidity of the river water. Consequently, this may impact the users dependant on the
Naryn River and the aquatic life present there.


    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                    Page 26
Description of Impacts



•      Construction Site Operation

       -      Crushing Plant and Batching Plant: As per the potential site installation area presented by AFRY in their Draft
              Interim Report, two batching plants and two crushing plants will be set up, one of each unit on either side of
              the river. Aggregate production at the crushing plants and concrete mixing at the batching plants will involve
              handling of large quantities of cement, fly ash and fine aggregates which if not managed properly may enter
              into runoff streams and increase the turbidity and TSS of the Naryn River which may impact the health of users
              dependant on Naryn River’s water resources and aquatic life. The aggregate and concrete production will take
              place during a substantial portion of the construction period and hence the impacts must be mitigated
              properly. In addition, fly ash to be used in the concrete mix may contain heavy metals which has the potential
              to contaminate the surface water bodies.

              AFRY’s Kambarata-1 FS June 2025 Update recommends using natural pozzolans as an alternative to fly ash. This
              would involve quarrying, crushing and blasting operations which may generate unacceptable levels of
              contaminant materials which if not properly managed may enter the streams and ground water sources
              adversely altering the baseline water quality and impacting the health of all consumers dependant on these
              water resources.

       -      Construction Wastewater: As per the Interim Technical Report presented by AFRY, the main dam itself will be
              requiring 10 million m3 of concrete. In addition, concrete will also be required for powerhouse construction
              and consolidation grouting of the dam foundation. One of the key materials of concrete mix is water, and also
              water is required for curing of concrete. Other than concrete mixing and placing, construction water will also
              be required for other activities related to the dam construction such as sprinkling on stockpiles, cooling of
              boring and drilling equipment used for tunnelling, washing of equipment and vehicles. It should be noted that
              the FS 2014 reports evidence of radioactivity, especially Alpha emissions in a significant number of surface
              water samples which has also been referenced in several literature as discussed in Chapter 8: Biophysical
              Baseline Condition. Therefore prior to using of the Naryn River water for construction purposes, it is
              recommended that the EPC contractor carry out water quality tests by engaging accredited Laboratories to
              detect the presence of alpha and beta radioactive emissions and corroborate the results with the
              corresponding permissible standards for use as construction water.

              In addition, this construction water will lead to generation of construction wastewater which if untreated
              before disposal may have the potential to degrade the natural water quality of the Naryn River hampering the
              health of downstream users and aquatic species.

       -      Storage Facilities and Workshops: The interim report of AFRY mentions the requirement of storage facilities
              for aggregates and fly ash /supplementary cement materials (natural pozzolans). If no effective dust control
              measures are implemented in these storage facilities, construction materials like fine aggregates and sand from
              stockpiles may contaminate surface water streams and rivers, altering their natural characteristics.
              Additionally, chemicals, grease and oil from effluents generated in workshops, if not properly treated could
              degrade the quality of the water. These impacts will be localized and reversible through mitigations and
              management measures.
       -      Quarries: The quarrying activities for rock and aggregate production will involve using of explosives which have
              the potential to generate large quantities of hydrocarbons and nitrates which may contaminate natural runoff
              streams flowing towards the main channel of the Naryn River if not properly managed. Also, additional quarry
              sites for extraction of natural pozzolans if used as an alternative to fly ash may similarly generate large amounts
              of hydrocarbons and nitrates contaminating the surface water sources.
       •      Excavation and Installation involving construction of Main Dam, River Diversion Tunnels and Cofferdams,
              Powerhouse and Power Intake Tunnels, Substation construction:

       The construction of the aforementioned components of the dam will involve excavation and tunnelling activities
       which are associated with generation of muck and spoils. If runoff water at the construction zones is not properly
       managed through installation of storm drainage networks and catchpits for sediment trapping, it may wash away
       the spoils and muck into the Naryn River thereby increasing its turbidity and TSS. However, the impact will be




    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                       Page 27

    localized because the downstream dispersion is limited due to stilling water of the Kambarata-2 HPP and absence
    of a free-flowing channel.

    •      Construction Camps

    As per the AFRY’s Kambarata-1 FS June 2025 Update, a suggestive average 4622 labours would be assigned for
    the construction activities. Labours residing in construction camps would require water for domestic purposes.
    Water supplied to the construction camp for domestic activities if not properly treated may cause health
    complications of the users of the supplied water. As mentioned previously, that during the Autumn Season 2024
    Monitoring it was found that there were significant heavy metal concentrations in surface water and groundwater
    which could not be justified due to the absence of anthropogenic sources in the adjoining. In addition, the Spring
    Season 2025 monitoring found more plausible results in regard to surface water and groundwater quality where
    heavy metal concentrations in most samples were well within permissible limits. Hence, it is recommended that
    the EPC contractor undertake a detailed water quality analysis to ascertain that all elements of concern including
    heavy metals and radioactivity (alpha and beta emissions) are well within permissible limits. Presence of heavy
    metals and/or radioactivity in surface water and ground water sources would have severe health impacts on
    construction workers if this water is used for drinking and domestic purposes. Consequently, domestic
    wastewater will also be generated from these camps which if not treated properly before disposal may introduce
    unwanted pathogens, pollutants and nutrients into the surface water bodies and adversely alter surface water
    quality.

10.4.1.10.2 Hydrology
    •      Secondary Access Roads/site roads construction:

    Building secondary access roads and tunnel portals, especially on the steep right bank of the Naryn Valley, may
    lead to localized changes in slope drainage patterns and overland flow. The installation of culverts, road
    embankments, and cut slopes may redirect minor surface runoff. However, these effects are anticipated to be
    limited in scope and reversible with the implementation of erosion control measures.

    •      Construction Site Operation:

    Grading platforms, spoil stockpiles, and stormwater control measures during the construction site operation may
    modify natural overland flow paths, especially in the flatter construction areas near the dam site.

    •      Main RCC Dam (Including Spillways and Mid Outlets):

    The construction of an RCC dam will permanently change the river's longitudinal flow regime and connectivity.
    Once the river is diverted, the in-channel flow at the dam site will be cut off and redirected, which may lead to
    temporary change in the watercourse, limited to the period of Construction only.

    •      River Diversion Tunnel and Cofferdams:

    Installation of twin D-shaped diversion tunnels and upstream/downstream cofferdams will fully redirect river
    flows during dam construction. This may result in a major but temporary change to flow pathways through
    alteration of near-field hydrological dynamics and reduction in flow continuity in the original channel.

    •      HPP Water Intake, Headrace Tunnel and Shafts, Tailrace Tunnel

    Excavating the intake shaft on the riverbank may temporarily disrupt local flow patterns near the right bank, but
    these effects will be minor and spatially confined. The excavation of long headrace tunnels and associated shafts
    will take place outside the river corridor, potentially causing localized drainage disruption on steep slopes near
    tunnel portals, but no changes to the river flow regime are anticipated. Excavating the tailrace outlet to discharge
    into the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir may locally alter bank flow near the release point, but no broader impact on
    the flow regime is expected.

    •      Reservoir Filling (“First Fill”)

    The initial filling of the Kambarata-1 HPP reservoir may permanently change the upstream hydrological regime by
    submerging the existing river channel to the extent of the reservoir edge. During this period, the flow to the
    downstream the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir will be temporarily reduced. However, releases from the K-1 HPP can
    be managed to maintain water levels in the K-2 HPP. While the overall impact is significant, it can be controlled
    through operational management.

10.4.1.11 Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
The major activities during the operation phase including maintenance that may have impacts on the surface water
resources and quality are as follows:

    •      Flow Regulation
    •      Infrastructure Maintenance
    •      Power Generation
The Phase-3 activities will mainly involve reservoir operation and power generation along with maintenance activities.
The primary impacts that may arise during Phase-3 of the project have been discussed below:

10.4.1.11.1 Surface Water Quality, Thermal Regime and Reservoir Water Quality
Flow Regulation and Thermal Stratification

Under normal operating conditions, no environmental flow releases are required from the Kambarata-1 HPP, as the
downstream reach to Kambarata-2 is fully impounded and lacks free-flowing riverine habitat. Water will typically be
released via turbines, which are supplied by a fixed-level intake located at EL 1090.6 masl. Spillways and emergency
outlets are used only during high-flow or emergency conditions.

However, during non-routine operational scenarios—such as initial reservoir impoundment, emergency drawdowns, or
seasonal refilling—temporary flow regulation may be required to maintain water level stability in the Kambarata-2
reservoir. These measures are outlined in the Environmental Flow Management Plan (EFMP) and include staged
releases, ramping protocols, and coordinated operations between the K-1 and K-2 HPPs to prevent shoreline erosion,
slope instability, or rapid drawdown.

Seasonal thermal stratification is expected to develop in the Kambarata-1 reservoir, with the thermocline depth varying
depending on meteorological conditions, reservoir morphology, and operational levels. The headrace tunnel intake is
located at EL 1090.6 masl—below the expected thermocline and the extended Minimum Operating Level (EL 1116
masl)—and is expected to draw water from the hypolimnion. This is likely to result in the release of colder, oxygen-
depleted water.

These releases will enter the fully impounded backwater of the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir, a lentic system with limited
longitudinal gradient and no free-flowing riverine habitat. Consequently, any thermal or dissolved oxygen (DO)-related
impacts are expected to be confined to the K-2 HPP reservoir, where vertical mixing dominates and no sensitive aquatic
habitats are present.

To inform final intake elevation and operational strategy, dedicated thermal stratification and water quality modelling
should be undertaken during detailed design. This will help define realistic thermocline behavior under local conditions
and assess the potential for cold, low-oxygen water releases. However, as the current design includes a fixed-level intake
at EL 1090.6 masl and does not incorporate a multi-level or selective withdrawal system, the ability to adaptively manage
withdrawal depth is limited. These considerations are addressed in the Water Quality and Resource Management Plan
(WQRMP), which includes vertical profiling of temperature and DO during reservoir filling and early operations.
Power Generation

As the headrace tunnel inlet will generally fall below the thermocline transition boundary, water from the lower
temperature layers may flow into the HRT, penstocks and released to the downstream river via the TRT. This water of
relatively low temperature levels compared to the ambient downstream water quality may alter the surface water
quality with regard to temperature. However, as stated previously, the effect may be confined to the the K-2 HPP
reservoir as no free-flowing river or aquatic habitats exist between the K-1 HPP and the K-2 HPP.

10.4.1.11.2 Hydrology
•        Flow Regulation:

Operation of the Kambarata-1 HPP reservoir will create a long-term shift in the hydrological regime of the Naryn River
between the Kambarata-1 dam site and the Toktogul reservoir. Natural seasonal variability in this reach will be
attenuated, with flow patterns increasingly governed by reservoir storage levels, turbine operation schedules, and
electricity demand. Although the downstream Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir already impounds the river and limits the
extent of free-flowing habitat, the operational regime of Kambarata-1 will further regulate inflows to this reach,
affecting flow seasonality, thermal profiles, and sediment transport dynamics.
However, the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir has minimal active storage and limited operational flexibility, offering only
short-term (sub-daily) modulation of flow. It cannot buffer seasonal or inter-annual variations introduced by upstream
regulation. Instead, flow alterations from Kambarata-1 will propagate downstream and be largely absorbed or adjusted
at the Toktogul reservoir, which serves as the principal inter-seasonal balancing reservoir for the entire Naryn cascade.
Toktogul’s substantial active storage enables seasonal flow management to meet agricultural, environmental, and
energy demands in downstream riparian states.
This cascading regulation marks a basin-significant transformation of the natural river regime. It converts an historically
snowmelt-dominated and seasonally dynamic river system into a series of impounded reaches with managed releases.
These changes have important implications for downstream water availability, ecosystem function, and transboundary
water governance, which are addressed in the cumulative impact assessment and basin-wide management
recommendations.
 •       Infrastructure Maintenance

Routine maintenance of mid-outlets, low-level outlets, and spillway structures is generally scheduled to avoid
prolonged flow interruption. However, temporary shutdowns or flow isolation during inspection, sediment removal,
or mechanical repair may lead to short-term changes in discharge patterns. These events are managed through
advance planning and flow coordination to minimise downstream impacts. The effects are localised and reversible but
may be noticeable during dry periods or if maintenance coincides with sensitive operational windows.

    • Power Generation

Power generation at the Kambarata-1 HPP will be governed by the operational mode adopted (e.g. base load vs.
peaking). Under peaking operations, short-term fluctuations in flow releases may occur, particularly during high-
demand periods. Although these releases enter directly into the fully impounded Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir,
repeated daily or seasonal variation may lead to fluctuations in water level within the Kambarata-2 HPP reservoir.
These secondary fluctuations may affect littoral zone dynamics, reservoir bank stability, and aquatic habitat
conditions. However, due to the storage volume of the Kambarata-2 HPP and its operational flexibility, these effects
are expected to be buffered under most scenarios. Overall, the shift in flow regulation pattern from a natural regime
to a generation-driven regime is considered basin-significant, though downstream attenuation moderates local
hydrological impacts.

magnitude (M), extent (E), and duration (D), as outlined in the ESIA methodology. The results of this scoring are
detailed in Table 10-14 to Table 10-16 in Appendix 10-A. A summary of the outcomes is provided in Table below. The
impact which are highly significant would-be further studies from Likelihood and Consequences perspective and any
specific mitigation measures required to minimize the impacts would be studied.

Sediment Transport
This section assesses the potential impacts on sediment transport processes during the pre-construction, construction,
and operational phases of the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant Project (the K-1 HPP). It focuses on sediment
mobilisation, trapping, and delivery within the project footprint and immediate downstream reach, taking into account
the influence of major infrastructure including diversion works and the K-1 HPP reservoir.
The sediment transport regime in the Naryn River basin is characterised by seasonal sediment loads, with higher
sediment transport occurring during the spring and early summer due to snowmelt and rainfall runoff. The river is also
influenced by seasonal fluctuations in flow rates, with peak flows typically occurring in the spring, resulting in higher
sediment transport capacity. The existing sediment regime is influenced by both natural processes (e.g., erosion from
catchment areas, glacial melt) and human activities (e.g., upstream hydropower operations, agriculture).

10.4.1.13              Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
During the pre-construction phase, activities are limited to site mobilisation, vegetation clearance, early geotechnical
investigations, and the construction of temporary access roads and laydown areas. These activities are unlikely to cause
significant changes to the sediment transport regime, though some localised impacts may arise due to earthworks or
soil disturbances. Sediment mobilisation is expected to be minimal and localised, with risk highest where vegetation
clearance exposes erodible soils on steep terrain near the river. Erosion control measures should effectively mitigate
these short-term impacts. This phase is considered low significance for impacts on sediment transport.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3           Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                             SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                          11 August 2025                                 Page 32
Description of Impacts



10.4.1.14              Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
The construction phase will involve significant physical interventions, including tunnel excavation, river diversion, and
dam construction. Temporary works such as cofferdams, diversion tunnels, and access roads will expose large areas of
land to erosion, particularly where vegetation is cleared and soils are disturbed on steep slopes. These activities increase
the risk of local sediment mobilisation, especially during rainfall events or snowmelt. Excavation of the dam foundation,
tunnelling, and construction traffic may also contribute to sediment‑laden runoff entering nearby watercourses if
erosion controls are not in place.
The proposed diversion concept involves two D‑shaped diversion tunnels on the right bank, with flow directed through
them via upstream and downstream cofferdams. This avoids direct in‑channel works within the main riverbed, but there
remains a risk of sediment mobilisation during excavation of the intake and outlet portals, tunnel works, and temporary
flow redirection—particularly if rainfall coincides with earthworks.
Sediment trapping in the Kambarata‑1 reservoir itself will not occur until impoundment begins. However, during
construction, diversion tunnels and cofferdams may temporarily trap coarser fractions of the load, while most fine
sediment and suspended material will continue downstream. The river will remain hydraulically connected to the
Kambarata‑2 HPP reservoir, which AFRY (2025) confirms already functions as a sediment sink, with bathymetric
surveys showing average annual accumulation of 6.3 million m³/year (8.2–8.8 Mt/year). Additional suspended
sediment generated during construction is therefore expected to be rapidly deposited in the upper Kambarata‑2
reservoir, with limited passage further downstream. While such inputs may temporarily affect flushing efficiency at
Kambarata‑2, the impacts are expected to be localised and manageable if effective erosion and sediment control
measures are applied at the Kambarata‑1 site.
Once impoundment commences, sediment accumulation within the new reservoir will begin to alter downstream
sediment dynamics. However, sediment starvation effects are not anticipated in the reach immediately below the
Kambarata‑1 dam, as the existing Kambarata‑2 reservoir already fully inundates this section. Short‑term increases in
fine sediment during the construction phase remain the main risk, primarily to downstream water quality, rather than
to sediment continuity.
Given the scale of earthworks, steep terrain, and erosion potential, the risk of sediment‑related impacts during the
construction phase is assessed as moderate to high in the absence of robust erosion and sediment control measures.
With appropriate controls and monitoring, impacts can be reduced to a moderate and localised level.

10.4.1.15              Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
During the operation phase, the Kambarata‑1 HPP reservoir will act as an almost complete sediment trap. AFRY’s
updated modelling (2025) estimates mean annual inflows of ~16.5 Mt/year (15.4 Mt suspended, 1.1 Mt bedload),
equivalent to ~11.5 million m³/year of deposition. Given the absence of flushing or bypass outlets, essentially all of this
material will be retained within the reservoir. Over a 50‑year horizon, cumulative deposition could reach ~573 million
m³, representing approximately 11% of gross reservoir capacity at full supply level. Unless future sediment management
measures are implemented, this will progressively reduce live storage and energy generation potential.
This trapping has important cascade implications. Sediment delivery to the Kambarata‑2 HPP reservoir will decline
sharply, as nearly all upstream material will be intercepted by Kambarata‑1 HPP. AFRY’s bathymetric surveys (2010–
2021) indicate that Kambarata‑2 HPP has historically accumulated ~6.3 million m³/year (8.2–8.8 Mt/year). With
Kambarata‑1 HPP in operation, these inflows will largely cease, effectively relieving sedimentation pressure on
Kambarata‑2 HPP, extending its effective life, and eliminating the need for future flushing. Further downstream,
Toktogul Reservoir will only receive contributions from the ~5,000 km² intermediate catchment between Kambarata‑2
HPP and Toktogul, estimated at ~2 Mt/year. This represents a major reduction compared to naturalised inflows of 19–
20 Mt/year prior to cascade regulation.
Both Kambarata‑2 HPP and Toktogul have long acted as effective sediment sinks, with Toktogul serving as the dominant
trap in the basin. Natural sediment delivery to downstream riparian zones has therefore already been substantially
interrupted for decades. Additional trapping at Kambarata‑1 will reinforce this cascade‑scale reduction but is not
expected to generate new or measurable impacts on soil fertility downstream of Toktogul. Agricultural productivity in
the Syr Darya Basin depends primarily on irrigation infrastructure rather than floodplain sediment deposition. These
issues are addressed further in the Cumulative Impact Assessment (Chapter 13).
Immediately downstream of the Kambarata‑1 HPP dam, sediment starvation impacts such as channel incision, bed
coarsening, or habitat degradation will not occur, since the Kambarata‑2 HPP reservoir already inundates the reach.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                     Page 33
Description of Impacts



However, the cumulative loss of sediment supply across the cascade has implications for Syr Darya morphology,
floodplain dynamics, and deltaic processes. These are transboundary issues that require ongoing monitoring and
cooperative management at basin scale.
Occasional drawdown or maintenance operations may cause limited resuspension of fine material within the
Kambarata‑1 HPP reservoir. Some of this may be transported into the upper Kambarata‑2 HPP reservoir during high‑flow
events, but the magnitude will be negligible relative to long‑term trapping. Overall, sediment‑related impacts during
operation are considered high significance, given the scale of permanent trapping and its long‑term consequences for
storage capacity and cascade sediment continuity.



10.4.1.16              Significant Impacts
Monitoring of sediment deposition patterns and capacity loss in the Kambarata‑1 HPP reservoir should be incorporated
into the long‑term reservoir management plan. AFRY’s updated sedimentation study (2025) confirms that the
designated dead storage volume will gradually fill under the expected sediment load (~11.5 million m³/year), with
cumulative deposition reaching ~573 million m³ over 50 years (approximately 11% of gross capacity). Because no
sediment sluicing infrastructure is planned and the bottom outlet is for inspection only, essentially all sediment will be
retained within the reservoir. These findings confirm the long‑term significance of sediment management requirements.
Impacts have been evaluated using a standard significance scoring framework based on magnitude (M), extent (E), and
duration (D), as outlined in the ESIA methodology. Table 10-8 presents a consolidated summary of all identified impacts
related to sediment transport across the project lifecycle.

A detailed sedimentation assessment is being prepared by AFRY to determine whether long-term sediment
accumulation within the designated dead storage zone of the Kambarata-1 HPP will provide adequate management
capacity without requiring active sediment flushing. As of this ESIA draft, no sediment sluicing infrastructure is
planned, and the bottom outlet is not intended for sediment release. The significance of this passive management
approach will be reassessed upon completion of the AFRY sedimentation report, expected later in 2025. These
findings should be revisited once the final AFRY sedimentation assessment becomes available, and the significance
ratings updated accordingly if required.

Groundwater
Groundwater within the project area occurs primarily as fissure water in fractured metamorphic and granitic rocks, and
as alluvial pore water in the riverbed and terraces of the Naryn River. Springs are scattered throughout the gorge,
particularly near the base of slopes where geological contacts and faults enhance groundwater emergence. While the
volume and extent of groundwater use in the project footprint are limited, the physical and chemical integrity of local
springs and shallow aquifers may be affected by excavation, tunnelling, and reservoir impoundment activities. Impacts
are likely to be spatially localised but may persist beyond the construction period, particularly where flows are
intercepted or redirected by underground works.

10.4.1.17              Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
During the pre-construction phase, activities are limited to site mobilisation, vegetation clearance, early geotechnical
investigations, and the construction of temporary access roads and laydown areas. These activities are unlikely to cause
significant impacts to groundwater resources, though localised effects are possible. Soil compaction and the
construction of temporary drainage systems may reduce infiltration rates temporarily, particularly in areas with
permeable soils. This could lead to localised disruption to groundwater recharge.
The risk of contamination from site runoff or spills is low but should be managed through basic mitigation measures
such as bunded fuel storage and sediment control. No significant impacts on groundwater quality or flow are anticipated
during this phase, as it does not involve excavation at depth or in-river works.

10.4.1.18              Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
During the construction phase, significant physical interventions will occur, including tunnel excavation, river diversion,
and the early stages of dam construction. A key activity in this phase is the first fill of the reservoir, which presents
unique risks to groundwater systems, particularly in areas where the project intersects fractured rock aquifers and
spring-fed groundwater sources.
Excavation for tunnels and the river diversion channel will create temporary disruptions in local groundwater flow.
These activities are expected to interfere with natural groundwater pathways, particularly where tunnels pass through
fractured rock zones. While these disruptions are likely to be localised, their effect on nearby springs should be closely
monitored. This impact is considered moderate, as the extent of disruption depends on the proximity of tunnelling to
spring-fed groundwater systems and the characteristics of the underlying geology.
The risk of contamination from construction activities, such as blasting, grouting, and dewatering during tunnel
excavation, is moderate. While specific contaminants are not identified at this stage, it is possible that suspended solids
or chemicals associated with construction materials could mobilise into the groundwater. It will be important to
implement water quality monitoring during and after construction to track any changes in water chemistry. This impact
is considered moderate, based on the possibility of mobilisation, but specific contaminants have not yet been identified.
Monitoring will be essential to assess the scale of any changes in water quality.
 The first fill of the reservoir presents a distinct set of hydrogeological and ecological risks. As impoundment progresses,
the groundwater table will rise along the reservoir perimeter, potentially saturating adjacent slopes and altering pre-
existing flow paths. This process may generate hydraulic uplift pressures in the dam foundation and near-reservoir
zones, as well as initiate slope instability or spring emergence in unconfined aquifers. In ecological terms, the rising
water table is likely to cause waterlogging of root zones in terrestrial vegetation situated near or below the Full Supply
Level (FSL) of 1198 m. Land cover mapping within the broader study area—including the dam site, reservoir footprint,
and a 3 km reach downstream of Kambarata-1—indicates the presence of extensive grassland, cold desert, and rocky
habitats, interspersed with wetland and shrub-dominated patches. These systems may be vulnerable to altered
groundwater regimes, particularly where saturation persists seasonally or year-round. Although some habitats lie



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                     Page 36
Description of Impacts



outside the final inundation zone, they may still be affected by fringe groundwater rise due to capillarity, lateral flow,
or perched aquifer interactions.
Based on land cover classification and hydrogeological context, wetland patches and spring emergence zones may occur
within and adjacent to the reservoir footprint. These features are potentially sustained by shallow groundwater
discharge and may support discrete groundwater-dependent ecosystems. As the reservoir fills and the hydrostatic
gradient shifts, such features could experience flow reversal, drying, or permanent inundation, depending on their
location and elevation. This impact is considered high due to the irreversible nature of vegetation loss and the potential
degradation of aquifer-linked ecological systems.
In addition, while not confirmed by current baseline studies, two potential risks have been identified that warrant
precautionary consideration in the ESMP:
    •      The removal of unconsolidated and weathered surficial materials during construction (e.g. quarrying, slope
           cuts, road or powerhouse excavation) may locally alter recharge pathways or intercept perched aquifers that
           sustain groundwater-dependent ecosystems (GDEs). Although no GDEs have been explicitly mapped in these
           zones, targeted pre-clearance inspections should be implemented to identify and preserve any sensitive
           features encountered during works.
    •      The filling and long-term operation of the reservoir may affect the availability or quality of water in natural or
           artificial waterholes, particularly if they are supported by shallow groundwater discharge. This could impact
           wildlife access to seasonal or permanent water sources. In the absence of site-specific inventories, monitoring
           of groundwater levels and spring behaviour in areas adjacent to known ecological habitats is recommended
           during early operations, to verify that ecological function is maintained.

10.4.1.19              Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance.
During the operational phase, the primary impact on groundwater will be due to the first impoundment of the reservoir,
which will cause a permanent change in the groundwater table along the reservoir perimeter. The long-term effects on
groundwater flow, levels, and quality will need to be closely monitored, particularly in areas near springs and
groundwater-dependent ecosystems.
Seepage from the reservoir into surrounding groundwater systems may alter water quality, particularly if contaminants
from the reservoir bed or surrounding rock formations are mobilised. While specific contaminants are not identified at
this stage, the potential for changes in groundwater chemistry exists. This impact is considered moderate, as seepage
may lead to minor but long-term changes in groundwater quality, which would need to be monitored regularly.
The operational phase will also be impacted by potential fluctuations in groundwater levels due to changes in reservoir
water levels during maintenance or emergency operations. These fluctuations could draw groundwater from
surrounding areas, potentially destabilising adjacent slopes or altering groundwater flow in the surrounding
environment. This impact is considered low, as the effects will be localised and temporary, though they may still need
to be managed through monitoring.

Geology and Ground Conditions
This section identifies and characterises the activities affecting the geological and geomorphological Valued
Environmental Components (VECs) within the study area, including potential ground contamination, land erosion,
deposition, and stability issues. It assesses the magnitude and duration of those impacts as well as the sensitivity of the
VECs.

10.4.1.21              Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
During the pre-construction phase, preliminary site development activities such as vegetation clearance, access road
formation, and establishment of laydown areas and temporary infrastructure will disturb the ground surface and expose
underlying geological materials. These activities will be localised but have the potential to affect slope stability and soil
structure, particularly in steep terrain or where weak geological units are present. Anticipated impacts and their
mechanisms are summarised below.
    •      Surface disturbance associated with site clearance, access road development, and establishment of temporary
           infrastructure is expected to degrade topsoil integrity, expose underlying strata, and increase susceptibility to
           wind and water erosion. This will expose previously stable geological formations to erosion and landslide
           processes. Vegetation clearance and site stripping will reduce soil cohesion and root reinforcement, increasing
           susceptibility to shallow slope failures and surface erosion. Soil bearing capacity may be reduced in localised
           areas due to waterlogging or repeated loading from construction vehicles.
    •      Excavation and surface disturbance may expose fractured rock, leading to minor loosening or disintegration,
           especially in weathered or weak strata. Similarly, the rock massifs will be weakened and experience fractures
           during the blasting activity.
    •      Excavations for temporary facilities, access roads, and laydown areas could result in localized destabilization of
           slopes, especially in areas of steep terrain or weak geological substrates. Stockpiling of construction materials
           and waste could lead to soil compaction and localized ground contamination through accidental spills of fuels,
           lubricants, and chemicals.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3            Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                              SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                           11 August 2025                                 Page 38
Description of Impacts



The magnitude of these impacts is considered low to moderate depending on the sensitivity of the terrain, with impacts
generally short to medium term if appropriately managed through erosion control measures.

10.4.1.22              Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
Construction-phase activities will involve major earthworks, excavation of the dam and tunnel foundations, river
diversion, and associated infrastructure development. These works will significantly modify natural topography and
disturb geological formations across multiple zones of the project area. Additional impacts are expected during initial
reservoir filling, which will introduce new hydrostatic and geotechnical forces on the surrounding landscape.
     •     During the construction phase, major earthworks, dam construction, river diversion, and excavation of
           foundations will substantially alter local topography and slope profiles due to excavation, cut-and-fill
           operations, and river diversion works. Extensive excavation and blasting during dam and tunnel construction
           will significantly alter slope geometry and may reduce slope factors of safety, particularly in geotechnically
           sensitive areas such as those with unconsolidated sediments, weathered marls, or steep topography.
     •     Blasting activities for quarrying or foundation preparation may cause vibrations leading to micro-cracking of
           adjacent rock formations, potentially triggering landslides or slope failures in vulnerable areas. Spoil disposal if
           not managed carefully could further destabilize slopes and contribute to sedimentation downstream.
     •     Rocks exposed during construction are subject to mechanical destruction (vibrations and blasting) and chemical
           deflation (from gypsum, salts, marls). Clay-rich and evaporitic rocks (e.g., shales, marls, and salts) may deform
           slowly under load through creep, particularly in saturated conditions.
     •     Initial reservoir filling will lead to saturation of adjacent slopes, increasing the likelihood of landslides and
           shoreline erosion as previously dry soils become waterlogged. Fluctuating water levels during this early stage
           will exacerbate instability along the reservoir perimeter.
Magnitude of impact is assessed as moderate for localized areas with sensitive geology (e.g., unconsolidated sediments,
steep slopes), and duration is medium to long-term depending on stabilization measures implemented.

10.4.1.23              Phase 3 - Operation Phase including maintenance
Long-term operation of the reservoir will result in sustained geotechnical changes due to cyclic drawdown, hydrostatic
loading, and continuous interaction between water and geological formations. These processes may lead to progressive
deterioration of foundation materials and increased risk of slope instability. These processes can affect the stability of
the structure and require constant monitoring.
Changes in the soils and rocks of the foundation will occur due to long-term deformations, filtration process and
suffusion, chemical deflation and corrosion. Consolidation process can be an example of long-term deformation at
which the clayey soils continue to slowly compact under the load. Other examples are creep which is possible in weak
zones (clay shale, salts) and gradual dissolution as well as formation of voids of karst rocks (gypsum, limestone).
Improper drainage or seepage control of small particles (sand, sandy loam) through the dam body or base may lead to
the formation of filtration channels. Likewise, the development of karst in gypsum and limestone will increase water
permeability. Chemical deterioration and corrosion processes may occur due to sulphate attack, particularly in gypsum-
bearing rocks, which can affect concrete and foundation materials. Salinization of soil in the evaporation zone, in arid
regions is another case which leads to chemical deflation.
Bank erosion can lead to gradual land loss and instability, particularly where soils are unconsolidated. Seepage through
the dam foundation and abutments, if not properly managed, could result in internal erosion (piping) that threatens
dam stability and the surrounding geological formations. It is also necessary to consider that in the flood zone there are
Neogene deposits, which consist of gypsum, rock salts, gravel stone, clays, marls, sands, which during the construction
and operation of the dam can carry the following changes.
a)   Gypsum (CaSO₄·2H₂O)
Dissolution and karst formation:
     -     Upon contact with water, gypsum can dissolve, forming voids and caverns, resulting in the strength reduction
           of the base;
     -     Intensification of karst processes when the groundwater level changes (for example, during drainage or
           flooding);



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                        Page 39
Description of Impacts



Swelling/shrinkage:
     -     When moistened, gypsum can swell slightly, and when dry, it cracks.
b) Rock salt (NaCl, halite)
Intensive dissolution:
     -     Even weak water exchange leads to rapid desalinization, the formation of funnels and sinkholes
     -     Sudden subsidence is possible when opening salt layers.
Creep:
     -     Under load, salt can slowly deform, which is dangerous for the stability of structures.
c)   Gravel Stones (cemented gravel rocks)
Failure of cementation:
     -     During opening and vibrations from equipment, the binder material (clay, carbonates) can collapse, turning
           gravel stones into loose gravel and pebble deposits.
Change of filtration properties:
     -     Cracking increases water permeability, which requires additional waterproofing measures.
d) Marl (a mixture of clay and carbonates)
Softening while moistening:
     -     When saturated with water, marl loses strength and can turn into a plastic state.
Weathering and disintegration:
     -     Outdoors, it breaks down quickly to form a clayey material that is prone to erosion.
e) Sands (different-grain size)
Compaction under the load:
     -     Under the weight of equipment and bulk materials, the sands are compacted, but local subsidence is possible.
Suffusion (removal of fine particles):
     -     When water is filtered, fine fractions are washed out, which can lead to the formation of voids.
Liquefaction risk in saturated fine sands
     -     At a high level of water saturation, sand can turn into a quicksand state, complicating excavation works.
Maintenance activities, such as dredging of sediments or repair of embankments and roads, may cause additional
localized disturbances but are expected to be minor compared to the initial construction impacts. Overall, the
magnitude of operational phase impacts is considered moderate but long-term, requiring continuous monitoring and
maintenance to ensure stability.

Soils
The Impact Assessment exercise was conducted based on the findings from baseline soil quality monitoring results of
winter season. It discusses the effects on Valued Environmental and Social Components (VECs) of Soil from different
project activities across each phase of the project.

10.4.1.25              Findings from First Season, Baseline Soil Monitoring, 2024
Environmental Baseline Monitoring of the Kambarata-1 HPP area was conducted from 14th to 23rd October 2024, where
soil samples from 7 locations were analysed for pH, sulphate, chloride & iron content, percentage of organic carbon,
heavy metal contents and mineral contents. The findings of the first season monitoring with respect to soil quality, are
presented in Chapter 8 of the ESIA report and summarized below.
    •      pH Levels: The soil pH ranges from 7.22 to 7.68, slightly alkaline.
    •      Sulphate, Chloride & Total Iron Content:
                 -     Sulphate: 102.5 to 426±59.6 mg/kg
                 -     Chloride: 115±24.2 to 142±21.3 mg/kg
    •      Total Iron: 115.2 to 387±77.4 mg/kg
    •      Heavy Metal Contents
                 -     Lead (Pb): 2.46 to 19.95 mg/kg
                 -     Cadmium (Cd): > 2 mg/kg
                 -     Arsenic (As): > 2 mg/kg
                 -     Mercury (Hg): 0.23 to 1.32 mg/kg
In addition to the monitoring conducted in October 2024, there are plans to carry out another round of monitoring in
Spring Season (April 2025). This upcoming assessment aims to capture the seasonal variations in soil quality following
the winter season. The Impact Assessment exercise has been carried out in context of the findings of the October 2025
baseline soil quality monitoring results, where the impacts on VECs from various project activities under each phase of
the project has been discussed.
The sub-sequent sections illustrate the impact on Soil Quality VEC, due to various activities at each phase of the project
i.e.,
    •      Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
    •      Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
    •      Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance

10.4.1.26 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
    •      Impact on soil quality for construction of Access Road/ Bridge: Construction activities can significantly impact
           soil quality by introducing various pollutants and altering its natural properties. During construction, oils,
           chemicals and waste from machinery & material handling, can seep into the soil, leading to contamination.
           This pollution can harm plant and animal life, disrupt microbial activity, and degrade overall soil health.
           Additionally, the disturbance of natural soil layers during excavation and other construction processes can
           cause changes in soil pH, organic carbon and mineral content.
           Now, the intensity of this impact will be around 5-10% of the available resource. This impact will spread <5 Km
           outside the project footprint. This impact will persist for <10 years. Hence the scoring for magnitude, extent
           and duration of this impact are considered as 20, 20 & 5 respectively and total score (45) indicates low score
           impact.
    •      Soil contamination due to installation of Power Supply Systems: The installation of power supply systems,
           particularly those involving DG sets and mobile equipment, can have a detrimental impact on soil quality as
           fuel, waste oil and grease spills can seep into the soil, leading to contamination

10.4.1.27 Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
    •      Improper handling of spoil material generated at Secondary Access Roads and Tunnels: The construction of
           secondary access roads and tunnels can adversely affect soil quality if spoil material is improperly handled. This
           spoil material mixed with drilling and tunnelling oils may contaminate the soil, leading to degradation of soil
           health and fertility.
           Now, the intensity of this impact will be around 5-10% of the available resource. This impact will spread <5 Km
           outside the project footprint. This impact will persist for 10-20 years. Hence the scoring for magnitude, extent
           and duration of this impact are considered as 20, 20 & 10 respectively and total score (50) indicates low score
           impact.
    •      Impact on soil quality due to Construction Site Operations: Construction site operations, including activities
           at crushing plants, workshops, quarries and batching plants, can impact soil quality in various ways:
                 -     Fugitive Dust Emissions: Crushing plants and quarries generate fugitive dust emissions, which can
                       settle on the soil and alter its physio-chemical properties. This dust can change the soil's texture,
                       reduce its fertility and affect its ability to support plant life.
                 -     Heavy Metal Contamination: In workshops, the handling of lubricants, diesel and other materials can
                       lead to spills and leaks. These incidents can introduce heavy metals such as lead (Pb) and cadmium
                       (Cd) into the soil. The presence of these heavy metals can be toxic to plants and microorganisms,
                       disrupting the soil ecosystem and reducing soil health.
                 -     Management of Wastewater from Batching Plant and Effluent Treatment Plant (ETP) at worker’s
                       camp: Improper handling of wastewater from batching plants, as well as from ETP at workers’ camp,
                       can result in seepage into the soil. This wastewater can carry various contaminants that alter the soil's
                       pH and fertility. Changes in pH can affect nutrient availability and microbial activity, further degrading
                       soil quality.
           Overall, these construction site operations can lead to adverse effects on soil health, impacting its ability to
           support vegetation and maintain ecological balance.
           Now, the intensity of this impact will be <5% of the available resource. This impact will spread <5 Km outside
           the project footprint. This impact will persist for 10-15 years. Hence the scoring for magnitude, extent and
           duration of this impact are considered as 10, 20 & 10 respectively and total score (40) indicates low score
           impact.
    •      Improper disposal of muck generated at Main RCC Dam and foundation base of powerhouse: The excavation
           work for the dam foundation and powerhouse will generate significant amount of muck, which consists of
           fragmented rock, soil debris and dust particles. These mucks if not managed properly may impact the soil

           Now, the intensity of this impact will be around 5-10% of the available resource. This impact will spread <5 Km

           and duration of this impact are considered as 20, 20 & 10 respectively and total score (50) indicates low score
           impact.

10.4.1.30                 Pre-Construction Phase (Including Site Clearance)
Overview: The pre-construction phase initiates significant land transformation due to site clearance and the
establishment of essential construction facilities.
Key Activities during Pre-construction Phase is as follows:
•        Site clearance and vegetation removal
•        Establishment and construction of labor camps
•        Construction of access roads and a bridge over the Naryn River
•        Installation of electricity transmission lines, water and sewerage systems, heating and air supply systems
•        Setting up of aggregate production and batching plant
Impacts
•        The pre-construction phase of the project may lead to transformations in the land use & land cover due to site
         clearance and the establishment of key construction support facilities as listed above. Although these installations
         are for construction purposes only, these may significantly alter the existing land use and land cover of the area.
•        The development of these facilities may necessitate the removal of approximately 1 hectares of tree cover,
         primarily within the proposed crushing plant area. This vegetation loss may lead to a decline in local biodiversity,
         elimination of habitat for small mammals and bird species, and a reduction in carbon sequestration potential. In
         addition, around 4.51 hectares of grassland which may potentially impact livelihood of dependant local
         communities.
•        Construction of the workshop facility may require the clearance of approximately 3 hectares of shrub and bush
         land, with additional vegetation removal for associated infrastructure. This may lead to habitat fragmentation,
         interference with faunal movement, and noticeable changes to the visual landscape.
•        Furthermore, some of the area under exposed rocky surfaces and rock outcrops will be converted to industrial
         area where crushing plant & batching plant will be set up.

10.4.1.31                 Construction Phase (Including Initial Reservoir Filling)
Overview: The construction phase involves large-scale earthmoving activities, structural works, and the initial filling of
the reservoir. This phase will cause irreversible changes in land use, converting terrestrial ecosystems into aquatic
environments and formation of industrial zones.
Key Activities:
         •    Secondary Access Roads/site roads construction
         •    Construction Site Operation (Crushing plant, workshops, quarries and batching plant)
         •    Main RCC Dam (including Spillway and Outlets) construction
         •    River Diversion Tunnel (Excavation and Installation)
         •    Coffer Dam Construction (Upstream and Downstream)
         •    HPP Water Intake (Excavation and Installation)
         •    Headrace Tunnels and Shafts (Excavation and Installation)
         •    Powerhouse Building (Excavation and Installation)
         •    Tailrace Channel (Excavation and Installation)
         •    Substation Construction
         •    Reservoir Filling (“First Fill”)
Impacts:
•        During the construction phase, land use change will be significant due to change in large land area for quarrying.
         However, the existing land use of the quarries is Exposed Surface/ Rock Outcrop, where quarrying will be carried
         out ; and permanent alteration of land use and land cover is envisaged.
•        Existing grassland and shrub-dominated areas will be converted into industrial use, for workshops and quarrying.
         Hence, this change may lead to a lasting transformation from vegetated terrain to altered hardened surfaces,
         potentially impact soil quality including fertility and causing adverse impact on ecosystem.
•        The construction phase may include initial reservoir filling, which will result in the permanent submergence of
         large tracts of land, including about 10.7 sq. km of grassland and about 8 sq. km of vegetated land, permanently
         altering the land use and land cover to waterbody. This will adversely impact economic livelihood of communities
         dependant on livestock.

10.4.1.32                 Phase 3- Operational Phase including Maintenance
Post-construction, the use of some of the facilities like batching/crushing plant, quarry, workshop ceases or is reduced.
These areas would be restored and rehabilitated but the LULC will be primarily altered.
Key Activities:
•        Operation of the dam and hydroelectric facility
•        Management of water flow and reservoir levels
•        Maintenance of access roads, tunnels, and energy infrastructure
Impacts:
•        Tree cover, shrubs, and grasslands once cleared for construction are unlikely to regenerate naturally without
         dedicated restoration efforts. Quarry sites, if left unrestored after the useful life of project construction, may pose
         safety risks due to unstable slopes or abandoned pits.
•        There will be permanent submergence of about 10.7 sq. km of grassland and about 8 sq. km of vegetated land,
         permanently altering the landuse and land cover to waterbody. This will adversely impact economic livelihood of
         communities dependant on livestock.
