Waste Management
Identification of the activities contributing to or affecting the capacity of existing facilities to deal with Wastes
generated by the Project.

This section presents a comprehensive assessment of the potential environmental and social impacts associated with
waste generation throughout the various phases of the Kambarata-1 HPP—Pre-Construction, Construction, and
Operation. The project involves large-scale infrastructure development, including dam construction, tunnelling,
installation of power systems, and support facilities, each contributing to the production of diverse waste types.
The types of waste likely to be generated from various phases of Kambarata-1 HPP include biodegradable waste (e.g.,
vegetation and organic matter), inert waste (e.g., soil, rubble, and excavation debris), construction and demolition
waste, and potentially hazardous waste (e.g., waste lubricants and shuttering oil, used oil, STP sludge, biomedical
waste). These wastes, if not properly managed, may adversely affect key Valued Environmental Components (VECs)
such as Land Environment with particular emphasis on sub-VEC Soil Quality, Water Quality, Visual and Aesthetic
Values, and Human Health & Safety.
This chapter adopts a VEC-wise and activity-wise approach to evaluate the nature, extent, and significance of impacts
arising from waste generation during each project phase. It also presents qualitative and quantitative scoring
(magnitude, extent, and duration) to determine the severity of each impact. Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including
site clearance

10.4.1.34 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
During the Pre-Construction Phase (Phase 1) of the Kambarata-1 HPP, several key activities such as site clearing,
excavation, and installation of infrastructure would generate various types of waste, including biodegradable dry waste,
debris (earth, stumps, rocks), and construction-related materials. While these wastes are generally non-hazardous
under normal operating conditions, their mismanagement can lead to a range of environmental and social impacts. This
includes degradation of soil quality, visual and aesthetic conditions, water quality, and potential risks to human health.
The following section provides a focused VEC-wise and activity-wise analysis of these waste-related impacts, specifically
during the pre-construction phase.
The Pre-Construction Phase (Phase 1) of the Kambarata-1 HPP involves a series of preparatory activities such as:
•        Land clearing and site excavation


    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3            Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                              SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                           11 August 2025                              Page 51
Description of Impacts



•        Installation of utilities (power, water, sewerage, heating, air systems)
•        Establishment of aggregate production and concrete mixing systems
•        Development of construction sites and worker camps
The VEC wise impact on the activities related to Pre-Construction Phase is mentioned below-
1. Impact of Land Environment (Soil Quality): During pre-construction phase, activities involving site preparation,
   including land clearing, excavation, material storage and vegetation clearance will result in the generation of
   biodegradable dry waste besides debris like earth, stumps and rocks. These wastes are not envisaged to pose any
   significant impact to the soil quality or the soil characteristics due to shorter duration and limited to site and its
   immediate vicinity only, thereby resulting in poor aesthetics in the locality and visual impairment when stockpiled
   or left unmanaged at site indiscriminately. Leakage of spillage from earth moving equipment and machinery and
   vehicles carrying materials to the site are not envisaged to be significant under regular operating conditions to impact
   the soil considerably.
      In addition, the establishment of the construction camp involves site preparation activities such as land clearing,
      excavation, vegetation removal, and material storage, all of which is likely to contribute to the generation of
      biodegradable dry waste and inert debris, including earth, stumps, and rocks. If these wastes are left unmanaged or
      indiscriminately stockpiled at the site or along transportation routes to disposal locations, they can negatively affect
      soil characteristics and significantly degrade the visual and aesthetic quality of the area. However, given the nature
      and limited scale of the activities, these impacts are primarily confined to aesthetic concerns and are assessed to be
      low in terms of their effect on the overall soil quality of the locality.
      Now, the magnitude of this impact will range between <5% to upto10% of the available resources. This impact will
      be mostly limited to project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. Most of the
      impacts is envisaged to persist for <10 years with some impacts persisting >10 years. Hence the overall impact
      significance scoring varies between 35 to 45, indicating low impact.
2. Impact on Visual and Aesthetics of the Local Community and Workers: During pre-construction phase, activities
   involving site preparation, including land clearing, excavation, material storage and vegetation clearance will result
   in the generation of biodegradable dry waste besides and debris like earth, stumps and rocks. These wastes when
   indiscriminately stored at site or disposed indiscriminately in the site and in the vicinity; may result in poor aesthetics
   in the locality and visual impairment when stockpiled or left unmanaged. This may impact the community staying in
   proximity of the work zone or along the haulage route(s) of these wastes to the designated disposal site, and the
   workers onsite. However, considering the pre-construction activities would be for a short duration, the impact is
   envisaged to be low to moderate depending upon quantum of waste generated.
      During the construction of Workers Camp, Potential for generation of biodegradable dry waste besides and debris
      like earth, stumps and rocks from the camp location due to site preparation, including land clearing, excavation,
      material storage and vegetation clearance which may pose impact on the local aesthetic conditions and visual
      impairment when stockpiled or left unmanaged at site indiscriminately, and during transportation to the disposal
      location(s). However, impacts are mostly limited to poor visual and aesthetics and are envisaged to be moderate
      considering their short-duration and no major health impact on the workers or local inhabitants.
      Now, the magnitude of this impact will be >10% of the available resources. This impact will be mostly limited to
      project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. Most of the impacts is envisaged to
      persist for <10 years and are reversible in nature without remediation. Hence the overall impact significance scoring
      is 85, indicating moderate impact.
3. Impact on Human Health and Safety (construction workers and community): the potential impact on human health
   due to the following activities are given below-
         −       Access Road / Bridge Construction: Impact on Occupational Health and Safety and Community Health and
                 Safety due to exposure to wastes arising from pre-construction activities during waste handling (loading and
                 unloading) and haulage (transportation for disposal/reuse) of the wastes to the disposal/reuse site
                 respectively.
         −       Installation of Power Supply System: Impact on occupational health and safety of the worker during handling
                 and transportation of the pre-construction wastes. Community staying close to the transportation route may
                 be vulnerable to impact though direct contact with the waste is not envisaged.




    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007

         −       Installation of Water Supply & Sewerage System: Impact on occupational health and safety of the worker
                 during handling and transportation of the pre-construction wastes. Community staying close to the

      Now, the magnitude of this impact will range between <5% to upto10% of the available resources. This impact will
      be mostly limited to project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. The impacts are
      envisaged to persist for <10 years. Hence the overall impact significance scoring varies between 35 to 45, indicating
      low impact.
4. Impact on Water Quality and Aquatic Flora and Fauna: During site preparation for developing Access Road / Bridge
   Construction, Installation of Power Supply System, Installation of Water Supply & Sewerage System, Installation of
   Heating & Air Supply System and Aggregate Production and Concrete Mixing System in construction of Worker’s
   Camp; no activities are envisaged that would generate wastes with a potential to contaminate the surface or
   groundwater quality under normal operating conditions (i.e., except accidental spill over/disposal). However, run-
   off may carry debris like earth, stumps and rocks and vegetation wastes into the surface water bodies impacting the
   Physico-chemical characteristics of the Naryn River or streams feeding into it, increasing the turbidity due to silt load,
   pH and nutrient content in the water beside enhanced methane emissions and impact on the existing Riparian
   species. However, considering Naryn River is not used for human consumption immediately downstream of the
   construction zones, and the natural self-purification by the river, the impact is assessed to be low to moderate on
   the water quality and riparian and aquatic flora & fauna during the pre-construction phase due to varying quantum
   of wastes carried by the run-off from each of the above activities.
      Now, the magnitude of this impact will be >10% of the available resources. This impact will be mostly limited to
      project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. The impacts are envisaged to persist
      for <10 years. Hence the overall impact significance scoring varies between 85 to 45, indicating moderate to low
      impact.

10.4.1.35 Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
During the construction phase of the Kambarata-1 HPP, several major engineering activities are undertaken, each
contributing to the generation of different types of waste. These include:
•        Construction of Secondary Access Roads and Tunnel Networks
•        Operation of Construction Sites, including Crushing Plants, Workshops, Quarries, and Batching Plants
•        Construction of the Main Roller Compacted Concrete (RCC) Dam, including Spillway and Outlet Structures
•        Excavation and installation of the River Diversion Tunnel
•        Construction of Cofferdams (both Upstream and Downstream)
•        Excavation and installation of the Hydropower Plant (HPP) Water Intake Structure
Each of these activities is associated with the generation of biodegradable waste (e.g., vegetation and organic debris),
inert materials (e.g., soil, rocks, and rubble), construction and demolition waste, and potentially hazardous substances
(e.g., fuel or lubricant spills). If not properly managed, these wastes can affect soil quality, visual and aesthetic values,
water quality, biodiversity and human health and safety. The following section provides a VEC-wise and activity-wise
assessment of potential waste-related impacts during this phase.
1. Impact of Land/Soil Quality:
         −       Construction of Secondary Access Roads and Tunnel Networks: The potential impacts anticipated from this
                 activity include:
                    o     Potential for land contamination due to indiscriminate handling and disposal, spillage and leakage of hazardous
                          wastes e.g. waste oil, used oil, oil rags, chemical contaminated soil/earth/tars arising from operation and

                       maintenance of construction equipment, construction vehicles and machinery, into the adjoining agricultural
                       impacting soil quality and productivity, potentially contaminating the soil due to leachates formation.

                 o     Indiscriminate storage and disposal of muck and spoils generated from tunnelling and blasting activities, and

                 o     Potential for land contamination due to indiscriminate handling, storage, and disposal, spillage and leakage of

                 o     Excess and unused concrete wastes generated during construction have the potential to degrade the soil quality

                 o     Stockpiles of excavation waste if stored on unpaved and open area, the same may be blown away by wind or rain
                       and spread on the adjoining land surfaces impacting its quality and productivity.
                 o     Excess and unused concrete wastes generated during construction have the potential to degrade the soil quality
                       if the same is disposed indiscriminately on the open and unpaved surface.
                 o     Metal wastes if not properly segregated and stored for reuse/recycling may get mixed with the unpaved adjoining
                       soil/surface and reduce the soil quality.
   Now, the magnitude of this impact will range between 5-10 and >10% of the available resources. This impact will be
   mostly limited to project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. However, some
   impacts have the potential to extend beyond 5km radius. Most of the impacts are reversible in nature with few
   impacts needing mitigation/remediation measures for restoration and is envisaged to persist for <10 years with
   some impacts persisting >10 years. Hence the overall impact significance scoring varies between 25 to 90, indicating
   low to moderate impact.

   some impacts persisting >10 years. Hence the overall impact significance scoring varies between 25 to 90, indicating
   low to moderate impact.
3. Impact on Visual and Aesthetics:
During construction phase, in each stage the construction activities, raw materials transportation and unloading,
material handling and transfer, movement and operation of equipment and vehicles will result in waste generation. The
muck and wastes generated from these activities if not stored, handled and disposed in an environmentally sound
manner may result in nuisance not only at the site but also in the areas adjoining the project area and the nearest
settlements through which material and waste transport takes place. This may impact the visual and aesthetic
conditions of the workers in the work zone and labour camp, and the community staying in proximity of the work zone
or along the haulage route(s) of these wastes to the designated disposal site.
Potential for generation of construction wastes, hazardous wastes and biodegradable wastes like sewage sludge from
the labour camp may pose impact on the local aesthetic conditions and visual impairment when stockpiled, left
unmanaged or disposed indiscriminately at site and during transportation to the disposal location(s).
Now, the magnitude of this impact will range between 5-10 and >10% of the available resources. This impact will be
mostly limited to project footprint and its vicinity of radius <5 Km outside the project footprint. Most of the impacts are
reversible in nature with few impacts needing mitigation/remediation measures for restoration and is envisaged to

10.4.1.36 Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance
During the Operational Phase of the Kambarata-1 HPP, waste generation continues primarily from routine plant
functions and maintenance activities. While the intensity and volume of waste are significantly lower than in the
construction phase, key operational activities such as Flow Regulation, Infrastructure Maintenance, and Power
Generation still produce various types of waste. These include biodegradable waste (e.g., from canteen and
landscaping), hazardous waste (e.g., used oil, oily rags, and STP sludge), and biomedical waste (e.g., from the
Occupational Health Centre).
If unmanaged, these wastes could lead to localized environmental degradation, impacting soil and water quality, visual
aesthetics, and occupational health and safety. The following sections provide a VEC-wise and activity-wise evaluation
of potential impacts from waste generation during this phase.
1. Impact on Land Environment (Soil Quality):
•        Flow Regulation: No notable impact on soil quality is envisaged due to waste generation, handling and disposal
         activities arising during the flow regulation during operational phase because of the nature of operations/activities
         which will generate waste.
•        Infrastructure Maintenance: Hazardous Wastes like used oil, waste oil, oily rags, grease, STP sludge would be
         generated from the infrastructure maintenance which if stored, handled, and disposed indiscriminately may
         impact the soil quality by contaminating the soil. However, the impact is anticipated to be limited to project
         boundary only.
•        Power Production: No notable impact is envisaged due to waste generation, handling and disposal the power
         production in the operational phase because of the nature of operations/activities which will generate waste.

         impact the occupational health and safety of the O&M workers by posing fire hazards, explosions and other
         occupational hazards.
         -      Biomedical waste would be generated from Occupational Health Centre (OHC) of the plant area which would

Traffic and Transport
Introduction

The development of the Kambarata-1 HPP is structured in multiple phases, such as Pre-Construction, Construction, and
Operation, each exerting varying levels of impact on the regional traffic and transportation infrastructure. This section
focuses on assessing the impacts on key roadways, specifically the Osh-Bishkek Highway and the approach road from

Construction, Construction, and Operation & Maintenance (O&M) phases of the project. These impacts affect key
Valued Environmental and Social Components (VECs), including traffic flows, road infrastructure, public transportation,
and road safety.
The study area for the traffic and transport study of the Kambarata-1 HPP encompasses all regions directly or indirectly
influenced by increased vehicular movement and transportation infrastructure usage during the pre-construction,
construction, and operational phases. This includes primary access roads that connect the dam site with regional supply
points, notably along the traffic flow monitoring points, TF_1 (Kara-Jygach), TF_2 (Uch-Terek – Connector to Kambarata
HPP 2 Route), and TF_3 (Kambarata Access Road – Direct project site access), as shown in Figure 4. These roads would
experience a surge in construction-related traffic such as trucks carrying cement, aggregates, equipment, and buses
transporting workers.
Manual classified traffic volume counts were conducted at three key locations for 24-hours during April, 2025, in both
directions (inbound and outbound) and analyzed for the following parameters:
    •      Total vehicular count and classification by type

Estimation of additional traffic due to the Project Activity:
For construction of all project components of the Kambarata-1 HPP, a total of 10.78 MCM of concrete will be required
to be produced, spanning across the entire timeline of the construction period. For production of concrete, the main
raw materials required will be cement, flyash and aggregates. For this purpose, cement and flyash will be required to
be procured from identified sources and will be delivered to the project site via trucks (25 tons assumed). However,
aggregates will be obtained from nearby identified quarry sites and taken to the site by means of conveyor belts. In
order to estimate the additional traffic load that will be associated with delivery of cement and flyash, it was assumed
that the monthly cement and flyash requirement would be 20,000 tons and 35,000 tons respectively, as referred in
AFRY FS May 2025. Based on these assumptions, the number of trucks for cement and flyash delivery during the
average and peak construction period has been presented in Table 10-17 below,

Impacts on the Traffic and Transportation due to the Project Activity:
The development of a hydropower plant will significantly affect local traffic and transport systems, primarily due to
increased movement of materials and personnel. These impacts can be broadly categorized into two types: material
transport, which includes the movement of construction equipment, machinery, and raw materials such as cement,
sand, and aggregates; and other transport, which involves the daily commute of workers, contractors, and support
staff. Both categories contribute to higher traffic volumes, potential road degradation, and increased risks of
accidents, especially in rural or ecologically sensitive areas. Proper planning and mitigation measures are essential to
minimize disruptions and ensure road safety.

Pre-Construction Phase
The pre-construction phase of the Kambarata HPP-1 project marks the initiation of site-level activities in preparation
for full-scale construction. Although not as intense as the construction period, this stage introduces significant
changes to the local traffic and transport systems. The key aspects and corresponding implications on Valued
Environmental Components (VECs) are outlined below:


    1.     Initiation of Project Mobilization
                 o     Activities include site surveys, topographical and geotechnical investigations, and land clearing.
                 o     Setup of temporary infrastructure such as worker camps, access roads, material storage areas, and
                       office units.
    2.     Traffic Flows
                 o     Moderate increase in traffic due to the movement of administrative personnel, survey teams, and
                       engineers.
                 o     Periodic movement of heavy-duty vehicles delivering excavation and drilling machinery.
                 o     Transport of construction materials (e.g., fencing, containers, prefabricated structures) for
                       temporary facilities.
                 o     Localized traffic congestion near access roads, especially during equipment unloading or peak
                       delivery periods.
    3.     Road Infrastructure
                 o     Use of rural and unpaved roads for initial site access causes wear and deterioration of surfaces.
                 o     Dust generation from construction vehicle movement, especially during dry periods, affects both
                       visibility and air quality.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3         Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                           SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                        11 August 2025                                 Page 62
Description of Impacts



     4.    Public Transportation
                 o     Increased vehicle presence may overlap with existing local transport routes, causing temporary
                       disruptions to community commuting schedules.
                 o     Lack of coordination may result in delays or route conflicts with public minivans
     5.    Road Safety
                 o     Absence of traffic control measures, temporary signage, and lighting around newly developed access
                       roads increases the risk of accidents.
                 o     Movement of construction vehicles during early morning or evening hours poses risks to pedestrians
                       and local traffic.
                 o     Unfamiliarity of local drivers with increased traffic volumes and types (e.g., flatbeds, trailers)
                       contributes to safety challenges near settlements.
Construction Phase
During the peak construction phase, the Kambarata-1 HPP is expected to witness a significant increase in traffic due to
the concurrent construction of the RCC main dam and powerhouse building, which would experience an overlapping of
35 months of construction (AFRY, June 2025) within the entire construction schedule.. This surge in traffic can be broadly
divided into two main categories: Material Transportation and Other Transport (including workforce movement,
service vehicles, and heavy equipment relocation). Each category is associated with specific environmental and social
impacts, particularly in the vicinity of the key traffic monitoring locations.
Impact due to Material Transportation

During the construction of the HPP, the following additional vehicles are envisaged in the roads leading to the Project
site-

     •     Cement Trucks (25-ton): Operating 93 trips per day during peak period, with assigned PCU value of 3.0 under
           HCM
     •     Fly Ash Trucks: With 163 daily trips during peak period,with assigned PCU values of 3.0 (HCM) [This estimate
           will not be under effect if Supplementary Cement Materials (SCM) will be used as alternative material to fly
           ash, available near the Dam Site.]
     •     Equipment Trucks: Averaging 1.5 trips per day (on a monthly basis), with assigned PCU values of 3.5 (HCM)
     •     40-seater Shuttle Buses: Making 175 trips daily during peak period, with assigned PCU values of 2.5 as per
           HCM
Monitoring Locations and Localized Impact Zones
The primary road corridors intersecting the following traffic monitoring stations are expected to bear the most
significant construction-related traffic load and associated environmental-social impacts:
TF_1 – Kara-Jygach (National Highway M41)
1.    Entry/Exit Point to National Highway Network:
      The TF1 point is located directly along the Osh–Bishkek Highway, near the settlement of Kara-Jygach. This makes
      it a key node for entry and exit of project-related transport. It serves as the principal link between the national
      road network and the project site, especially for heavy construction materials and machinery arriving from
      northern Kyrgyzstan and Bishkek.
2.    High Traffic Volume:
      The baseline assessment identifies this highway segment as a high-traffic corridor, with an average daily volume
      of 18310 PCUs, dominated by light vehicles (82.5%) and a moderate share of cargo trucks (18%). The road is
      already operating under stress, which may be exacerbated by construction-phase transport.
3.    Strategic Importance:
      This section of the corridor is not only crucial for long-haul freight and workforce movement but also interfaces
      with agricultural lands and residential zones near Kara-Jygach. Its proximity to populated areas implies increased
      risks of road safety, dust emissions, and noise, warranting focused mitigation during construction.

or impact the regional traffic network. The indicative layout of the access and haul road network, including all supporting

      areas near the project site.
•     High Traffic Volume:
      The baseline study shows the highest overall vehicle volume of 9,880 PCU/day, with a significant
      proportion of medium and heavy vehicles.

•     Transportation of cement from Osh, including:
•     Aravan Cement (~298 km distance by road)
•     Long-distance delivery of construction materials and specialized equipment

      zones. This requires specialized equipment such as cranes and trailers, and often temporary roads or
      platforms.

 ~15–20 monthly trips involving oversized or heavy equipment carrying large mechanical components like

•     Future Logistics Route: Although presently the traffic flow is low, as this is the only access road to the site
      for transfer of raw material, equipment and workforce.

The traffic impact analysis has been conducted using methodologies outlined in the Highway Capacity Manual
(HCM) to assess the Level of Service (LOS), which reflects the efficiency and quality of traffic flow. The evaluation
considers key parameters such as traffic Volume (V), road Capacity (C), and the Volume-to-Capacity (V/C) ratio
to determine road performance under baseline conditions as well as with the anticipated increase due to
construction-related traffic. The PCU/ Hour has been calculated considering 12 hours per day of peak
construction activity. The table below provides a comprehensive analysis of daily vehicle trips and their
corresponding Passenger Car Unit (PCU) values for different vehicle types required during construction of the
HPP-1.

     •     Baseline Conditions:
                 o     The estimated baseline traffic volume is 1,526 PCU/h, which slightly exceeds the designed road

                 o     This results in a V/C ratio of 1.09, indicating that demand is just above the infrastructure capacity.

                 o     An additional construction-related traffic load of 223 PCU/h is anticipated, primarily due to material

TF2 – Monitoring Point 2:
     •     Baseline Conditions:
                 o     The current traffic volume is 1,583 PCU/h against a road capacity of 1,400 PCU/h.

                 o     LOS is F, denoting poor traffic flow conditions, frequent delays, and compromised road safety and

                 o     The inclusion of 223 PCU/h from construction activities raises the total traffic volume to 1,806
                       PCU/h.
                 o     The revised V/C ratio increases to 1.29, further deteriorating operational efficiency.
                 o     LOS remains at F, underscoring critical congestion, increased queuing, and limited road functionality
                       during the construction phase.
TF3 – Monitoring Point 3 (Access Road):
     •     Baseline Conditions:
                 o     TF3 records a baseline traffic volume of 136 PCU/h, which is significantly lower than the road's
                       design capacity of 800 PCU/h.
                 o     The resulting V/C ratio is 0.17, indicative of underutilization and free-flowing traffic conditions.
                 o     LOS is A, reflecting optimal user experience with minimal delays or vehicle interactions.
     •     With Construction Traffic:
                 o     With the addition of 229 PCU/h from construction-related traffic, the total volume reaches 359
                       PCU/h.
                 o     The updated V/C ratio becomes 0.45, remaining well within acceptable capacity limits.
                 o     LOS will be changing to B, suggesting reasonably free flow of traffic even during peak construction
                       periods.
Both TF1 and TF2—located along the Osh-Bishkek Highway—are currently operating over their designed capacity (V/C
> 1.0) under baseline conditions. The introduction of construction traffic further exacerbates congestion levels,
leading to longer travel times and increasing the likelihood of traffic delays and safety risks during peak construction
activities. In contrast, the access road at TF3 shows minimal baseline traffic and remains well within capacity even

 after accounting for construction-related traffic. Consequently, significant traffic-related impacts are not anticipated
 for TF3 during the construction and operational phases of the project.
 However, in the addition of construction-related traffic to the existing baseline volumes is expected to increase road
 safety risks, which will be discussed in the subsequent section-
 A. Road Safety Risks
 TF_1: Kara-Jygach
 Kara-Jygach lies at a key nodal point where local roads intersect with regional highways. The influx of heavy vehicles
 including trucks carrying RCC material, cement, aggregate, equipment, and buses transporting workers will increase
 the risk of road accidents, especially at intersections and pedestrian crossings.
1.     Unregulated Junction:
       The visible intersection lacks traffic signals, signage, and channelization. With multiple informal access paths
       converging onto the main road, the potential for vehicle conflicts and accidents is high, particularly with increased
       construction traffic.
2.     Limited Visibility:
       The terrain and scattered structures along the junction may restrict clear sightlines for both local and project-
       related vehicles, increasing the risk of side collisions and pedestrian accidents.
3.     Vulnerable Road Users:
       Residential and agricultural structures are in close proximity to the road, suggesting regular use by pedestrians,
       livestock, and non-motorized traffic. These users are highly vulnerable to injury in the absence of dedicated lanes
       or crossings.
4.     Lack of Road Barriers and Lighting:
       Absence of roadside barriers, guardrails, and street lighting can lead to vehicles veering off the road, especially
       during night-time or adverse weather conditions.
5.     Soil Erosion and Dust:
       The surrounding unpaved areas may contribute to loose gravel and dust on the road surface, impacting braking
       efficiency and visibility, thereby compounding road safety risks.
6.     Emergency Access Risk:
       Congestion or obstruction at this intersection could hamper emergency response or evacuation routes for nearby
       residents, particularly during peak construction activities.
 TF_2: Towards Uch-terek via intersection with K1 Access Road
 Located further along the regional road towards Osh, this area serves as a feeder zone for construction traffic coming
 from multiple directions. This will be a convergence point for trucks, staff buses, and public vehicles.
 Road Safety Concerns:
        •     The surrounding industrial/construction activity and road configuration suggest frequent use by heavy-duty
              cargo vehicles. This raise concerns related to braking distances, turning radii, and accident severity in the
              event of collisions.
        •     Multiple intersecting tracks and access roads may create confusion and increase the risk of side-impact or
              rear-end collisions, especially without clear signage or right-of-way control.
        •     The adjacent terrain includes steep slopes with no visible roadside protection (e.g., guardrails or
              embankments). This increases the likelihood of run-off-road incidents, particularly during night-time or
              adverse weather.
        •     The agricultural setting implies likely presence of pedestrians, cyclists, or animal crossings. In the absence of
              footpaths, signage, or designated crossings, the risk of vehicle-pedestrian conflicts is elevated.
        •     Road curvature and elevation variations may limit visibility for drivers, affecting safe overtaking and reaction
              time to obstacles or slow-moving vehicles.
 TF3 – Kambarata Access Road




     Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
     Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
     Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                      Page 71
Description of Impacts



TF3, serving as a key access road to the Kambarata HPP-1 and HPP-2 sites, presents several road safety concerns due
to its anticipated heavy construction traffic and existing infrastructure limitations.
Road Safety Concerns:
     1.    High Share of Heavy Construction Traffic
           TF3 will experience a disproportionately high volume of heavy-duty vehicles such as dumpers, cement mixers,
           and equipment carriers during the construction phase of Kambarata HPPs. This increases the risk of road
           degradation, vehicle rollovers, and collision severity.
     2.    Potential Bottleneck or Conflict Zone
           As an access point, TF3 may face congestion from construction-related traffic converging with local or
           regional traffic, especially during peak material movement periods. This raises the risk of delays and accidents
           due to insufficient road width or turn radius.
     3.    Inadequate Road Geometry and Surface Conditions
           If the access road is not designed or upgraded for heavy loads, poor pavement condition, narrow lanes, or
           sharp turns could compromise safety, particularly under adverse weather conditions common in
           mountainous or riverine areas.
     4.    Lack of Safety Features
           The absence of clear signage, lighting, lane markings, and guardrails poses a serious hazard—especially for
           night-time driving or poor visibility scenarios. This is critical for a road supporting heavy construction logistics.
     5.    Interaction with Local Traffic and Vulnerable Road Users
           TF3's proximity to settlements and agricultural areas indicates possible interactions with pedestrians, cyclists,
           and livestock. Without dedicated provisions, this mixed use increases accident risks.
Key Environmental and Social Impacts:
In addition to that, the construction activities are expected to generate a range of environmental and social impacts.
The expected Environmental and Social Impacts due to the construction of Kambarata HPP 1 is given below-
A. Air Quality Degradation
           •     Emissions from long-haul diesel trucks transporting fly ash and cement are expected to elevate
                 concentrations of PM₂.₅, PM₁₀, NOx, and CO₂, especially near densely populated areas such as Kara-Jygach
                 and Uch-Terek.
           •     Dust re-suspension from trucks moving over partially sealed or unpaved roads may further deteriorate
                 local air quality and reduce visibility.
B. Noise Pollution
           •     Continuous passage of heavy-duty trucks will contribute to elevated ambient noise levels, primarily due to
                 engine braking, gear shifts, and honking, impacting community health—particularly children, elderly, and
                 patients with pre-existing health conditions.
C. Road Infrastructure Degradation
           •     Repeated movement of 25-ton trucks over under-designed rural roads (Access Road and Haul Roads) will
                 cause pothole formation, and edge cracking, increasing both accident risks and maintenance costs.
           •     Clogging of road drainage due to sediment load and debris from construction transport may cause seasonal
                 waterlogging and soil erosion, affecting roadside settlements.
           •     Landslide risks: Road segments running through steep, mountainous terrain, particularly during spring
                 thaw or heavy rains are prone to landslides which would lead to-
                       o     Sudden road blockages can trap vehicles or disrupt evacuation and emergency response.
                       o     Rockfall and slope failure may directly endanger road users, especially near cut slopes without
                             protective netting or retaining structures.
Impacts from Other Transport (Workforce, Services, and Equipment Handling)

      Primary Traffic Sources:

A. Traffic Congestion and Access Disruption
           •     The daily movement of nearly 300 vehicles for workforce transport, particularly around shift-change hours,
                 is likely to cause traffic bottlenecks at TF_1 and TF_2.
           •     Public services such as school buses, ambulances, and local markets may experience delays, hampering
                 community mobility and emergency response.
B. Safety Hazards from Mixed Traffic
     •     Interaction of construction workforce vehicles with local non-motorized traffic (pedestrians, livestock,
           cyclists) creates a high collision risk, particularly at TF_2 and TF_3 where informal road usage is prevalent.
     •     Inadequate signage and absence of dedicated access lanes for construction personnel can further increase
           the frequency of road-related incidents.
C. Community Well-being and Perception of Risk
     •     Local communities may perceive the workforce movement as intrusive, leading to:
                 o     Social stress and mistrust, particularly if road accidents occur.
                 o     Perceived neglect of local safety measures or lack of compensation for increased risks.
     •     Potential SEA/SH risks resulting from drivers of vehicles carrying construction materials, seeking temporary
           residence in the local residential areas, may impact the well-being of local community
D. Oversized Equipment Transport Risks
     •     The movement of large mechanical parts (e.g., turbines, cranes, batching plants) may:
                 o     Require temporary road closures or diversions, disrupting local access.
                 o     Pose hazards during turns, slopes, or bridges, particularly if escort vehicles or safety marshals are
                       inadequate.
                 o     Cause damage to the existing infrastructure, including bridges and culverts, due to overloading or
                       improper manoeuvring.
Operation & Maintenance Phase
As the Kambarata-1 HPP transitions into the operational phase, the intensity of construction activities declines, but
traffic movements associated with maintenance, logistics, and staff mobility continue. These activities, if not carefully
managed, can lead to persistent safety risks and health-related concerns for both local communities and the
operational workforce.
      1. Road Safety Risks
      • Continued movement of maintenance vehicles—including fuel trucks, inspection vans, and transporters
      carrying large components—poses an ongoing safety concern, especially on narrow or previously degraded roads.
      • Residual damage from construction traffic (e.g., worn-out road surfaces, broken shoulders, faded markings)
      can become hazardous if not repaired and maintained regularly. Such conditions increase the risk of:
      •    Vehicle skidding or loss of control, especially in hilly or curved segments.
      •    Accidents at unregulated junctions or informal crossings near villages.

      • Lack of signage or inadequate lighting, particularly on access routes used during early mornings or evenings,
      may increase collision risks.
      • Speeding by staff or contractors, especially if not monitored, may persist as a behavioural legacy of the
      construction phase.
      • Emergency vehicle delays may arise if roads remain narrow or obstructed by unplanned parking or poor
      maintenance.
     2. Community Health Implications
      • Dust and emissions from ongoing traffic, especially on unpaved or poorly maintained roads, can lead to
      respiratory issues such as asthma or bronchitis in nearby populations, particularly among children and the elderly.
      • Noise pollution from routine vehicular movement, especially during equipment servicing or large
      maintenance deliveries, may disturb residents living near the powerhouse or access roads.

Landscape and Visual
This section examines the visual and landscape impacts of the K-1 HPP and its associated facilities. It defines visual
impacts as changes observable in the scenic quality and aesthetic character of the environment, while landscape impacts
relate to alterations in the natural configuration and spatial continuity of the terrain.
The analysis shows that, due to the project's location in a rugged, sparsely populated mountainous area, the overall
visual character remains largely intact, with only subtle changes associated with the reservoir formation—changes that
could even yield positive tourism opportunities similar to those seen at Issykul and Toktogul lakes.

10.4.1.38 Definition and Context
In this chapter, “landscape impacts” refer to alterations in the physical environment, spatial configuration, and aesthetic
quality of the terrain resulting from project interventions. “Visual impacts” focus on the perceptible changes in the
appearance of the area when viewed from defined observation points (such as residential areas or public viewpoints).
For clarity, the perspective adopted here is that of local communities, stakeholders, and decision-makers, differentiating
these impacts from functional access changes (for example, those resulting from new or altered reservoir access routes),
which are covered separately in the social impacts section Section 10.5 (Social Impacts).

10.4.1.39 Scope of Landscape and Visual Impacts
The World Bank’s Environmental and Social (E&S) standards require projects to clearly identify and mitigate impacts
that alter the aesthetic and visual qualities of the landscape. These standards expect that adverse changes—such as
disruptions to scenic views, alteration of natural landforms, and fragmentation of visual continuity—are adequately
addressed.
Measures must be implemented to preserve, enhance, or restore the natural and constructed elements that define the
local landscape, ensuring a balance between development needs and environmental stewardship.
Localised impacts to land use and land cover due to construction and operation activities are assessed in
Section 10.4.12. Impacts, from changes in visual and landscape aspects, on local livelihoods and tourism, such as
changes that could influence community-based economic activities or visitor experiences, are covered in the social
impacts section in Section 10.5 (Social Impacts). Similarly, effects on cultural and heritage assets are being addressed
separately in Section 10.5.18 (Cultural Heritage) and will be mitigated through the Cultural Heritage Management Plan
provided as part of the ESMPs. Actions related to the rehabilitation of construction-disturbed lands—such as quarries—
are incorporated under various subplans, including the Borrow Pit Management Plans and Spoils and Waste
Management Plans, as part of the ESMPs.
This section therefore focuses on the remaining aspects of landscape and visual impacts. These include changes in the
scenic quality of the terrain, disruptions to the natural topographical aesthetics, and alterations in the visual continuity
of the environment resulting from the project infrastructure. The assessment will cover impacts such as altered vistas
and panoramic views, modifications to vegetation patterns and landform aesthetics, and changes in the overall visual
integration of project components within the surrounding natural landscape.

10.4.1.40 Current Landscape and Visual Characteristics
Exhibits 10-1 to 10-3 below provide photographs of the left and right banks of the Naryn River, taken from both the
ground and onboard a helicopter. The photographs have been taken from locations ranging from about 25 km upstream
of the proposed dam site to about 3 km downstream. The images demonstrate that both the construction area and the
area to be submerged by the future reservoir are predominantly mountainous terrains with little to no population. The
current visual character features rugged topography and scenic vistas consistent with natural mountainous landscapes,
forming the baseline against which project-induced changes will be assessed.
The terrain also comprises areas used for pasture. Impacts to these pasture areas resulting from inundation are being
covered in detail in Section 10.5 (Social Impacts).
In addition to pasture lands, there exist some structures in the inundation zone. Section 10.5 (Social Impacts) provides
a more in-depth review of this aspect.

10.4.1.41 Potential Impacts on Visual and Landscape Quality

10.4.1.41.1 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase (Including Site Clearance)
During the pre-construction phase, activities such as site clearance may introduce localized disturbances; however,
these interventions are limited to areas with minimal human activity. The overall visual character of the rugged,
mountainous terrain remains largely unaltered, preserving the natural configuration and scenic vistas that form the
baseline for future assessments.

10.4.1.41.2 Phase 2 – Construction Phase Including Initial Reservoir Filling
As the project moves into the construction phase and the reservoir begins to fill, visual alterations may occur. These
include potential shifts in the visual continuity of the river valley and subtle changes in the landscape appearance due
to temporary infrastructure and earthworks. However, the core scenic features—such as the natural landforms and
vegetation patterns—continue to be preserved, ensuring limited deviation from the established baseline.

10.4.1.41.3 Phase 3 – Operation Phase Including Maintenance
In the operation phase, routine maintenance activities and adaptive management measures are implemented to further
integrate the project’s components with the natural landscape. Continuous monitoring and effective mitigation,
including landscaping and revegetation, support the preservation of visual aesthetics. The operational stage ensures
that any adjustments made over time do not compromise the inherent visual integrity of the region.

10.4.1.42              Significant Impacts
In terms of changes in scenic quality of the terrain, disruptions to the natural topographical aesthetics, and alterations
in the visual continuity of the environment resulting from the project infrastructure, there are no significant impacts.
This is due to the project's location in a rugged, sparsely populated mountainous area. The overall visual character
remains largely intact, with only subtle changes associated with the reservoir formation—changes that could even yield
positive tourism opportunities similar to those seen at Issykul and Toktogul lakes.

Climate Change and GHG Emissions
10.4.1.43 Climate Change
Hydropower is driven by streamflow which is heavily influenced by climatic conditions of the surrounding area (Ochieng
et al., 2022). The implications induced by climate change on hydropower generation have been documented in several
studies (Siegfried et al., 2023; Wasti et al., 2021) due to change in frequency and intensity of precipitation pattern,
temperature, humidity, and evaporation inducing widespread melting of glacier and snow, increased streamflow, floods
and droughts, with other subsequent impacts on irrigation, agriculture and pasture lands in high mountainous regions.
Such changes affect the infrastructure, design capacity, flow patterns and other operational activities of the hydropower
project.
Long-term sustainability efforts development is essential through mitigation and adaptation strategies and approaches
to address the impacts of climate change. This section includes the climate change impacts on the K-1 HPP based on the
climate model projection outputs assessed in the feasibility study10 followed by mitigation measures during pre-
construction, construction and operation phase.

10.4.1.43.1 Climate model projections
Climate change projections based on 22 GCMs from CMIP6 in terms of precipitation and air temperature have been
carried out for two scenarios, SSP2-4.5 (moderate) and SSP5-8.5 (worst case) for the period 2021 to 2100 for upstream
and downstream of the K-1 HPP. Snowmelt and shift in snow process, Glacial Lake Outburst Flood (GLOF) and Landslide
dam outburst floods (LDOF), and potential evapotranspiration have also been assessed in the feasibility study.
Below are the results obtained in the feasibility study, 2024-25 of climate change and related hydrological assessment:

10.4.1.43.2 Climate change impacts
Climate change impacts on the K-1 HPP during pre-construction, construction and operation phase are addressed in this
section. Significant challenges may be observed in terms of technical and logistical planning induced by climate change
on the K-1 HPP.
                          Climate change impacts during pre-construction phase
No such impacts are envisaged during this phase as the pre-construction will be completed within 2 years. Although few
of them may cause implications in terms of geotechnical impacts due to minor landslips triggered by increase in winter
precipitation while clearing site.
                          Climate change impacts during construction and initial reservoir filling phase
During construction phase, several challenges may affect the schedule, cost, safety and other environmental impacts as
construction period is moderately long that would cover for 7-8 years. Below are the anticipated impacts during the
construction phase:
•        Increase in winter precipitation may affect the runoff that feed into the reservoir causing delays and disruptions
         in construction activities and creating an unsafe working condition upon the recommencement of construction
         activities in the forthcoming season.


    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                         Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                           SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                        11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



•        Increase in winter precipitation and shift in snow processes may affect the water quality due to increased runoff
         and sediment build up in water of Naryn river required for construction activities for concrete mixing and other
         processes.
•        Increase in winter precipitation may trigger minor landslips near the construction site that could lead to
         construction disruptions, foundation destabilization, increased costs for material delivery and loss of key
         construction materials.
•        Increase in temperature may cause ground instability due to permafrost thawing that could affect dam’s
         foundation and safety related to construction activities.
•        Filling of reservoir may face timing issues while filling due to shift in snow processes such as less snowfall and
         earlier melting. Reduction in water availability due to less snow accumulation during planned filling may also be
         expected. Permafrost thawing due to increase in temperature that could lead to ground instability may cause
         sediment or debris influx into the reservoir while filling.
                          Climate change impacts during operation and maintenance phase
Although the projected climate change is reported not to have strong impacts on the K-1 HPP as per the feasibility study,
considering the projected erratic nature of climatic factors which is not in line with the hydrological model results in the
feasibility study, the operation phase may be influenced by climate change impacts which could face long-term
consequences for sustainability and efficiency of the K-1 HPP operation.
Below are the anticipated impacts during operation phase:
•        Projected decrease in summer precipitation and shorter duration of snow cover period and earlier melting induced
         by rise in temperature may reduce the amount of water that flows into the reservoir which may result in lesser
         storage volume affecting the desired power generation and water supply for irrigation up to some extent although
         this would be compensated by increase in winter and spring precipitation.
•        Projected shifts in seasonal snow processes and decrease in glacier cover with increase in temperature may affect
         water availability in the reservoir. Additionally, projected decrease in high flow may reduce river flows that would
         subsequently affect peak reservoir volume and sustained power generation.

10.4.1.44 Impact of GHG on Air Quality
The Pre-construction, Construction and Operation & Maintenance (O&M) activities of Dam and its reservoir, besides
construction of Dam’s ancillary structures like spillway and middle outlet will result in significant greenhouse gases
(GHGs) emissions in the form of carbon-dioxide (CO2) and methane (CH4). The GHG emissions from its current pre-
impoundment phase will increase significantly due to consumption of construction raw materials during construction
of dam and its ancillary structures and particularly and from the filled reservoir, mainly due to decomposition of
vegetation and faunal species in the reservoir’s submerged areas, decomposition of new riparian flora and fauna, and
other anthropogenic emission sources in the reservoir areas which is envisaged to continue during initial 10-20 years
post commencement of the operations phase. Subsequently the GHG emissions will start declining and is anticipated to
continue declining steadily throughout the rest of the operational years till completion of its operational phase.
The sources of emissions during construction have been segregated under three emissions categories11:

       •      Scope 1 Emissions: These are direct greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from sources which the Project Proponent
              owns or controls
       •      Scope 2 Emissions: These are emissions which are indirect greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions that occur when
              the Project Proponent buys and uses energy for consumption. e.g., from Power Grids.
       •      Scope 3 Emissions: These are indirect greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions that occur throughout a Project
              Proponent’s Supply chain like emissions from Raw Materials Suppliers’ facilities etc.
For this current assessment, only Scope 1 emissions have been considered under Gross GHG Emissions for Construction
Phase. The Scope 1 emissions consider emissions from consumption of concrete used for construction of dam and its


11 The pre-construction phase mainly involves removal of vegetation, site clearance, grading and levelling. Hence no GHG emissions except from

vehicular and equipment movement is envisaged in the pre-construction phase. This GHG emissions is anticipated to be insignificant as compared to
GHG emissions from construction and operation phase; hence pre-construction phase GHG emissions are not included in this impact assessment.

the AFRY’s FS Update June 2025.

Estimation of Scope 1 GHG Emissions during Construction Phase

The potential sources of GHG emissions during construction phase are as follows:

     •     Use of Raw Materials for construction of 260.8m high dam i.e., concrete has been considered in the GHG
           estimation under Scope 1.
     •     In the absence of data regarding the Nos. of construction vehicles and equipment owned or controlled by
           Project proponent or their fuel consumption, emissions from fuel consumption due to movement of these
           vehicles and construction equipment could not be ascertained and excluded from the current scope 1
           estimation. However, considering the scale of emissions from these vehicles and emissions when compared
           with emissions from raw materials, the same is assessed to be considerably lower.
Based on the following considerations and assumption & exclusions, the total Scope 1 Emissions is estimated to be
~3,126,200 ton CO2e during the total construction period of 102 months.
     •     Assuming concrete used in RCC dam construction is prepared using cement with 30% fly ash replacement and
           aggregate. (in the absence of data on design mix)
     •     The emission factor of concrete mix is considered 290 kg CO2/m3 (i.e., 0.124 kg CO2e/kg) based on data from
           Singapore Emission Factors Registry (SEFR) database. The primary data source is Singapore Green Building
           Council (Climatiq, n.d.).
     •     Due to non-availability of information of the diesel consumption for production of concrete in Batching Plant
           at this stage, vehicular movement, construction equipment operations, GHG emissions due to diesel
           consumption in concrete mixing process in Batching plant are excluded.
     •     Batching plant would be located within 1km of the construction zone; hence the emissions due to vehicular
           movement from batching plant to construction zone is assumed to be negligible as compared to other scope 1
           emissions and excluded. Additionally, from the FS Report prepared in 2025, it is understood that concrete mix
           from batching plant to dam construction site would be sourced through conveyor belts which will be based on
           power consumption from the grid which is currently not available; hence excluded from Scope 2 emissions.

Estimation of Reservoir GHG Emissions during Operational Life Cycle of Dam and Reservoir

The potential sources of GHG emissions during the operation stage are mainly from the decomposition of vegetation
and faunal species in the reservoir’s submerged areas, decomposition of new riparian flora and fauna, and other
anthropogenic emission sources in the reservoir areas.

GHG Emissions Estimation:




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                                      Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                                        SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                                     11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



The estimation of GHG emissions has been carried out using International Hydropower Association’s (IHA) “The GHG
Reservoir Tool (G-Res Tool)” for freshwater reservoirs12, which was developed based on the recommendation from the
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) and is a tool to calculate the net carbon footprint of freshwater
reservoirs. The dam reservoirs are a source of greenhouse gases (GHG) (carbon dioxide (CO2), methane (CH4), and
nitrous oxide (N2O). The calculation of net emissions in G-res includes four parts as mentioned in the following equation
below:

Net GHG emissions = [Post‐impoundment GHG balance from the catchment after the introduction of a reservoir] –
[Pre‐impoundment GHG balance of the catchment before the introduction of a reservoir] – [Emissions from the
reservoir due to Unrelated anthropogenic sources] + [GHG due to construction]

Where the emissions from the existing landscape before introducing the reservoir through construction of dam is
referred to as pre-impoundment. The reservoir once formed post construction of the dam resulting in the inundation
of a considerable portion of existing landscape adjoining the Naryn River is considered as post-impoundment. In G-Res
Tool, the reservoir area is generated based on 30m spatial resolution SRTM digital elevation data via an earth engine
after inputting the exact coordinates of the dam and the normal operating level above sea level. Then the land types
within the reservoir area are estimated using 300 m spatial resolution ESACCI land cover data. The last procedure is
multiplying the surface area of each land type with the corresponding emission factor for both CO 2 and CH4.

Unrelated anthropogenic sources (UAS) indicate the carbon emissions in the reservoir area due to sewage discharge
containing phosphorus (nutrient) and from other anthropogenic sources. Here the input is the catchment area, which
is the scope of land where precipitation collects and drains off into a common outlet. For the current GHG estimation,
the upstream watershed region controlled by the dam has been considered as the catchment area. Since no major
industrial activities are envisaged in the upstream watershed region of the dam, mainly community and minor industrial
activities (e.g., stone quarrying) which occur in the catchment area make contributions to nutrients and carbon flowing
into the reservoir. Land types within the catchment area are needed to estimate the natural background load and high
or low land-use intensity is set manually to represent anthropogenic activities. The construction term refers to the
emissions related to the production of materials, transport and plant stages to build the dam and other associated
infrastructure, which are estimated from the consumption of material and power, as well as corresponding emission
factors (Karbin et al, 2021).

For the current GHG estimation, various input data were collected from the Technical Reports of AFRY, Secondary
literature sources and estimated using GIS map and Google Earth Engines as follows:

     •     Landcover details: Pre-impoundment Land Cover in the Reservoir Area, and Landcover in the Catchment Area
           post impoundment was estimated from Google Earth engine which included landcover categories like bare
           area, cropland, forest, grassland/shrubland, permanent snow/ice area, settlement and waterbodies.
     •     Reservoir technical details like catchment area in sq.km, reservoir area in sq.km and reservoir volume in km3,
           maximum depth (in km), construction materials for dams (e.g. concrete, steel etc.), Hydropower Plant’s
           Installed Capacity in MW, and Total Annual Generation in GWh/year was considered from the technical design
           documents.
     •     Data like mean depth (in km) along the reservoir, thermocline depth (m), No. of population in the catchment
           area, sewage generation and the nutrient load (phosphorus) entering into the reservoir, Soil Carbon Content
           under Impounded Area, Phosphorus concentration in water (microgram/liter), water residence time and
           Reservoir Mean Global Horizontal Radiance (KWh/m3/day) were assumed based on review of secondary
           literatures for similar project in the same climatic region.
     •     Data like runoff, wind speed was considered through review of secondary data of the region and then based
           on landcover in the catchment area (% wise), the average run-off contributing to the reservoir was estimated.




12 Source: The G-res tool v2.1 Technical documentation, 2019

     Based on the above input data, G-Res GHG Estimation Tool of IHA was used to calculate the Reservoir GHG
     Emissions.

     Findings of GHG Emissions Estimation

     The projected Pre-impoundment GHG Balance before dam construction and Post-impoundment GHG balance of
     the reservoir has been discussed below:

     Pre-impoundment GHG Balance before dam construction

           Prior to the impoundment of the landscape by the reservoir and their subsequent inundation due to
           construction of the dam, various ecological sub-units exist in different landcovers within the projected
           inundated areas have their GHG balance. The GHG emissions by ecological sub-units in each of the landcovers
           are driven by the land-use intensity. For example, while a piece of the forest generally absorbs CO 2 from the
           atmosphere through photosynthesis, wetlands not only deposit that carbon as peat but also typically emit CH4.
           Similarly, natural streams, rivers, and lakes tend to emit both CO 2 and CH4. In the projected reservoir area of
           61.7 sq.km, prior to impoundment, bare (open) areas make a substantial share in the land cover (56.7%)
           followed by Grassland/Shrubland (28%) before the introduction of the dam. Average Soil Carbon Content in
           the impounded area is considered as 20KgC/m2.

           Annual CO2 and CH4 in pre-impoundment were 6548 tCO2e/year (at an emission rate of 113 gCO2e/m2 /yr)
           and 377 tCO2e/year (at an emission rate of 6 gCO2e/m2 /yr) respectively for total pre-impoundment GHG
           emissions of 6925 tCO2e/year. One of the probable reasons for very negligible CH4 emissions in the pre-
           impoundment stage is presence of high oxygen level in the water leading to oxic condition in the bottom level
           thus limiting methanogenesis by the bacterial population present.

     Post-impoundment GHG Balance after dam construction
         The dam height would be about 260.8 m high from the FGL. The water level from the current 955m above MSL
         would go up to 1198m above MSL post impoundment, which indicates that the maximum depth of the water
         would be approximately 243m and average depth along the gradient of the reservoir was assumed to be
         approx. 90m. The total catchment area contributing run-off to the Naryn River upstream of the Dam including
         K1 Reservoir is computed to be ~46, 171sq.km based on study of the contour data and projecting the same on
         GIS platform. Post impoundment with dam and introduction of the reservoir, the area contributing to the
         reservoir would be represented majorly by grassland/shrubland (approx. 76%) of the total landcover followed
         by bare (open) areas (17.3%). This landcover is projected based on the GIS mapping and taking into
         consideration the rise in the water level due to impoundment.

           There are three (03) pathways for GHG emissions in water reservoir - molecular diffusive flux, ebullition and
           degassing. As per the G-res tool, CH4 diffusive flux mainly depends on temperature and spatial parameters of
           the reservoir while CO2 diffusive flux is also controlled by surface soil carbon content (Jiang et al., 2018). The
           second gas transportation pathway is generated when an insoluble gas (e.g., CH4) produced in sediment cannot
           be dissolved in water, therefore gets emitted in the form of bubbles into the atmosphere by ebullition (Chanton
           et al., 1989). Furthermore, when water undergoes rapid depressurization or aeration, dissolved gases are likely
           to be emitted to the atmosphere. After the water passes through the turbines, GHGs are either emitted into
           the atmosphere or absorbed by microbes (e.g., CH 4 oxidation by methanotrophic bacteria). Large degassing
           emissions are expected when GHG content in spilled water is high.

           Annual CO2 and CH4 in post-impoundment are estimated to be 14,832 tCO2e/year (at an emission rate of 242
           gCO2e/m2 /yr) and 2535 tCO2e/year (at an emission rate of 41 gCO2e/m2 /yr) respectively for total post-
           impoundment GHG emissions of 17,367 tCO2e/year. In this case, CH4 emissions was found to increase during
           the post-impoundment phase. This can be attributed to submergence and decomposition of vegetation, and


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                  SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                               11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



            riparian flora and fauna in the reservoir, and formation of new riparian zone; and subsequently higher
            methanogenesis of the submerged biomass in the bottom level of the reservoir. Of the total CH4 emissions,
            91% are estimated to be diffusive flux (38 gCO2e/m2 /yr) and 9% are estimated to be bubbling emissions (4
            gCO2e/m2 /yr).

     Emissions from the Reservoir due to Unrelated Anthropogenic Sources (UAS)
         Unrelated Anthropogenic Sources are sources of GHG emissions (CH4 only) which are contributed by
         anthropogenic activities like sewage discharged by human beings and other animal excreta and carcasses
         brought in along with the incoming water entering the reservoir. These sources are indirect in nature
         contributing to the GHG emissions which in case of reservoir in the Naryn River is anticipated to be contributed
         by the sewage generated from the local human settlements of approx. 84000 people within the catchment
         area of ~46, 171 sq.km. These sewage from all anthropogenic activities will finally flow into the reservoirs
         through branches, which accounts for part of the total emission of post-impoundment.

            Annual CH4 emissions from UAS in post-impoundment are estimated to be 190 tCO2e/year (at an emission
            rate of 3 gCO2e/m2 /yr). This is estimated under water residence time is approx. 0.5 years in Naryn the K-1
            HPP reservoir. In case water residence increases in the K-1 HPP reservoir, GHG fluxes may escalate. Any
            additional flow of community and industrial sewage in the catchment in future has the potential to increase
            nutrient concentration and escalate CH4 emissions.

     Net Annual GHG Emissions from the K-1 HPP Reservoir in Naryn
         GHG emissions before the dam introduction into the catchment is estimated to be much lower (6925
         tCO2e/year) compared to the GHG emissions after the reservoir is constructed post introduction of the dam,
         due to which GHG is anticipated to increase to 17,367 tCO2e/year which is about 2.5 times the pre-
         impoundment emissions. Furthermore, GHG emissions, due to anthropogenic activities (UAS) in the catchment
         area are estimated to be 190 tCO2e/year. Therefore, following the equation as mentioned above, Net GHG
         Emissions are 10,252 tCO2e/yr, out of which CO2 emissions are 8284 tCO2e/yr and CH4 emissions are1968
         tCO2e/yr. The G-Res Calculation Sheets are provided in Appendix B of this report.

     Total Lifecycle GHG Emissions from the K-1 HPP Reservoir in Naryn
         The total lifecycle GHG Emissions from K1 HPP Reservoir in Naryn for an operational life cycle of 100 years is
         estimated to be 1,025,205 Ton CO2e.



    Summary of GHG footprint
Finally based on results obtained above for both Construction Phase and 100 Years Operational Lifecycle 13 of the K-Dam
and its Reservoir, the GHG emissions are summarized below. :

From the above assessment, it is understood that Gross GHG emissions (2,465,000 ton CO2e) during construction phase
would be occurring over a period of ~72 months involving consumption of raw materials for construction of Dam and

From the above assessment it is estimated that total annual Reservoir wide GHG Emissions during the operation phase
would be in the tune of 10,252 tCO2e/yr which is apparently a moderate to low emissions to the atmosphere. However,
in 2023, Kyrgyzstan's total greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions amounted to 17.4 million metric tonnes of carbon dioxide
equivalent (MtCO2e). Therefore, the total annual emissions from the Reservoir of the K-1 HPP Dam are estimated to
contribute <0.06% only of the total GHG emissions of Kyrgyzstan. Moreover, the K-1 HPP being a renewable project
itself emits significantly lower GHGs compared to other Power Plants of similar capacity based on fossil fuel e.g., thermal
power plant. Also, the Total Lifetime GHG Emissions of the K-1 HPP Dam and Reservoir is estimated to be 1,025,205CO2e
over the entire operational lifecycle of 100 years.

The analysis of Net GHG emissions in the K-1 HPP Reservoir shows that the CO2 diffusive flux reduces drastically with
growing reservoir ages, whereas CH4 diffusive flux reduces steadily with the reservoir ages, thereby reducing the overall
GHG emissions as the reservoir ages. Figure 6 and Figure 7 show reduction of GHG emissions with reservoir age.

Estimation of Loss in Net Primary Production of Biomass due to Reservoir Impoundment

As per Energy Profile of Kyrgyzstan published by International Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA) in 2021, the Net
primary production (NPP) of Biomass in Kyrgyzstan is approx. 1.5-ton C/ha/yr. NPP is the amount of carbon fixed by
plants and accumulated as biomass each year. It is a basic measure of biomass productivity. The chart below shows the
average NPP in the Kyrgyzstan(tC/ha/yr)15.




Based on landcover estimated to be submerged post impoundment of the reservoir, it is estimated that the total forest
area and grassland/shrubland area to be submerged would be 18.5 sq.km which amount to approx. 1850 hectares.
The average NPP of Kyrgyzstan is estimated to be 1.5 ton-C/ha/yr. Therefore for 1850 hectares of submerged vegetated
land, an anuual loss of NPP of biomass amounting to approx. 2775 ton-C/yr is estimated i.e., 27,75,000 Kg-C/yr.

Microclimate
Hydropower projects are built for purposes like water supply, power generation, and flood control. While they
contribute to economic development, they can negatively impact the local climate (Fonseca & Santos, 2021; Liu R et al.,
2025), altering temperature, precipitation, humidity, wind speed, and direction. These changes can affect the local
environment, ecology, and resource capacity (Yu et al., 2021). Microclimate assessments usually rely on pre- and post-
operation climate data. Construction-induced land use changes affect the water cycle and radiation, influencing
atmospheric interactions. However, the feasibility study's climate models 16 didn’t include the K-1 HPP, complicating the
assessment of its impact on the local climate. Therefore, this section provides a qualitative evaluation of the anticipated
microclimate impacts, mitigation measures, and recommendations, addressing phase-wise impacts during pre-
construction, construction, and operation for clarity.

Considering the limitations stated above, a baseline of daily air temperature and precipitation below table 1 collected
from the Toktugul observatory is presented here. This would help us in assisting with a preliminary estimation of the
climate parameters of the K-1 HPP. However, note that the distance between Toktugul observatory and the K-1 HPP
dam is 39 km. The average monthly daily air temperature and precipitation are computed for the period 2014 to 2023
to observe the variations.
