10.4.1.47              Significant Impacts

Significance scoring of impacts to microclimate VECs associated with different stages of the construction and operation
of the K-1 HPP has been conducted. The results of this scoring are detailed in Table 10-14 to Table 10-16 in Appendix
10-A.

Based on the scoring, a highly significant impact is found during the operational phase only. Based on the significance
and likelihood of this impact, the risk of micro-climatic impacts during the Operations phase would be further studied
in chapter 12. Nevertheless, no specific control measures are readily available on microclimate VECs. It may be expected
that the community who are the major receptors would gradually become accustomed to these long-term changes in
microclimate VECs. Having said this, the company would continue to engage with the community periodically to collect
feedback and suggestions for consideration to minimize the impact.

Natural Hazards
This section assesses the potential impacts of natural hazard processes on the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant Project
(the Kambarata-1 HPP), including flooding, slope instability, and seismic activity. These hazards may affect construction
activities, operational infrastructure, and the surrounding physical environment across all project phases.
Unlike other components of the biophysical impact assessment that focus on impacts to specific Valued Ecosystem
Components (VECs), this section treats natural hazard processes (e.g. floods, seismic events, landslides) as physical
drivers of environmental and infrastructure-related impacts. These are not assessed as standalone VECs but are instead
evaluated in terms of their potential to affect dam safety, slope stability, construction viability, and overall project
resilience. This approach is consistent with the World Bank Environmental and Social Standard ESS4, which emphasises
the protection of infrastructure and communities from disaster risk.
The assessment draws on baseline conditions described in Chapter 8 (Natural Hazards) and evaluates hazard-related
impacts across the pre-construction, construction, and operational phases of the project. The key natural hazards
affecting the project area include:
     •     Flooding. Seasonal floods and extreme inflow events may cause inundation of temporary or permanent
           infrastructure, erosion of access roads and riverbanks, and disruption to construction or operational activities.
           The Probable Maximum Flood (PMF) has been estimated by AFRY at 7,100 m³/s under historical conditions and
           9,200 m³/s under a +4°C climate scenario. These values are critical for the sizing of spillways and mid-outlets.
           During construction, high flows may overtop cofferdams or exceed diversion tunnel capacity. During
           operations, residual risks may arise from sedimentation, compound snowmelt-rainfall events, or reduced
           spillway efficiency due to progressive sediment build-up.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                                Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                                  SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                               11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



     •     Slope instability. Earthworks for roads, dam abutments, and tunnel portals may disturb steep terrain and
           trigger shallow landslides or debris flows, particularly in weathered or unconsolidated materials. During initial
           reservoir filling or rapid drawdown, pore water pressure changes may destabilise slopes along the reservoir
           rim. Localised slope failure may damage infrastructure, obstruct haul roads, or affect reservoir safety margins.
           Areas of greatest concern include the steep left bank and any reservoir-side zones with exposed colluvium.
     •     Seismic hazards. The project site is located in a seismically active region. AFRY’s design parameters include a
           Safety Evaluation Earthquake (SEE) with PGA 0.62 g and a Maximum Credible Earthquake (MCE) at 1.17 g. While
           the dam is designed to withstand these loads, seismic events during construction could damage partially
           completed structures, destabilise unsupported slopes or excavations, and pose safety risks to workers. During
           operation, strong ground motion may trigger structural deformation, slope failures into the reservoir, or
           disruption of access roads, power tunnels, and other infrastructure.
These hazard-related impacts are considered across all project phases. Significance ratings are summarised in Table 10–
9, and specific mitigation measures are detailed in Chapter 11 Mitigation and Management.
Residual risks of catastrophic dam failure are not treated here as a primary hazard category, since such failure would be
a consequence of other natural hazards (e.g. earthquakes, floods, or slope instability). These scenarios—including
potential cascade effects from upstream HPPs such as At‑Bashy or Kulanak—are addressed in AFRY’s 2025 Dam Break
Analysis and incorporated into the Emergency Preparedness and Response Plan (F‑EPP, 2025). Due to the long travel
distance (150–160 km) and the large attenuation capacity of the Kambarata‑1 HPP reservoir, upstream dam break waves
are not expected to pose a significant hazard at the project site. The results of AFRY’s dam break modelling, covering
both Kambarata‑1 and upstream cascade failure scenarios, are integrated into Chapter 11 (Mitigation and Management)
and the F‑EPP to ensure alignment between structural design, operational preparedness, and emergency response.

10.4.1.48 Phase 1 – Pre-Construction Phase including site clearance
During the pre-construction phase, physical interventions will be limited to site mobilisation, vegetation clearance, and
construction of temporary infrastructure such as access roads, camps, and laydown areas. However, these works may
still be affected by natural hazards present in the Naryn Valley.
Flooding during this phase may result from spring snowmelt or convective rainfall events. Low-lying works areas located
on river terraces may be exposed to temporary inundation or waterlogging. These effects could lead to delays in site
mobilisation or damage to temporary roads and drainage systems. While formal flood design criteria are not typically
applied to temporary works, it is recommended that infrastructure be designed to accommodate at least a 1:10-year
rainfall event. Standard mitigation measures include elevated siting, local drainage design, and dry season scheduling
of works.
Slope instability may be triggered by vegetation clearance or minor earthworks, particularly on steep or unconsolidated
terrain. Cut-and-fill activities for roads or camps may result in shallow slips, debris flows, or surface erosion. The severity
of impacts depends on slope gradient, rainfall intensity, and soil cohesion. Erosion and sediment control measures will
be implemented at all disturbed locations.
Seismic activity, although unlikely to cause major impacts during this phase, could destabilise shallow excavations, slope
cuts, or unanchored temporary infrastructure (e.g. fuel storage or chemical containers). AFRY’s updated seismic hazard
assessment (2025) identifies peak ground accelerations up to 1.17 g under Maximum Credible Earthquake conditions,
which have been factored into the design basis. For temporary works, risks will be mitigated through appropriate siting,
anchoring of sensitive equipment, and slope stabilisation where required.
Residual risks from upstream dam failures (e.g. At‑Bashy, Kulanak) are considered negligible during this phase, due to
the long travel distance (~150–160 km) and attenuation within the valley system. These scenarios are addressed in
AFRY’s Dam Break Analysis (2025) and incorporated into the Emergency Preparedness and Response Plan (F‑EPP, 2025).

10.4.1.49 Phase 2 – Construction Phase including initial reservoir filling
During the construction phase of the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant Project (the Kambarata-1 HPP), activities will
expand in scale and complexity. Works will include in-river construction (such as cofferdam installation and diversion
tunnel excavation), dam foundation preparation, tunnelling, major slope cutting, and the installation of permanent
infrastructure. These activities will increase exposure to natural hazards, particularly flooding, slope instability, and
seismic ground motion, which may disrupt works or compromise site safety if not effectively managed.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                  Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                    SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                 11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



Flooding presents a significant hazard during this phase due to the combination of in-river works, seasonal snowmelt,
and convective summer storms. Construction of the diversion tunnel and cofferdam will temporarily reduce channel
conveyance. During periods of elevated flow, the river may overtop temporary structures or bypass partially completed
works, potentially resulting in:
     •     Inundation of dam foundation areas, laydown zones, and haul routes.
     •     Erosion of spoil heaps, access roads, or embankments.
     •     Damage to equipment, scaffolding, and temporary structures.
     •     Safety risks for personnel working in low-lying or confined areas.
The risk of flooding is particularly acute if high flows coincide with excavation works below riverbed level. Based on
available hydrological data, peak flows in the Naryn River typically occur in spring, with additional short-duration peaks
during intense summer rainfall events. To minimise potential impacts, diversion tunnels and cofferdams should be
designed to safely convey at least the 1:20 to 1:50-year flood. Real-time hydrological monitoring, construction-phase
flood contingency plans, and the avoidance of in-river activities during peak runoff periods are also recommended.
Slope instability is a key concern during earthworks and the initial stages of reservoir impoundment. Excavation of steep
slopes for access roads, tunnel portals, and dam abutments may result in localised instability, particularly in areas
underlain by weathered rock or unconsolidated material. Risks include:
     •     Shallow landslides or slumping along road cuttings and slope benches.
     •     Debris flows originating from poorly managed spoil disposal sites.
     •     Rockfall from undercut or unsupported slopes near construction fronts.
During reservoir filling, rapid saturation of slope toes and elevated pore water pressures may induce instability along
the reservoir margin, especially on the steep left bank. These risks may be exacerbated if filling occurs too quickly
without adequate drainage or monitoring. Mitigation measures include:
     •     Pre-construction geotechnical investigations and slope stability modelling.
     •     Implementation of engineered slope reinforcements (e.g. retaining walls, shotcrete, rock bolts).
     •     Staged reservoir impoundment to allow for progressive adjustment and geotechnical inspection.
     •     Installation of surface drainage and sediment control systems along disturbed slopes.
Seismic activity also presents a hazard during this phase, particularly for partially completed underground works and
large unsupported excavations. Strong ground motion from a moderate to large seismic event could:
     •     Destabilise exposed tunnel faces and open-cut slopes.
     •     Lead to collapse of temporary formwork, scaffolding, or crane systems.
     •     Dislodge unsecured equipment, fuel tanks, or other hazardous materials.
Although the probability of a major seismic event during the construction window is relatively low, the vulnerability of
temporary structures means the potential consequences could be severe. To manage these impacts, the following
measures should be implemented:
     •     Temporary support systems for tunnels and open cuts, designed for seismic loading.
     •     Anchoring or bracing of heavy equipment and fuel storage.
     •     Adherence to seismic construction standards for formwork and vertical infrastructure.
     •     Site-specific Emergency Response Plans to guide immediate post-seismic inspections and response.

10.4.1.50 Phase 3 – Operation Phase including maintenance
During the operational phase of the Kambarata‑1 Hydropower Plant Project (the Kambarata‑1 HPP), the dam and
associated infrastructure will be exposed to natural hazard processes over an extended period. The principal hazards
include seasonal and extreme floods (including the Probable Maximum Flood), strong ground motion from regional
seismicity, slope instability linked to reservoir drawdown, and secondary effects on the downstream Kambarata‑2 HPP



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                              Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                             11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



reservoir caused by regulated releases. Although the project is designed for long‑term resilience, residual hazards
remain that may affect dam safety, reservoir function, or downstream conditions.
Flooding is expected to be regulated under normal operating conditions through managed reservoir releases. However,
the facility must remain capable of routing extreme inflow events, including the Probable Maximum Flood (PMF). AFRY
(2025d) has estimated the PMF at 7,100 m³/s under historical conditions and 9,200 m³/s under a +4°C climate warming
scenario. In the event of extreme floods, impacts may include:
     •     Overtopping of spillways or mid-outlets if capacity is reduced by sedimentation.
     •     Hydraulic loading of dam structures and energy dissipation systems.
     •     Emergency releases with limited warning time, increasing downstream flood risk.
Residual flood risk may increase over time due to sediment accumulation in spillway approach channels, particularly
during compound events (e.g. snowmelt combined with rainfall). Mitigation measures during the operational phase
include:
     •     Regular bathymetric surveys and reservoir sedimentation modelling.
     •     Maintenance and desilting of critical discharge structures.
     •     Real-time inflow and reservoir level monitoring, including seasonal snowpack tracking.
     •     Continued review and testing of the Emergency Response Plan (ERP), including dam break scenarios.
Slope instability around the reservoir may result from repeated or rapid drawdown cycles. Cyclic fluctuations in water
level can destabilise saturated slopes, especially along the steep left bank and unconsolidated shoreline zones.
Potential impacts include localised slumping, shallow landslides, or rockfalls affecting intakes and shoreline
infrastructure. While widespread failure is not anticipated, this remains a moderate residual hazard. Mitigation
measures include:
     •     Controlled drawdown protocols to avoid rapid pore pressure changes.
     •     Real-time slope monitoring and routine geotechnical inspections.
     •     Targeted stabilisation and revegetation works during early operational years.
Seismic hazards remain a long‑term risk. AFRY (2025c) defines the Safety Evaluation Earthquake (SEE) with a PGA of
0.62 g and the Maximum Credible Earthquake (MCE) at 1.17 g, based on rupture of the nearby Southern Fault. Although
the dam is engineered for these loadings, a major earthquake could result in structural deformation, slope failures into
the reservoir, or damage to power tunnels and appurtenant structures.
The consequences of a major earthquake are potentially high, particularly if multiple systems are affected
simultaneously. Long-term mitigation measures include:
     •     Installation and maintenance of structural health monitoring instrumentation.
     •     Periodic updates to seismic hazard data and site-specific design criteria.
     •     Routine dam safety inspections and condition assessments.
     •     ERP protocols for post-seismic inspection, emergency notification, and downstream risk management.
Secondary effects on the Kambarata‑2 HPP reservoir may arise from regulated outflows of the Kambarata‑1 HPP.
Variable releases may cause water level fluctuations in Kambarata‑2, potentially affecting shoreline stability in steep or
unconsolidated areas. Localised shoreline erosion, shallow slope instability, and minor mass‑wasting events may
degrade nearshore habitats or water quality. While not considered a dam safety concern, these effects warrant
monitoring. Recommended measures include targeted shoreline inspections in the Kambarata‑2 reservoir, visual checks
of rim stability, and localised protection or replanting works where degradation is observed.
Residual risks of catastrophic dam failure are not treated here as a primary hazard category, since failure is a
consequence of other hazards (flood, seismic, slope instability). Such scenarios — including potential cascade effects
from upstream HPPs such as At‑Bashy or Kulanak have been modelled in AFRY’s Dam Break Analysis (2025). Results are
incorporated into Chapter 11 (Mitigation and Management) and the Emergency Preparedness and Response Plan
(F‑EPP, 2025).

10.5Social Impacts
This section describes the direct and indirect social impacts that the Kambarata-1 HPP and its associated facilities will
cause during its construction and operation stages. The project area of influence considered in the assessment covers
thirteen villages in the Toktogul and Toguz-Toro districts, which have been described in Section 7: Social Baseline
Conditions. This section covers the following subsections:
     1.    Local Economy and Employment
     2.    Labor and Working Conditions
     3.    Community Infrastructure and Access to Services
     4.    Community Health, Safety and Security
     5.    Land Acquisition, Resettlement and Restriction of Access
     6.    Inclusion, Vulnerabilities and Gender
     7.    Cultural Heritage

Local Economy and Employment
The Project area of influence has a local economy and level of livelihoods that are strongly tied to livestock breeding, as
well as seasonal and traditional ways of migrating. It is noteworthy that none of the AA (Toluk, Sary- Kamysh, Immanaliev
and Atai) have any value-add agro-processing facilities (for example dairy, meat ir canning plants). According to the
Pasture Specialist Report (April 2025) it was observed that this lack of infrastructure results in the loss of added value,
with the outflow of raw materials, resulting in limited local employment opportunities. Toluk and Sary-Kamysh are
particularly vulnerable: despite widespread engagement in agriculture, residents lack access to local processing, which
undermines income generation and economic resilience.

Therefore, the Project area will be highly sensitive to the impacts of the Kambarata-1 HPP activities, as there will be
submergence of a major portion of the pasture lands presently used for grazing. The activities which may lead to impact
on local economy and employment are as follows:
Phase 2 - Construction Phase
     •     Developing and strengthening of access road, bridge across Naryn and transportation tunnel
     •     Construction of the Dam and Power Plant
     •     Influx of workers and setting up of workers’ camps
     •     Establishment of new business
Phase 3 - Operation Phase
     •     Filling up of reservoir and subsequent submergence
     •     Operation of Power Plant
The baseline situation assessment reflects that all the four Aiyl Aimaks (AAs) affected by the Project have an agrarian
economy with a main dependency on livestock breeding, followed by beekeeping and agriculture. The economy and
way of life are deeply intertwined with pastoralism; a practice rooted in generations of tradition. Shepherd families
migrate with their livestock, primarily sheep, but also horses and cows, to seasonal pastures. These pastures play a
crucial role in their livelihoods, providing sustenance and income. Beekeeping is a small yet significant livelihood activity
in the area, practiced by a limited percentage (around 30% of households in Toluk and Sary-Kamysh AAs). Beekeeping
is generally not a primary source of income but serves as a supplementary livelihood alongside livestock farming. Crop
farming is also limited and primarily practiced for personal consumption rather than large-scale commercial production.
For some families, it is the secondary source of income.
The economic assessment shows that about 34% of the population is categorized as ‘poor families’, with the highest
being in Toluk, followed by Atai. It was found that the population is increasingly finding it difficult to balance between




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                  SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                               11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



income and expenditure. Further, the limited employment opportunities and a heavy reliance on livestock farming
restrict income diversification and hinder improvements in living standards.

10.5.1.1          Phase 2 - Construction Phase
During the construction phase, there will be both beneficial and adverse impacts on the local economy and employment
in the area. This section will mainly discuss the positive impacts associated with generating employment in the area,
better infrastructure to boost local economy, and better opportunity for trades. The adverse impacts on pasture-users,
who will feel the strain of development and will be at a risk of livelihood loss or reduction in income, will be discussed
in detail in the Section 0 of this Report. It should be noted, however, that during pre-construction, early construction
and main construction phases, livelihood restoration activities would be required to restore the livelihoods and
inpounded infrastructure upstream K1.

Employment Generation & Local Economic Development
According to the Kambarata-1 Feasibility Study Update (AFRY, June 2025), an estimated average of 4,622 personnel per
year will be needed over the 8.5-year construction period, with up to 7,000 personnel are expected to be employed at
the construction site during peak periods. Peak workforce—ranging from 5,000 to 7,000—anticipated between years 4
and 8 of the construction phase (Figure 10-7). Once operational, the project will require approximately 200 personnel
for the permanent operations and maintenance (O&M) team, covering all staffing levels.




Figure 10-7 Staff at Kambarata 1 during construction (AFRY, 2025e)

The peak period for workforce influx will be in years 3-8 , during construction. During this period, labour influx can have
several beneficial impacts on the employment generation and local economic development, including:
•             Job Creation
Construction and operation of a hydroelectric plant create numerous direct and indirect employment opportunities.
These jobs can range from engineers, project managers, and technicians to labourers, truck drivers, and security
personnel. Local businesses may also see an increase in demand for services such as food, transportation, and
accommodation during the construction phase.
•             Skills Development and Training
Workers involved in the hydro project may acquire new technical skills, particularly in construction, engineering, and
plant operations. These skills can help improve the local workforce's capabilities and may lead to long-term employment
opportunities in other sectors. Training programs and workshops for local workers can contribute to their professional
growth and enhance their employability in future projects or industries.
•             Economic Growth:
The influx of labor can boost local spending, especially for small businesses and service providers.
This can have a ripple effect on the local economy, promoting growth in order sectors like retail, hospitality, and
transportation.
Increased wages and salaries paid to local workers can elevate their standard of living and contribute to improved quality
of life in the community.
•             Increased Tax Revenues:



    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                           Client Reference No. 5018007
    Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                             SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                          11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



The employment generated by the project leads to higher income tax revenues for local governments, which can be
reinvested into community infrastructure, healthcare, education, and social services.
•             Improvement in Local Infrastructure:
The construction of a hydroelectric plant often requires enhancements to local infrastructure, such as roads, bridges,
and utilities, to accommodate workers and support the project. This infrastructure can have lasting benefits for the
community once the project is complete.
The project will aim through a range of measures (e.g. training and skills development through partnerships with
education institutions) to focus on sourcing unskilled workers from the local area to reduce labour influx and generate
local employment. However, considering the present education level and skill set of the local residents, the requisite
skills of construction workers are unlikely to be met short term. It was found that most of the population has education
attainment to high school; however, they have not developed further specific skills post high school attainment. To
enhance employability, the Project can provide training and skill development programs, equipping local workers with
relevant construction and technical skills. These programs will not only meet project needs but also offer long-term
career benefits.
To support communities and upskill, Human Resource Development Contractors can be engaged to assist in working
with communities to fill job vacancies for the Project, and deliver capacity building programs including on-the-job
training models to increase benefit for directly affected communities. Examples include leading, designing and
promoting an enabling environment on both the supply and demand sides of the local labour market, and creating
and maintaining a roster of all eligible individuals in the Project area, and a database list of in-demand local hire jobs,
skills and service contracts.
Furthermore, there will be an increase in local economic activities due to the influx of large number of people. This
includes not only direct workers but also service providers, traders, and job seekers who are drawn to areas experiencing
rapid development. The incoming workforce will create a demand for local goods and services over the construction
and operation period of at least 10 years. There will be increased spending on local businesses including goods and
services: shops, eateries, guest houses, medical services. Similarly, the private sector may respond to new commercial
opportunities by establishing more shops, clinics, restaurants, and logistics services, especially in areas close to project
sites or worker accommodations. The extent that local communities can expand businesses to adapt to the increased
demand provides a significant opportunity for the local population gaining further employment opportunities. Thus,
construction activities would have a positive impact on developing the socio-economic conditions in general and
employment generation specifically within the project affected villages and the Region (Jalal-Abad) generally.
Additionally, this surge in economic activity can, in turn, lead to improvements in local services. For example, local
authorities may invest more actively in expanding and upgrading public infrastructure and services—such as healthcare,
education, water supply, and sanitation—to meet the rising demand and maintain social stability. Kyrgyz Government
will be investing in local infrastructure such as roads, transport facilities, for the largest HPP in the country, which will
help develop the local markets, improve accessibility and encourage tourism. The improvement in public infrastructure,
service delivery systems and transport service, which will lead to better economic condition of the people.
Overall, there is likely to be a significant boost to the local economy and a positive impact on the socio-economic
condition of the local people. Based on the ESIA methodology, significance scoring is not required.

Economic Strain on Local Communities
However, increased demand for goods and services may lead to price inflation, making essential items less affordable
for local residents. if the demand for commodities and services essential to the local population is not managed, this
will increase pressure and prices on local goods and services necessary to local communities. It could also affect the
consumption of resources, including water, energy, land use, in addition to local services. As a mitigation measure, the
contractor should procure worker and camp supplies in a manner that does not affect the available of essential
commodities and services for the local population.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
       •      Magnitude High (60): The Project may cause significant, potentially irreversible economic disruption across
              communities, especially affecting vulnerable groups and livelihoods due to labor influx and market strain.




    Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                             Client Reference No. 5018007

    Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                            11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



     •     Extent Regional (60): Impacts will be extended to the villages outside the footprint, affecting multiple villages
           and engaging broader community and stakeholder concerns; hence, the extent of impact has been
           considered as regional.
     •     Duration Mid-term (10): The impact will last through the 5–10-year construction phase and gradually decline
           as the project enters operation and migrant worker numbers decrease.
     •     Overall Impact Significance: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the
           potential adverse impacts on local economy and employment are assessed as High (130) if not properly
           managed.

10.5.1.2          Phase 3 – Operation Phase

Reduction in Workforce Demand
The employment rate will gradually decrease by the end of the construction phase. As construction ends and the Project
moves into operation, direct employment for the local communities will sharply decrease. The exact number of workers
and their composition (by skill level) that will be needed during the operation of the dam are not known at this time. It
is likely that these will be a combination of technical, engineering and environmental/social jobs, for which the local
communities may not have the requisite skills. This will directly and adversely impact the communities and create a loss
of income and a decrease in livelihoods that many households will have adapted to during the construction period.
It is important to have a system in place to help local people laid off by the contractors after construction acquire
enhanced or new skills through training and skill development. Otherwise, there will be sudden spurt of unemployed
persons in the area, which will have a negative impact on the local economy.
As can be interpreted from the above section, loss of jobs will have a severe impact on the livelihood and adversely
impact the overall local economy.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude High (60): Although an estimate of unskilled job opportunities for local workers is currently
           unavailable and will be determined in later project phases, it is likely that job losses could exceed 10% of the
           local labor force, indicating a high impact magnitude.
     •     Extent Regional (60): The construction workers who will lose employment will not be limited to those staying
           in the affected villages but from regions other than Jalal-Abad, indicating a broad geographic impact.
     •     Duration is Long-term (20): Without training, capacity-building programs, and post-construction employment
           opportunities, job losses could have long-term impacts on the community.
     •     Overall Impact Significance: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the
           potential adverse impacts on employment loss are assessed as Critical (140) if not properly managed.

Potential Impacts on Fisheries
During the operation phase of the hydropower project, the formation of the reservoir is expected to create new
opportunities for fishery-based livelihoods in upstream areas. The reservoir ecosystem may support the growth of
certain fish species, which can be harvested by local communities for both subsistence and income generation. This has
the potential to diversify local livelihoods, especially in communities previously dependent on agriculture or seasonal
labor, offering a relatively stable and accessible source of income.
However, the development of fisheries in the reservoir also brings potential challenges. If the increase in fishing activity
is not regulated or managed properly, it could lead to overfishing, biodiversity loss, or ecosystem imbalance.
Additionally, without proper oversight, there is a risk of non-compliance with national fishery regulations or licensing
requirements, which could expose communities or project stakeholders to legal or administrative penalties. These risks
could further strain relations between local communities, government agencies, and project developers.
Generally, the potential increase in fisheries suggests positive impacts on livelihood of the local communities,
hhowever, it requires robust monitoring systems and capacity-building efforts to ensure legal compliance and long-term
sustainability. According to the ESIA methodology, significance scoring is not applicable.

Labor and Working Conditions
The area of influence has a population that lives in remote villages on mountainous terrain, living and working in
traditional ways. Therefore, it will be highly sensitive to the arrival of a large, predominantly male workforce not familiar
with the local traditional way of living and cultural practices, as well as to the introduction of potentially unfair or unsafe
labor and working conditions.
The project impacts on Labor and Working Conditions mainly arise during the Phase 2 – Construction Phase and Phase
3 – Operation Phase. The following sections will discuss the impacts in detail.

10.5.1.4          Phase 2 - Construction Phase
At peak construction, up to 7,000 personnel are expected to be employed. While many roles will demand specialised
technical expertise, most unskilled and trade-related jobs will be handled by subcontractors. Key risks and impacts
during this phase include those related to labor management, working conditions, and occupational health and safety
(OHS).

Labor Management and Working Conditions Impacts
In addition to potential labor influx risks, such a large workforce will pose potential labor management and working
conditions risks. These risks may be applicable to workers coming from outside the community, as well as community
members working in construction activities. These risks can be regarding:
     •     Terms and conditions of employment: Workers may not be aware of the terms, conditions and rights
           regarding their work. Timely and full payment of wages; requiring workers to work overtime beyond national
           requirements and safety practices; payment for overtime work; requisite break and rest periods; poor
           working conditions; retaliation against workers who raise concerns about safety of working conditions may
           become issues.
     •     Non-discrimination and equal opportunity: There may be discrimination, unequal pay risks and unequal
           opportunities to get employment between men and women, and between foreign workers and local workers.
     •     Workers’ organization: There may be barriers for workers to form and join workers’ organization to bargain
           collectively. The laws of the Kyrgyz Republic provide for the right of workers to form and join trade unions,
           though the law does not apply to migrant workers. In this case, the law of country of the contractor may
           govern the workers of the contractor.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3                                                 Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                                                                   SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                                                                11 August 2025
Description of Impacts



     •     Child labor: The labor code sets 16 as the minimum age for work and 18 as the minimum age for hazardous
           work. The risk for child labor in this project is anticipated to be low.
     •     Forced labor: The labor code prohibits and criminalises forced labor and compulsory labor in the Kyrgyz
           Republic. Nevertheless, there may be risks of contractors taking possession of passports and other travel
           documents of migrant workers and/or instituting other mechanisms restricting workers’ ability to freely leave
           employment.
     •     Worker accommodation and facilities: Project workers need to be provided with facilities appropriate to the
           circumstances of their work, including access to canteens, hygiene facilities and appropriate areas for rest.
           The quality of accommodation should promote the health, safety, and well-being of project workers, and
           provide access to services that accommodate their physical social and cultural needs.
     •     Workplace discrimination and harassment: Foreign workers and women may face discrimination and
           harassment, creating social tensions within the workforce and local communities.
     •     Sexual exploitation and abuse (SEA)/sexual harassment (SH): A high potential risks among the workforce,
           with women and local workers at higher risk.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): Since the effects will directly impact the project workforce, and
           due to the nature of labor management challenges, inadequate mitigation could affect a significant portion of
           workers, potentially exceeding 10% of the total workforce.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) since the effects will occur on the Project workforce who come from
           outside the region or even other countries.
     •     Duration of Impact is Mid-term (10) as it mainly happens during the construction phase which is expected to
           last 8.5 years.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of labor influx are assessed as High (130) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed.

Impacts on Occupational Health and Safety (OHS)
During construction, there will also be OHS risks for all workers. Common activities undertaken during construction can
introduce high risks to the health and safety of the construction workforce. The main potential health and safety
construction risks associated with the project will be related to the following:
     •     Use of explosives for tunnelling
     •     Working at height in the tunnels and dam
     •     Working in confined spaces
     •     Hot work, such as welding
     •     Repetitive working and hard manual labor
     •     Exposure to physical hazards from use of heavy equipment and machinery
     •     Working with pressurized equipment
     •     Excavation on steep, potentially unstable slopes
     •     Trip and fall hazards
     •     Exposure to noise and dust
     •     Exposure to electrical hazards from the use of tools and machinery
     •     Carrying heavy loads
     •     Exposure to chemicals (e.g., paints, fuels, fresh concrete)
     •     Transportation within the project tunnels
     •     Working in both hot and cold conditions due to the climate extremes

     •     Risk of road traffic incidents
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): Since the effects will directly impact the project workforce, and
           due to the nature of labor management challenges, inadequate mitigation could affect a significant portion of
           workers, potentially exceeding 10% of the total workforce.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) since the effects will occur on the Project workforce who come from
           outside the region or even other countries.
     •     Duration of Impact is Mid-term (10) as it mainly happens during the construction phase which is expected to
           last 8.5 years.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of labor influx are assessed as High (130) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed.
These potential adverse impacts will be managed through robust labor management procedures and Codes of Conduct
for the workers, as well as safe, contained and effective worker accommodation and sensitization of the local
communities.

10.5.1.5          Phase 3 - Operation Phase
As the Project transitions to the operation phase, the construction workforce will be significantly reduced. While the
exact number of operation-phase workers is unavailable at this time of reporting, industry trends suggest that a large-
scale hydropower plant typically requires 400 to 700 workers, depending on automation levels, plant complexity, and
operational demands. Nearly two-thirds of these roles are in operations and maintenance, primarily consisting of skilled
labor such as engineers, technicians, and administrative staff. The remaining 30% includes security, maintenance
personnel, and other support roles, which generally involve unskilled labor (IRENA & ILO, 2023).

Impacts from Labor Management and Working Conditions & Occupational Health and Safety (OHS)
With a smaller workforce and the local community having adjusted to migrant labor over the 8.5 years of construction,
the impacts of labor influx are expected to be eliminated during the operation phase. However, key labor management
risks will still apply, albeit on a much smaller scale. These include risks related to labor management, working conditions,
and OHS during operations, though the likelihood and severity of such risks will be significantly lower compared to the
construction phase.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): Since the effects will directly impact the project workforce, and
           due to the nature of labor management challenges, inadequate mitigation could affect a significant portion of
           workers, potentially exceeding 10% of the total workforce.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Site-specific (10) since the effects will occur on the Project workforce within the
           Project footprint.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20) as it will happen throughout the operation phase which is expected to
           last more than 20 years.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of labor influx are assessed as Moderate (90) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed.

Significance scoring of impacts on Labour and Working Conditions associated with different stages of the construction
and operation of the K-1 HPP has been conducted. The results of this scoring are detailed in Appendix 10-A. A
summary of the outcomes is provided in Table 10-28.

Community Infrastructure and Access to Services
10.5.1.7 Phase 2 – Construction
Analysis of GIS map data and consultations with local communities has indicated that 83.42 km of horse and foot trails
will be inundated by the reservoir (Figure 10-8). In addition, eleven (11) bridges including two bridges used to cross the
Toluk River and Temirken River will be submerged under water. Table 10-29 below shows the roads, trails and bridges
that will be affected by the project in the four AAs as determined in Focus Group Discussions (FGDs) with the
communities.
The loss of these trails will prevent access to certain pastures. This impact and proposed mitigation to address those
impacts is addressed in Section 10.5.5 of this report.
According to consultation with local authorities, FGDs and Key Informant Interviews (KIIs), no school, healthcare
facilities, irrigation, and water supply system will be impacted by the Project.

10.5.1.8 Phase 3 – Operation
By the time of operation of the dam, together with benefit sharing and livelihood restoration planning, road, electricity
and water infrastructure of the communities will be renovated and improved. In FGDs with the communities,
communities raised poor road infrastructure, water scarcity and quality, and unreliable electricity as their biggest
challenges for productivity, livelihoods, access to markets, health, living quality and wellbeing.
Water scarcity and quality were repeatedly brought up by local communities as in FGDs as a challenge touching all areas
of their lives: Health issues like water borne diseases from lack of clean drinking water; livelihoods issues affecting crop
growing, sustaining herds, and beekeeping; sanitation issues at education and healthcare facilities.
Similarly, poor road infrastructure affects all areas of local communities’ lives from access to markets and livehoods to
timely and easy access to education and healthcare services.
There are a number of infrastructure improvement proposals that could be considered for Community Benefit Sharing
. However, the details of Community Benefits Sharing extend beyond scope of ESIA and relevance to K1 HPP are
discussed in Chapter 11. Notwithstanding this, improvements in these areas will be critically positive for the
communities. According to the ESIA methodology, significance scoring is not applicable.

Community Health, Safety and Security
The area of influence for Community Health, Safety, and Security (CHSS) impacts extends well beyond the immediate
project footprint. It includes both direct impact areas —comprising 10 villages—and indirect areas such as nearby
settlements, potential labor camp locations, and local marketplaces where workers and community members may
interact. As the exact location of the workers' camp has yet to be finalized, the area of influence is currently estimated
to extend up to 30 kilometers from the Project site, with the most significant impacts expected in the E. Imanaliev and
Toluk AAs, where the main construction activities will take place.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3           Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                             SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                          11 August 2025                                 Page 110
Description of Impacts



The project area of influence has a low-density population. Therefore, there is high level of sensitivity associated with
the transport, excavation and ground works of the project, which can lead to direct health and safety impacts on local
communities. These health and safety impacts can be varied, ranging from noise and air pollution, reduced safety of
local families, traffic accidents, communicable and sexually transmitted diseases, gender-based violence, and security
issues.
During the Pre-construction Phase, most of the activities such as construction of access road, installation of Power
Supply System, Water Supply and Sewage System, Heating and Air Supply System will be localized and confined to the
Project site. As there are no settlements in the vicinity of the Project site and the access road, interaction of the Project
with the community will be minimum and no significant impact is envisaged on community health and safety during this
phase.
The main impact to community health, safety and security (CHSS) during the Project lifecycle will be due to labour influx,
increased traffic volume on local roads and increased pollution level for air quality, water quality and noise levels. The
activities which may lead to impact on community health and safety are as follows:
Phase 2 - Construction Phase
     •     Developing and strengthening of access roads
     •     Quarrying for stone aggregates and crushing activities
     •     Blasting for excavation
     •     Influx of workers and setting up of workers’ camps
     •     Transportation of construction materials (including hazardous) and equipment through multi-axle vehicles
     •     Movement of workers and increase in traffic volume
Phase 3 – Operation Phase
     •     Filling up of reservoir and subsequent submergence
     •     Operation of Power Plant
The baseline health condition of the area indicates that there is incidence of hypertension (especially in the high-altitude
villages), cardiovascular diseases, respiratory diseases, diabetes and anaemia are quite high. In addition to that there is
prevalence of diarrheal diseases among children, mainly in the villages of Toluk and Kyzyl-Ozgorush due to poor quality
drinking water quality. For health care service issues, these include shortages of medical supplies and personnel, lack of
infrastructures and poor access to the health care facilities in the area.

10.5.1.10 Phase 2 - Construction Phase
The construction activities as mentioned above are envisaged to have adverse impacts on public health, community
dynamics, community safety & security and will mainly affect community in proximity of the construction zones, worker
camps/accommodations and roads used to access the site. These residential areas will be highly sensitive to potential
health, safety and security impacts during construction.

Impacts on Community Health
The impact on health during construction may occur due to air pollution, increased noise levels, generation of
wastewater, improper storage of hazardous materials & wastes and labour influx. The details are discussed in the
following sections.
Impact due to Air Pollution: One of the main aspects affecting public health during project construction is air pollution
due to construction activities and transportation of construction materials and machineries. In case of the K-1 HPP, the
source of air pollutants affecting communities will be mainly from transportation as there are no settlements near the
work zone. The impact is expected to be nearer to the intersection of the North-South Highway and the access road to
the K-1 HPP, as there will be concentration of vehicles in this place, coming from both directions of Bishkek and Osh. So,
the settlements of Kara-Jygach and Osh-Terek will be impacted the most from incremental particulate and gaseous
(NO2, CO, etc) emissions from transport vehicles. In addition, stone mining activities in the slopes of the mountains and
near to the horse tracks/trails used by the villagers of Toluk can also have significant environmental impacts such as
noise, vibration, dust, smoke and ground disturbance and consequently health issues. As found in the baseline study,
there is already high incidence of respiratory and cardiovascular ailments in the area, which has the risk of aggravating
further due to pollution.
Impact due to Increase in Noise Levels: Potential noise pollution for the K-1 HPP will be associated with construction
work activities i.e. blasting, truck movements, excavations, ground works. As already mentioned, as there are no
settlements in the vicinity of the construction site, noise generated from excavation, groundwork, etc. will not have any
impact on the community. However, noise generated from blasting in the quarry areas and movement of heavy vehicles
will have impact on the local communities. It has been found that there are pastures and horse tracks/trails near to the
quarry areas. Although people using these roads may be impacted during blasting, however as the time of blasting will
be fixed, the impact will be negligible. The noise generated from the movement of vehicles will have significant impact
due to the noise level exceeding the human health threshold values as defined by WHO.
Water Pollution: Run-off from raw material stockyards, hazardous material/waste storages, civil activities from
construction may pollute the water of Naryn River, downstream from the Project site. Further, quarrying activities on
the right bank may degrade the water quality of Temirkent, a stream joining Naryn downstream of the Project site. It is
found that there is a water abstraction point at Uch-Terek, the water from which is used for domestic and agriculture
purposes. Pollutants from construction activities and improper management of wastes may increase the heavy metal
content of the river water, which will eventually impact the water consumers at Uch-Terek. The above factors will
further lead to incidence of waterborne diseases associated with poor water quality and poor sanitary conditions.
Hazardous Materials and Wastes: The construction activities will involve transportation and handling of various
hazardous materials such as fuels and inflammable gases such as acetylene and LPG. These hazardous materials and
wastes, if not handled and disposed properly, may pose a risk to the environment and to human health and safety. As
mentioned, the risk will be of spillage mainly during transportation as the storage will be at a secured place within the
Project site and away from any settlements. Contamination of water and soil due to run-offs from waste areas may lead
to chronic and terminal diseases such as cancer.
Labour Influx: Currently skill levels of the local population are low and coupled with low population density, it is
anticipated that a significant proportion the workers will need to be sourced from outside the local area. The influx of
workers to the community may cause impacts to public health, especially an increase in prevalence of diseases. Influx
of migrant labours during construction can cause physical interaction of the migrant workforce with the local people,
which may cause some adverse impacts to public health in the neighbouring villages with the potential for spread of
infectious diseases like sexually transmitted infections and HIV/AIDS. Improper sanitation facilities and disposal of
municipal solid waste in the worker camps and poor housekeeping in the construction sites can also trigger vector borne
diseases.
As can be interpreted from the above section, there are risks of impact on community health during the construction
phase of the Project. The impacts can be caused because of emission and discharge of pollutants, Waste & hazardous
waste handling and disposal as well as influx of labour force from outside areas.

Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked Moderate (60): Although there are no fenced communities and the distance
           of the nearest community is Kara Jygach (17km by road) as part of Nichke-Sai , the Project is expected to
           result in frequent and unavoidable interactions between non-local workers and local communities,
           particularly marketplaces and vehicles carrying construction materials. These interactions, combined with air
           and noise pollution from heavy vehicle traffic and quarry operations, as well as the potential contamination
           of local water sources, pose serious health risks to communities. Given the baseline prevalence of respiratory
           illnesses, these magnitude of impact on community health is assessed as High.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) Since the effects will occur on the communities away from the
           project footprint and extend beyond 5-km, the extent of Impact will be classified as Regional.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): construction is expected to last 8.5 years, however its consequence
           may last long-term (more than 10 years). Also, from past experiences it has been seen that a part of the
           population may settle in this area, even after completion of construction.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           on community health are assessed as Moderate (140) if not properly mitigated and managed

Impacts on Community Dynamics and Social Interaction
The direct area of influence for the project has 10 villages with a population of approximately 15,000 villagers. In
addition to the above, market places such as Uch Terek and Kara Jigach will also experience an increase in population.
At peak construction, the project is expected to employ 7,000 workers, equivalent to 20% of the directly affected villages
and 12% of the population in a broader area (i.e. four directly affected Aiyl Aimaks). Many positions require specialized
technical skills, while most unskilled and trade jobs will be managed by subcontractors.
According to the socio-economic baseline, most local residents have completed secondary education (9th grade), but
fewer advance to the 11th grade or to post-secondary education. Even fewer pursue professional training. Skilled
individuals from the area often migrate to cities such as Jalal-Abad, Osh, and Bishkek, or abroad to Russia and Turkiyë
for better economic opportunities.
Given the significant workforce demand and the technical skill gap among local residents, a large influx of in-migrant
workers is expected in the Project area. These individuals will come from various backgrounds and locations, including
non-local relatives of residents, daily or weekly commuters, project employees, and contractors or subcontractors from
outside the region or even other countries. To further service the labour influx, there will be other people such as
vendors, shop owners etc. further increasing the population in the area. In addition to the workers, there may be also
other Project Induced population immigration of unemployed or underemployed people looking for job opportunities.
The substantial increase in population in an area with low population density will place considerable strain on social
dynamics.
It has been found during interactions with village leaders that the communities are conservative and stay in closed
communities with very strong bonding. They are not used to people from other cultural backgrounds and the acceptance
of outsiders may not be easily accommodated by these communities. This will give rise to risk of conflicts between local
communities and the workers, especially near market areas such as Uch Terek and Kara-Jigach, which are the main
market areas nearest to the construction site. Workers from outside the area may not speak Kyrgyz and/or may not be
familiar with local cultural practices. Misunderstanding due to cultural differences will also lead to tensions or conflict
with community members.
As mentioned before, the project will also bring in a floating population who are not workers, but unemployed or
underemployed people looking for economic benefits from project. Even family members of workers may slowly
migrate to this area. This type of project induced migration will be difficult to manage and has the potential to become
an additional burden on the law enforcement agencies. Entry of these people in the area along with free movement of
migrant workers from different cultural backgrounds could increase risks of gender-based violence and sexual
harassment, exploitation and abuse, theft, physical assaults, substance abuse, prostitution, and human trafficking.
Another factor that can destabilize the social equilibrium is the disparity in income. It has been established that the
economic status of the locals is quite low, and a major portion of the population are just able to sustain their lives. The
migrant workers will have a steady source of income and thus will have greater purchasing power. This may lead to
price inflation in the area, further marginalizing the local population. This will also lead to risk of social discontent and



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                          Page 113
Description of Impacts



unrest. Examples of this can already be seen in the project area, with the commencement of large infrastructure
projects.
 A major influx of outside people with different cultural backgrounds in a close-knit community will have major impact
on the overall socio-cultural fabric of the area. As discussed above, this may lead to social conflict and discontent in the
community, even leading to law-and-order situations.
In summary, typical adverse effects of labor influx on Community Dynamics and Social Interaction may include:
     •     Cultural and Language Barriers: Workers from outside the area may not speak Kyrgyz and/or may not be
           familiar with local cultural practices, leading to tensions or conflict with community members
     •     Increased Crime and Security Concerns: The influx of workers and service providers may contribute to higher
           crime rates or a heightened perception of insecurity among locals. This could include theft, physical assaults,
           substance abuse, prostitution, and human trafficking. Local law enforcement may struggle to manage the
           increased demand for security.
     •     Health Risks and Disease Transmission: The arrival of new workers may introduce communicable diseases,
           including sexually transmitted infections (STIs), to the area. In turn, incoming workers may also be exposed to
           local diseases for which they have little immunity. This can overwhelm local healthcare systems, particularly if
           workers seek anonymous treatment at local clinics rather than project medical facilities. Additionally,
           emergency services may be under-resourced to handle an increase in industrial accidents at the construction
           site.
     •     Traffic and Transportation Challenges: The movement of construction materials and workers can contribute
           to traffic congestion and a higher risk of road accidents, placing additional strain on transportation
           infrastructure.
     •     Workplace Discrimination and Harassment: Foreign workers and women may face discrimination and
           harassment, creating social tensions within the workforce and local communities.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): The influx of 25% additional population into low-density, close-knit
           communities is likely to cause significant disruption, including cultural conflict, crime, health risks, and social
           unrest.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60): Since the effects will occur on the multiple communities away from
           the project footprint and extend beyond 5-km.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): construction is expected to last 8.5 years, however its consequence
           may last long-term (more than 10 years). Also, from past experiences it has been seen that a part of the
           population may settle in this area, even after completion of construction.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of Community Dynamics and Social Interaction are assessed as Critical (140) adverse if not properly
           mitigated and managed.

Impacts on Community Safety & Security
As can be interpreted from the above section, there are risks of impact on community safety and security during the
construction phase of the Project. The safety and security issues may be caused by transport of construction materials
and equipment, along with daily worker commutes to and from the project site, labour influx, construction activities
and entry of locals in the restricted zones.
Transportation of Project vehicles: There will be drastic increase in the traffic volume on the local roads, including the
North-South Highway, due to heavy vehicles carrying raw materials such as cement, fly-ash, stone aggregates, steel, etc
for construction as well as machineries for the Power Plant. It is envisaged that materials will be coming from the
directions of Bishkek as well as Osh. The population in this area are also not used to high traffic volumes and thus there
will be an increased risk of road accidents in the settlements adjacent to the Highway such as Uch-Terek and Kara-
Jaigach. However, there will be no risks on the access road to the K-1 HPP from the Highway, as there are no settlements
or traffic in the area, except for traffic moving into the K-2 HPP.

Construction activities: Stone mining activities for aggregates will be done at the vicinity of the construction site. As
these activities will be mainly done on the slopes of the mountains and there are trails used by the locals in these areas,
mainly Toluk, there may be safety concerns leading to accidents, if the areas are not properly demarcated and
barricaded. It has been found that the mining areas are prone to landslides, due to loose stones.
There will be major excavations at the the Plant construction site, which may be a safety issue for community people
and animals. Due to no settlements near the site, there will be minimal movements of people near these areas.
However, there is likely for communities to access pastures. The open trenches may pose a safety risk to the public if
not managed properly.
Labor influx: There will be reduced safety and security of local families, women and children resulting from potential
labor influx and movement of overseas workers into the area leading to potential local conflict. As already mentioned,
in addition to the workers, there will be other migrants such as jobseekers, small traders, and family members of
workers who will be seeking accommodation in the nearby villages, leading to direct interaction with the local people.
As these people will not be under control of the Project Management, this will raise security concerns in the
communities. There will be risk of increase in gender-based violence (GBV) and Sexual Exploitation & Abuse and Sexual
Harassment (SEA/SH). Further, the influx of about 7000 workers may attract sex-based activities in the area, thus
destroying the present cultural alignment in the area and putting the locals at safety and security risks
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): The influx of in-migrant population in the area will have a
           significant impact on the local population due to cultural conflicts, illicit and culturally unacceptable
           behaviour of the migrants, disturbance in the present socio-cultural fabric, etc.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) Since the effects will occur on the communities away from the
           project footprint and extend beyond 5-km.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): construction is expected to last 8.5 years, however its consequence
           may last long-term (more than 10 years). Also, from past experiences it has been seen that a part of the
           population may settle in this area, even after completion of construction.
     •     Overall : Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of safety and security are assessed as Critical (140) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed by
           project management and security agencies.

SEA/SH Risks
Kyrgyzstan faces ongoing challenges related to GBV, particularly in rural regions where social norms often tolerate
domestic violence, child marriage, and bride kidnapping. Although national laws prohibit sexual harassment, domestic
violence and marital rape, enforcement is weak—especially outside urban centers. National strategies and GBV
coordination groups exist but have limited reach in remote areas, where service provision is fragmented and under-
resourced.
The construction of a large hydropower plant in a rural area presents significant SEA/SH risks, largely driven by the influx
of a predominantly male, non-local workforce into small, traditional communities where services are limited and gender
norms are deeply rooted. The following SEA/SH risks are likely to arise during the construction phase of the Project:
     •     Sexual exploitation of local women and girls: Women and girls in project-affected villages may be exposed to
           sexual exploitation, including transactional sex, coerced relationships with workers, or abuse linked to unequal
           economic or social power. Further, there will be additional influx of people in form of goods vehicle drivers and
           floating population (including job seekers), who will have interaction with the local population. These risks are
           heightened where poverty levels are high, and livelihood alternatives are scarce.
     •     Harassment and sexual violence in public spaces and in the workplace: Increased presence of workers through
           local markets, paths, and service areas may lead to incidents of sexual harassment or assault—especially in
           areas lacking lighting, policing, or safe spaces for women. Girls walking to school or women accessing health
           services may be particularly vulnerable. In addition to that, there may risk of sexual harassment at the
           construction sites where women has been assigned work.
     •     Intimate Partner Violence (IPV): Construction projects often disrupt traditional roles and increase household
           stress. If local men feel displaced by outsiders or economic changes, IPV rates can rise. Increased income among
           male workers may also fuel alcohol use and associated violence.

 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                         Page 115
Description of Impacts



     •     Trafficking and exploitation risks: In the absence of strict oversight, temporary settlements near the
           construction site may give rise to informal economies, including sex work. This raises risks of trafficking,
           especially for adolescent girls or economically vulnerable women.
     •     Limited access to Grievance Mechanism: Survivors in rural areas may have no clear or trusted way to report
           abuse or seek support. Shame, fear of retaliation, and a lack of confidential services prevent many women from
           coming forward. Moreover, local health or legal services may lack GBV response training.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): The high magnitude reflects the serious risk of harm from a large,
           non-local male workforce entering rural communities with limited GBV services and entrenched gender norms.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) The regional extent arises from the potential impact across multiple
           villages, with risks of public concern and involvement of national stakeholders.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): construction is expected to last 8.5 years, however its consequence
           may last long-term (more than 10 years). Also, from past experiences it has been seen that a part of the
           population may settle in this area, even after completion of construction.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of safety and security are assessed as Critical (140) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed by
           project management and security agencies.

Risk of Security Personnel
According to the PMO, the K-1 HPP Project site has been designated a strategic facility under Kyrgyz Republic legislation
and will be subject to special security protocols as stipulated in Government Resolution No. 56 (February 12, 2015). This
mandates the deployment of armed security personnel, typically from state institutions, to safeguard the facility. As the
areas near to the construction site are used by locals to access pastures, especially from the villages of Toluk and Nichke-
Sai, the deployment of armed security force may carry significant social and human rights risks, particularly in rural and
socially cohesive communities.
In the context of the Project area, the presence of armed, uniformed personnel may be deeply unsettling for residents,
especially if security staff are non-local and do not share the community’s language, ethnicity, or religion. The
introduction of a visible military or police presence in villages unaccustomed to such forces can foster mistrust, fear,
and a sense of intimidation. These concerns might be intensified by Jalal-Abad Region’s legacy of tense relations with
state security forces, notably during the 2010 ethnic violence, when reports of arbitrary detention and excessive use of
force by police damaged public confidence in state institutions. Also, it has been already reported during the
consultations that there are conflicts with the security of the K-2 HPP and cases of animals being confiscated by security
personnel. This could be further amplified by the lack of clear communication or community engagement regarding the
presence and role of such personnel.
Furthermore, the potential lack of demographic alignment between the security forces and local communities may fuel
perceptions of exclusion or favoritism, especially if security actions are seen as protecting the interests of the state or
project developers over local well-being. These risks can escalate tensions and lead to social friction, erosion of trust, or
even conflict between the community and project stakeholders.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60): The use of armed, non-local state security personnel in culturally
           homogenous and historically sensitive rural areas poses a significant risk of intimidation, community tension,
           and human rights violations (e.g., excessive force, harassment). Legacy grievances with state forces and lack of
           community engagement increase the severity.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Regional (60) The regional extent arises from the potential impact across multiple
           villages, with risks of public concern and involvement of national stakeholders.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): construction is expected to last 8.5 years, however its consequence
           may last long-term (more than 10 years). Also, from past experiences it has been seen that a part of the
           population may settle in this area, even after completion of construction.
