 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                          Page 116
Description of Impacts



     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of safety and security are assessed as Critical (140) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed by
           project management and security agencies.

10.5.1.11 Phase 3 - Operation Phase
As the Project moves into the operation phase, the construction workforce will be significantly downsized. With fewer
workers and communities having adapted to the presence of migrant labor over the 8.5 years of construction, impacts
related to community health and social dynamics from labor influx are expected to diminish. However, some community
safety and security risks may persist, though at a much lower level.

Community Safety & Security Impacts
As can be interpreted from the above section, there are risks of impact on community safety and security during the
O&M phase of the Project. The safety and security issues may be caused by trespassing into the Project area, straying
of animals, etc. The analysis of the expected impact is as follows:
Safety issues related to access: During the O&M Phase, the main concern will be safety issues related to access to
pastureland and other service delivery systems. The establishment of a huge reservoir of length of about 68km will
increase the risk of community safety and security. There will be access restrictions due to the formation of the reservoir,
as it has been seen that many access routes will fall in the reservoir area or the sanitary Protection Zone. The loss of
access will mainly affect the village of Toluk and for Sary-Kamysh to a certain extent. Unless alternate routes are
identified and constructed before the filling of the reservoir, the villagers may try new routes near to the reservoir,
leading to safety issues related to drowning.
Security guard deployment: During operations, deployment of security guards near the Project and reservoir periphery
may lead to risks of conflict with local communities. The security may use excessive or disproportionate force on
communities in cases of conflict, leading to clashes. However, these events will be very infrequent and thus the impact
will be not very significant.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked Moderate (20) As there are no fence communities and the distance of the
           nearest settlement is far, the only possible interaction of the Project with local communities will be in the
           pastures near to the Project site. Conflict situations and accidents are expected to be infrequent and will not
           be very significant; Thus, the magnitude of Impact will be Moderate (20).
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Local (40) Since the impacts will primarily affect communities located within the
           reservoir and HPP area—situated within 5 km of the Project footprint.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long Term (20) The impact will persist throughout the project lifecycle.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of safety and security are assessed as Moderate (80) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed.

Land Acquisition, Resettlement, Loss of Livelihood and Restriction of Access
The population living and working in the Project’s area of influence significantly depend on pastureland for their way of
life and livelihoods. In FGDs, herders raised some existing pasture degradation and overgrazing issues. Inundation and
land acquisition under the Project will result in loss of many pastures, and loss of horse and foot trails detailed in the
above Community Infrastructure section will further cause loss of access to many more pastures. Therefore, there is a
high level of sensitivity associated with land-based impacts of the Project.
The Project land acquisition, resettlement and restriction of access impacts and mitigation measures will be assessed
and described in detail in the Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plans (LRRPs) that will be prepared for the
Project. Preliminary assessment and estimation of impacts are summarized below.

10.5.1.13 Phase 1 – Pre-construction Phase

Impacts on Community Perception
Land acquisition and resettlement planning are critical activities in the design and planning phase of the Project, as they
directly influence social stability. If not managed properly, these processes can lead to disputes over land tenure and
compensation, resulting in social instability, delays, and resistance from affected communities.
During the design and planning phase, social conflicts overcompensation and land tenure can arise due to concerns
about land tenure insecurity, fairness, transparency, and past negative experiences with land acquisition. Both formal
and informal grazing practices coexist in the pastures that will be required for the Project. Many local residents use
pastures without official agreements, particularly for winter grazing. This lack of formalized use may lead to conflicts
among pasture users, as well as conflicts with government authorities over land ownership, eligibility for compensation,
and the adequacy of compensation packages.
Moreover, past negative experiences with land compensation such as delayed or inadequate payments in of the
Kambarata-2 Hydropower Plant Project, can lead to distrust and resistance from local communities. Without meaningful
consultation, affected people may feel excluded from decision-making, further exacerbating tensions. To prevent such
conflicts, it is essential to conduct early and transparent consultations with stakeholders, ensure that affected people
are involved in resettlement planning, their concerns will be taken into consideration into the LRRP preparation.
Given past negative experiences with land acquisition and compensation, along with the significant livelihood
dependence of potentially affected households on pasture areas, the sensitivity to these impacts is considered high.
However, the magnitude of the impact is assessed as high. As a result, the overall impact of social conflicts
overcompensation and land tenure due to land acquisition and resettlement planning is classified as a critical adverse
impact before mitigation and management measures are implemented.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact – High (60): The land acquisition process will affect both formal and informal users of
           pastureland, with many relying heavily on winter grazing areas without official tenure. Disputes are likely due
           to perceptions of unfairness, past negative experiences with compensation (e.g., delayed or inadequate
           payments during the Kambarata-2 Hydropower Plant Project), and lack of clear land ownership or usage
           rights. If meaningful consultations are not conducted early and transparently, affected communities may feel
           excluded from decision-making processes, exacerbating tensions. These unresolved grievances could not only
           destabilize local social dynamics but also significantly hinder project implementation and timelines.

 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3            Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                              SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                           11 August 2025                                  Page 118
Description of Impacts



     •      Extent of Impact – Regional (60): The land acquisition impacts will affect multiple communities across the
            three districts. Conflicts may arise between local pasture users and government authorities, as well as among
            pasture users themselves in these areas, impacting regional social cohesion.
     •      Duration of Impact – Long-term (20): Although the risk of conflict arises primarily during the design and
            planning phase, its consequences—such as community mistrust, social fragmentation, and resistance to the
            Project—can extend into the construction and operational phases. If not adequately addressed through
            inclusive planning and transparent stakeholder engagement, these issues may persist for many years,
     •      Overall: Considering the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
            on community perception over compensation and land tenure are classified as Critical (140) before the
            implementation of mitigation and management measures.

10.5.1.14 Phase 2 – Construction Phase
During construction phase, land acquisition, compensation and resettlement is one of critical activities that is required
to be fully implemented before any construction activities commence.

10.5.1.14.1        Overview
Project Affected Persons. Based on consultations with Municipal Enterprises of the affected Aiyl Aimaks in February
2025 and latest data provided by Pasture Management Consultants in April 2025, as well as GIS mapping and household
surveys conducted in November 2024, preliminary estimates indicate that approximately 1,024 households—or around
6,211 individuals (calculated using average household sizes from the survey findings17)—will be impacted by the Project.
However, it is important to note that these figures are provisional. The current estimates, developed in consultation
with Municipal Enterprises, primarily account for pasture users in the reservoir area. Given local land use practices, it is
anticipated that some affected individuals may experience overlapping impacts, including:
     •      Loss of structures and crops within the reservoir area,
     •      Loss of access to pastureland due to the submersion of a key bridge, and
     •      Restrictions on pasture use resulting from the establishment of the Water Protection Zone (WPZ).
Affected Entities. In addition to the 1,024 affected households, two entities will be affected by the land acquisition for
the Project, including Toktokul State Forestry Enterprise, and the Zhoon – Terek Hunting Enterprise.
A comprehensive census survey of all affected households and inventory of losses will be conducted during the
preparation of the LRRPs. This will ensure a more precise determination of the total number of affected households and
help account for any overlapping losses.
Affected Land Areas. According to GIS mapping and calculations based on AFRY’s latest project design (June 2025),
approximately 7,722.04 hectares (equivalent to 77.22 km2) of land will be affected by the Project's key components.
The breakdown is as follows:
     •      Reservoir area: 6,170 hectares will be required at a water level of 1,198 meters.
     •      Main construction site and associated facilities: Approximately 12.56 hectares will be acquired for the dam
            and HPP components and 114.26 hectares will be affected due to various options of auxiliary works.
     •      Associated facilities: A total of 31.22 hectares, including:
                  o     Access Road: 28.62 hectares
                  o     Bridge over the Naryn River: 0.41 hectares
                  o     110kV Overhead Transmission Line Towers & Substation: 1.36 hectares
                  o     Other Infrastructure (e.g., water tank, treatment plant, general service facilities): 0.83 hectares
                  o     Tunnel (part of the access road): 1.38 km in length

10.5.1.14.3 Impacts due to Auxiliary Works
According to the AFRY (2025e), potential sites for auxiliary works—including the crushing plant, workshop, work camp,
and quarry site—have been identified. These components are expected to require approximately 114.26 hectares of
land (approximately 1.14 km2), primarily located in Toluk and Imanaliev AAs.
The identified land includes 76.19 hectares of pasture and 38.07 hectares of other public land. The number of affected
people in these areas remains undetermined at the time of this report. However, given the local pasture use practices,
it is expected that those utilizing this land will be accounted for in the total number of pasture users affected by the
reservoir area. A comprehensive inventory of losses will be conducted during the preparation of the LRRP for the main
construction site to identify overlapping land use and determine the total number of affected households.
It should be noted that the final selection of sites has not yet been determined, and it remains unclear whether all
identified locations will be used. As a result, the total area of affected land may change significantly once final decisions
are made. Additionally, it is still unknown whether these areas will be temporarily or permanently acquired from the
local community, pending further clarification at the later Project stage.

10.5.1.14.5 Impacts due to Worker Camps
The construction of the Project will require a large workforce over nearly a decade, peaking at approximately 7,000
workers, most of whom will be migrant workers. At the time of the ESIA preparation, the final location of the workers’
camp had not yet been determined. Possible locations include: i) Ketmen Tobo Village; ii) an affected village (Nickhe-
Sai); or iii) the dam site.
      •    Ketmen-Tobo
Ketmen-Tobo is an urban-type settlement located downstream of the Toktogul reservoir. It previously hosted a workers’
camp during the construction of the Kambarata-2 HPP between 2007 and 2010. The village was originally planned by
Soviet authorities in 1991 as a future city with a capacity of 40,000 people. However, it only reached a peak population
of 5,000 during the Kambarata-2 HPP construction. As of 2021, the population had declined to 3,263 due to limited job
opportunities.
Local authorities strongly support hosting the Kambarata-1 HPP workers’ camp, given its proximity—only 25 km from
the K-1 HPP construction site. They have identified available municipal-owned urban space suitable for the camp and
existing public infrastructure, including a school, to accommodate workers and their families. However, some of this
infrastructure requires substantial renovation, and the municipality faces financial constraints in maintaining it.
      •    Nichke-Sai Village
During initial consultations, some local communities expressed interest in hosting the workers’ camp, viewing it as an
opportunity for economic development. This perspective was particularly emphasized by local authorities in Nichke-Sai,
which is expected to be one of the most affected communities, with over 2,000 hectares—primarily pastureland—
expected to be inundated. Local authorities believe that establishing the workers’ camp in Nichke-Sai would provide
economic benefits to the affected population and have identified available land for its construction.



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3             Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                               SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                            11 August 2025                                Page 123
Description of Impacts



       •   Dam site
Locating the workers’ camp at the dam site would eliminate the need for worker transportation and allow for greater
control over the workforce due to its isolated location. However, this option presents significant challenges, as there is
currently no existing infrastructure to accommodate workers and their families. Additionally, it may require a larger
land acquisition to develop the necessary facilities.
Currently, there is no precise information on the land area required for each option. The PMO is assessing the potential
sites. Once a final decision is made, an analysis of land impacts will be conducted. Any land acquisition and associated
impacts will be managed in accordance with the Project’s LRRF, LRRP, and environmental management plans.

Findings from consultations conducted by Pasture Management Consultants with Toluk and Sary-Kamysh AAs indicated
that no cases were identified in which residents possessed documents confirming ownership or long-term use rights to
land within the inundation zone. A representative from the Toktogul District State Registration Service explained that
the Project has been planned since the Soviet era, and the inundation zone was officially transferred to the ownership
of OJSC “Electric Power Plants” in 2013. As a result, no legal ownership or use rights could have been issued for these
parcels of land.
However, during consultations in the villages of Nichke-Sai and Chorgochu, residents reported that three farming
households may hold such documentation.
One household, a representative of the “Turuk” farming enterprise (formerly part of the “Zhundubai” farm), was
allocated land under Decision No. 3 of the Nichke-Sai local council dated May 13, 1998. This decision granted him 3
hectares of irrigated land and 5 hectares of rainfed land. The approximate location of the land currently used by his
household has been mapped. The second household was allocated a 1-hectare plot in the Karagandy-Kolot area, but
the duration of the land-use right remains unclear.
Additionally, during the meeting, two State Acts were presented, confirming rights to ownership and permanent use of
lands from the special fund in the Uch-Terek area for agricultural purposes. These documents will require further legal
review to assess their validity in the context of potential compensation.
The land within the reservoir area also includes parcels belonging to the Toktogul State Forestry Enterprise, as well as
hunting grounds assigned to Zhoon-Terek LLC, which operates under a contract with the authorized state body
responsible for hunting and game management.
Land Type. The land that will be submerged comprises various types, including pastureland, agricultural land, forestry
land, shrubland, and others.

The following sections present a detailed description of the various anticipated impacts due to the reservoir area.
Loss of Pastureland
Based on GIS mapping calculations using Pasture Management Consultants land use data and the AFRY Project
design as of May 2025, the total pastureland affected is estimated at approximately 3,538.47 hectares (equivalent to
35.38 km2). This includes land from E. Imanaliev (1,869.72 hectares, 52.84%), Sary-Kamysh (834.35 hectares,
23.58%), Toluk (825.82 hectares, 23.34%), and other AAs (8.58 hectares, 0.24%). These figures are preliminary
estimates based on currently available data and will be verified during the LRRP preparation for the reservoir area.
Table 10-39 below shows the loss percentage of pastureland due to the planned reservoir formation in relation to the
total pastures area of each affected AA, based on the figures reported in the Socio-economic Passports (2025). The data
indicates that Toluk AA will experience the highest relative loss, with 4.74% of its total pasture area—equivalent to
825.82 hectares—falling within the reservoir zone. This is closely followed by Sary-Kamysh AA, where 834.35 hectares,
or 4.03% of the total pastureland, will be affected. E. Imanaliev AA will see a lower relative loss despite the highest
absolute area affected (1,869.72 hectares), as this represents only 2.48% of its much larger total pasture area of 75,505
hectares. While the total pasture area for the remaining AAs is not available, the affected land area from this group is
minimal at 8.58 hectares, and therefore, its overall contribution to the impact is considered minor.

Although the land is legally owned by “Electric Power Plants” OSJC, it continues to be used in practice as winter pasture
by residents of the Sary-Kamysh, Toluk, Imanaliev, and Atai AAs. During the meeting between a specialist from the
Toktogul District Department of State Registration and Pasture Management Consultants, it was noted that despite the
official transfer of ownership, these land parcels have not yet been removed from the state land inventory. This indicates
that the changes in ownership have not been fully reflected in the national land reporting system.
According to local pasture users, these lands have long served as traditional winter grazing areas, typically used from
November to March. Due to their proximity to the Naryn River, the local microclimate remains relatively mild and snow-
free during the winter months. These favorable conditions are especially important during the calving and lambing
season, prompting herders to continue using the area for winter livestock grazing.
Local communities and livestock owners can access and utilize these pastures through a formal contractual system. The
management and use of pasturelands are regulated under the Law on Pastures and overseen by local self-governments,
particularly Ayil Aimaks (rural municipalities), which allocate pasture areas based on community needs.Villagers can
obtain a one-year pasture use contract on behalf of their families and use pastures, which grants them legal access to
designated grazing areas. These contracts are issued by the Municipal Enterprises and are renewable annually based on
the register from pasture users. In some cases, livestock owners register for pasture use through shepherds, who act as
intermediaries. Shepherds often manage communal grazing areas and lease pastures on behalf of multiple livestock
owners. They coordinate grazing schedules, collect pasture fees, and register the livestock under their care with
Municipal Enterprises.
According to the data provided by Municipal Enterprises and annual livestock data collected by Pasture Management
Consultants in April 2025, the pasture areas are being used by 1,024 households with 6,211 persons. Among the 1,024
potentially affected households (AHs), 145 households have formal pasture use contracts with Municipal Enterprises
and 879 are reported not having any contracts (Table 10-43). It is noted that in Atai AA, out of 25 AHs, three shepherds
have signed pasture use contracts and are responsible for collecting and grazing cattle and sheep from 22 AHs.
In terms of payment, fees for pasture use are typically paid in two ways: (i) through the local bank: Payments are
deposited into the respective Ayil Aimak’s official bank account and (ii) in cash - payments are made directly to the

 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                                   Page 128
Description of Impacts



shepherd or Municipal Enterprises. Fees collected from pasture contracts contribute to pasture maintenance,
infrastructure development, and sustainably efforts.

The reservoir formation will lead to the loss of winter pastures and restrict access to higher-altitude areas. While initial
assessments suggested the remaining land would be too steep for grazing, discussions with local residents conducted
by the Pasture Management Consultants indicated otherwise. To investigate this, the Pasture Management Consultants
conducted analysis of a Slope Steepness Classified Map, derived from the 30m SRTM DEM (dwtkns.com/srtm30m). This
analysis identified areas with slopes under 35o, confirming the potential for some continued winter grazing above the
inundation zone.




                                       Figure 10-14: Slope Classification Map of of the Project Area

                                   Source: Pasture Report (Pasture Management Consultants, 2025)
Loss of Agricultural Land
Based on GIS mapping calculations using Pasture Management Consultants land use data and the AFRY Project design
as of May 2025, approximately 2.99 hectares of agricultural land will be inundated once the reservoir is filled. This
includes 0.19 hectares in Sary-Kamysh AA and 2.8 hectares in E. Imanaliev AA. At this stage, satellite imagery analysis
alone does not allow for a definitive classification of the affected areas as agricultural land without ground verification,
particularly in Sary-Kamysh. It is likely that local people cultivate fodder/hay for the animal; vegetables and other crops
and fruit trees, for their own consumption, on public land. The extent of agricultural land loss will be confirmed through
a full inventory of losses to be conducted during the preparation of the LRRP for reservoir area.
The number of affected people in these areas remains undetermined at the time of this report. However, given the local
pasture use practices, it is expected that those utilizing this land will be accounted for in the total number of pasture
users affected by the reservoir area. A comprehensive census survey will be conducted during the preparation of the
LRRP to identify overlapping land use and determine the total number of affected households and ownership of these
land plots.

Loss of Access to Pastureland
Beyond the direct loss of pastureland due to reservoir submergence, villagers in Toluk AA may also struggle to get access
their remaining grazing areas once the reservoir is filled. Assessment conducted by the Pasture Management
Consultants in March 2025 in consultation with local authorities revealed that in addition to the pastures that will be
submerged, 1,394 hectares of winter pastures, known as Achyk-Termirkent, may become inaccessible due to the loss of
horse trails and a key bridge over Toluk river. Seven (7) pasture users are currently using this area. This bridge has long
served as a vital connection for Toluk residents between Toluk village and its grazing territories (see Figure 10-17).
However, it is important to note that the pastures located on the left bank of the Toluk River are characterized by steep
and rugged terrain. These areas have already been deemed unsuitable for livestock grazing due to their limited
accessibility. As such, the restoration of access to these left-bank pasturelands is not considered necessary. Efforts to
restore access should therefore prioritize the route leading to the remaining viable pasture areas on the right side of
the river.
Based on local land use practices, some of the pasture users losing access to winter pastures are expected to overlap
with those affected by the reservoir's submergence in Toluk AA. To ensure an accurate assessment of the total affected
households and account for overlapping losses, a comprehensive census survey will be conducted during the
preparation of the LRRP.
Since winter pastures are the most valuable and their replacement is highly challenging, the loss of access could have
critical adverse impacts on local livelihoods if not properly mitigated and managed.




                                     Figure 10-17: Map of Pasture with Lost Access in Toluk AA
                                Source: Pasture Report (Pasture Management Consultants, May 2025)
The officially approved methodology for geobotanical surveys of natural forage lands in the Kyrgyz Republic (Order No.
256, Ministry of Agriculture, Water Resources and Regional Development, dated on August 2, 2021, classifies pastures
with slopes exceeding 45o as inaccessible. However, a detailed map of pastures in the Toluk AA reveals winter grazing
areas with slopes less than 35o above the projected inundation zone, suggesting their continued usability. During
consultations held in March and April 2025, community members strongly expressed concerns about losing access to
the winter pasture and proposed constructing an alternative road along the mountain slopes to maintain access. In line
with the World Bank’s ESS5 requirements to avoid or minimize resettlement through exploring project design



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                   Page 135
Description of Impacts



alternatives, the restoration of access routes is essential to prevent unnecessary disruption to livelihoods. Feasible
options to fully restore access to Termikent area have been identified, consulted with local pasture users and local
authorities, and presented in Chapter 11 and Pasture Report. These access restoration measures are critical to ensuring
that livestock grazing activities can continue and that economic displacement is avoided or minimized.




           Figure 10-18: Slope Steepness Map Source: Pasture Report (Pasture Management Consultants, 2025)

Loss of Structures
This section outlines the potential loss of private structures within the community. For impacts on graves, please refer
to Section 10.5.7, and for impacts on public infrastructure, see Section 10.5.3 of this report.
Consultations with affected communities and village heads conducted in October and November 2024 indicate that
there are no major permanent structures within the reservoir area. However, secondary structures exist to support local
grazing, including huts, sheepfolds, sheds, barns, fences, and trough pens. Herders primarily use these structures for
most of the year when they relocate from villages or towns during the winter season (October to March). While many
of these structures are scattered, some are clustered in areas with better road access and proximity to water sources.


According to the consultations with Municipal Enterprises and local communities, 41 structures owned by 41 households
(245 persons) will be potentially affected 18. Data from the household survey conducted in October and November 2024
indicated that among the 337 surveyed households, 13 households with 91 persons claimed that their livestock pens,
primarily made of wood are located within the reservoir area of the Project. These households include 6 in Sary-Kamysh
AA, 4 in Atai AA, and 3 in Toluk AA. The number of potentially affected structures, the number of affected households
and persons, purpose of use, severity of impacts (partial loss or full loss) will be validated during LRRP preparation.
According to the estimates and initial identification of the Municipal Enterprises, these 41 structures are categorized
into three categories: 18 tahm, 14 kepeh or 9 zhehr toloh. Below is the description of each type of structure in terms of
size, materials of walls, roof, and floor. Table 10-44Table 10-44 presents the number of potentially affected structures
in each AA based on initial identification of Municipal Enterprises. Figure 10-18 shows photos of typical affected



18
  The estimation of the number of persons have been done on the basis of the average household size (5.5) of the area
derived from Census and the household survey results. In the absence of ownership records, it has been assumed that each
structures identified have different ownership.

structures. It is noted that the affected structures are typically used by structure owners and their relatives during the
winter grazing season.
     •     Tahm is a small structure, typically measuring around 4×2 meters, built using clay bricks and stones. It usually
           consists of one or two rooms, with a roof made of reeds, wooden branches, and covered with a polyethylene
           plastic sheet or tarpaulin. Metal nails and wires are used to secure the roof frame. The structure has a single
           door with a padlock and one or two small windows covered with glass or a transparent plastic sheet. The floor
           is covered with a plastic sheet and reeds for insulation. Inside, there is a chimney and a simple fireplace for
           cooking. Tahm is primarily used by cattle owners when they bring their livestock for grazing.
     •     Kepeh or Zhehr Toloh is a semi-underground structure, usually consisting of a single room about 2×3 meters
           in size. The roof is constructed similarly to the Tahm, using reeds, wooden branches, and a polyethylene plastic
           sheet or tarpaulin, secured with metal nails and wires. It has one door with a padlock and sometimes a small
           window covered with a transparent plastic sheet. Clay and stones are often used to reinforce and elevate the
           sides of the structure.

Loss of Crops and Trees
Among the 337 surveyed households, 9 households (comprising 52 individuals) reported having crops and trees—such
as orchards and vegetables—within the planned reservoir area. Findings from Pasture Management Consultants
indicates that one household in E. Imanalieve reported having approximately 2,180 fruit trees – including walnut,
apricot, apple and peach trees) affected. Meanwhile satellite imaganery analysis conducted by CAICO identified another
household with an estimated 250-300 trees. In addition, a number of trees situated on land managed by the Toktogul
State Forestry Enterprise are expected to be affected. As noted above, the loss of these crops and trees will be verified
through the census and asset inventory that will be conducted during the preparation of the LRRP for the reservoir area.

Physical Displacement
It is noted that 3 out of the 1,024 potentially AHs reside in the affected pasture areas in E. Imanalieve.
     •     Household in Karagandy Kolot pasture area (6 persons): The pasture user and his family have been living there
           for four generations, maintaining a garden with apricot, apple, and walnut trees, as well as plots for growing
           potatoes, cucumbers, melons, and watermelons (Figure 10-23).
     •     Household in Bash Tunduk pasture area (9 persons): In addition to the AH, other 3 households who are their
           relatives (father and two brothers of the household head) use some of their properties for grazing livestock to
           make a living. However, these three households reside in other places (Figure 10-24).
     •     Household in E. Imanaliev (the number of persons is not available): This household is under assessment (Figure
           10-25).
As a result of submergence due to the reservoir, the three households of Imanaliyev AA will be physically displaced.

Economic Displacement
The inundation of pastureland due to the reservoir will affect both herding and beekeeping activities of local residents
and operation of the hunting ground of Zhoon-Terek LLC.
Livestock herding
As presented in Section 7.1, in the AAs affected by the Project, the majority of residents practice semi-subsistence
farming, with livestock rearing being the primary occupation and the main income source. Cattle are often sold live, and
livestock production accounts for 90-95% of household earnings. However, the reservoir will directly submerge
approximately 35.38 km2 of grazing land, 11 bridges and around 83.42 km of horse and footpaths. It is estimated that
1,021 pasture users and 3 shepherds will be directly affected by the inundation, with approximately 21,950 livestock—
including cattle, horses, sheep, and goats—losing their grazing areas (Table 10-46). Among the affected Ayil Aimaks,
Toluk is expected to be the most severely affected by pasture loss due to inundation. Approximately 14.25% of its winter
pastures—which are critical for sustaining livestock during the winter —and 1.34% of its spring-autumn pastures fall
within the reservoir area. In Sary-Kamysh, the reservoir formation will affect 8.57% of winter pastures and 3.56% of
spring-autumn pastures. For E. Imanaliev, the impact is more moderate, with 9.04% of spring-autumn pastures and 0.6%
of winter pastures expected to be submerged. As a result, herders will be forced to seek alternative grazing areas.
However, FGD findings revealed that existing pastures are already overburdened by overgrazing and degradation.
Farmers in Toluk and Kyzyl-Ozgorush, for example, have reported declining grass quality and shrinking pasturelands.
The relocation of livestock will pose further challenges, leading to increased costs for securing new pasture areas.
Additionally, the loss of traditional grazing routes, combined with poor road infrastructure, will limit livestock mobility
and market access, making it more difficult for farmers to sell live animals and dairy products. Without proper mitigation
measures, these pressures may force herders to reduce herd sizes, directly impacting household incomes. Additionally,
the impact will also cause disruption of these essential movements. An area of 1,394 hectares of winter pasture will
remain unaffected by submersion from the reservoir but may become inaccessible due to the loss of footpaths and the
bridge in Toluk. The number of affected users has yet to be determined.
In addition to these challenges, the loss of winter and spring-autumn pastures is expected to trigger a chain of
consequences that could disrupt both ecological balance and socio-economic stability in rural communities. Anticipated
effects include a growing shortage of winter fodder, increased pressure on near-village pastures—leading to accelerated
degradation—and a shift toward stall-feeding practices, which will significantly raise livestock production costs. These
impacts will severely challenge the rural economy, especially livestock-dependent households, potentially weakening
traditional livelihoods and threatening food and economic security in these regions.

The Pasture Report (Pasture Management Consultants, 2025) provides an assessment of current grazing pressure across
seasonal pastures and evaluates projected impacts due to pasture loss from the Project. The data indicate a moderate
overall increase in grazing pressure following the reduction in available pastureland. Notably, winter pastures in Toluk
and Sary-Kamysh AAs are expected to face a significant rise in grazing intensity, exacerbating the already critical overuse
of these areas. For example, in Toluk, the winter pasture livestock load is expected to increase from 1.67 LU/ha to 1.89
LU/ha due to the reduction in available winter pastureland. Similarly, in Sary-Kamysh, the winter livestock load will rise
from 2.90 LU/ha to 3.64 LU/ha. These findings highlight the urgent need for targeted mitigation measures and
sustainable grazing management strategies to prevent pasture degradation and ensure long-term viability.

In March and April 2025, the Pasture Management Consultants carried out a site visit and consultations to assess the
impact of the reservoir on pasture use. Several key concerns emerged during these discussions, which should be
carefully considered when developing mitigation measures for pastureland impacts.
Toluk AA:
     •     According to consultation with the local authorities, in addition to pastures that will be submerged due to
           flooding, other pastures may become inaccessible due to the loss of a key bridge over the tributary, which
           connects Toluk residents to their winter pastures. Winter pastures are irreplaceable, as no other similar
           quality pastures (capable of feeding the animals during the wintertime) are available in the area. Therefore,
           the reservoir inundation will significantly impact the livelihoods of these villagers if the mitigation measures
           are not well managed.
     •     Replacing these winter pastures remains highly challenging. Even with improved access roads, concerns
           persist about whether the remaining pastures will be sufficient to support livestock. A geobotanical study has
           been conducted to assess the carrying capacity of the remaining grazing areas.
     •     Several potential solutions have been discussed, including preparing hay for winter feeding, stable-based
           breeding and sheltering livestock in barns, sending livestock to summer pastures to ease the pressure on
           winter grazing, transitioning from cows to yaks. However, all these options are costly, complex, and may not
           fully compensate for the loss of winter pastures.
     •     Given the severity of this issue, a comprehensive pasture capacity assessment is required to determine
           whether alternative grazing areas can offset the loss of winter pastures. Additionally, technical feasibility
           studies must be conducted in close collaboration with experts to explore solutions for restoring access to
           these lands. A detailed evaluation of the costs, practicality, and impact of different mitigation measures is
           necessary.
     •     Since this is a primary concern for Toluk residents, it is essential to prioritize the technical assessment and
           develop a mitigation plan for restoring access to winter pastures. These findings should be presented in the
           second round of upstream consultations with the Toluk community to ensure informed decision-making and
           community participation.
Imanaliev AA:
     •     In Imanaliev AA, while a significant portion of pastureland will be submerged by the reservoir, the affected
           areas primarily consist of summer and spring-autumn pastures. Given the availability of these pasture types
           in the region, the impact on pasture-based livelihoods is less severe compared to Toluk AA.
     •     During consultations with local authorities, a few proposals were made to address pasture access, aside from
           constructing a 100 km road to the San and Zhoon Terek summer pastures. However, this proposal may face
           opposition from the Zhoon Terek hunting company, which has raised concerns about potential disturbances
           to wildlife.
     •     To support pasture livelihoods in E. Imanaliev AA, recommended measures include transitioning to
           alternative livelihoods, such as developing tourism and establishing sewing workshops for women to create
           additional income opportunities. Additionally, infrastructure improvements, particularly providing electricity
           to barns, could help facilitate year-round pasture use.
Sary-Kamysh AA:




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3        Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                          SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                       11 August 2025                                Page 143
Description of Impacts



     •     When discussing the loss of winter pastures, local stakeholders in Sary-Kamysh AA did not express major
           concern because they see other grazing alternatives. While losing pastures is not ideal, they believe that with
           proper support and access improvements, they can adapt to other available grazing lands.
     •     To address these concerns, stakeholders emphasized the importance of negotiating with forestry authorities
           to obtain land for grazing. Additionally, constructing new roads to provide access to alternative pastures was
           seen as a crucial solution. Once these roads are in place, the loss of current winter pastures can be mitigated,
           allowing communities to continue their livestock practices with minimal disruption
     •     Assistance is required for pastoralists to relocate pasture users may need 1–2 years of support to facilitate
           their transition to new pastures before achieving self-sufficiency.
In addition to the specific issues identified for each AA, several broader challenges may complicate the development of
the LRRPs:
     •     During the socio-economic surveys in November 2024, local residents highlighted ongoing conflicts between
           villages over pasture use. In addition, disputes exist between pasture committees and the forestry service
           over several pasture areas across the affected AAs. (The disputes relate to land whose ownership belongs to
           the forestry service, but there are no designated national state parks or Protected Areas in the study area.
           Further, the Project is not introducing any restrictions of access to any Protected Areas.) These conflicts may
           create obstacles for the Project when verifying actual pasture users and determining eligibility for
           compensation.
     •     Due to the nomadic nature of many pasture users, direct interviews were difficult to conduct, leading to
           reliance on village leaders for representation. However, this approach risks overlooking individual
           perspectives and specific needs. Some proposed solutions may not align with the practical realities of
           pastoral communities, as many suggestions come from individuals who do not directly use the pastures.
     •     In public meetings, community elders and leaders were nominated to speak on behalf of villagers, as many
           residents lacked the technical knowledge to articulate complex proposals. While this approach was
           considered democratic and culturally appropriate, village leaders often requested additional time to
           formulate and refine their community’s needs due to the legal weight of formally documented requests.
These factors underscore the importance of a careful and inclusive consultation process to ensure that individual needs
and perspectives are accurately captured in the resettlement planning.
Beekeeping
Similarly, the inundation of pastureland due to the reservoir will further exacerbate the challenges already faced by
beekeepers in the affected communities. Beekeepers in the study area already face challenges of water scarcity (the
lack of irrigation and water access significantly impacts honey production by limiting the growth of flowering plants
essential for bees), poor road infrastructure (poor road networks make it difficult for beekeepers to access pastures or
fields rich in wild plants, further restricting honey production), and disruption of wild plant ecosystems (these are at
risk due to erosion, drought, and environmental degradation).
Given that 5% to 20% of households in the affected AAs engage in beekeeping, with higher dependence in certain
villages like Toluk (where it contributes up to 30% of household incomes), the loss of pastureland and associated
vegetation will directly impact honey production. The submergence of flowering plants, wild herbs, and natural
ecosystems critical for bee foraging will reduce nectar availability, potentially leading to lower honey yields and
increased competition for remaining foraging sites. Additionally, environmental disturbances caused by reservoir filling,
including changes in microclimates and potential dust pollution from construction activities, may stress bee colonies,
leading to migration or decreased productivity.
During the March 2025 Field Visit, it was confirmed that beekeepers in Sary-Kamysh are not impacted by the Project.
Similarly, participants in a FGD held in Toluk AA in April 2025 reported that their beekeeping sites are not located near
the planned reservoir or project area. However, they expressed concern based on local historical knowledge. Elderly
villagers recalled that prior to the Toktogul HPP construction, surrounding lands were covered with vegetation, which
disappeared after the reservoir was built, leaving the area dry and barren. Fearing a repeat of this scenario, Toluk
residents are deeply concerned about the potential loss of diverse grasses and wildflowers critical to their beekeeping
practices. They also worry about possible microclimatic changes, including reductions in surface and groundwater
availability, and the broader environmental consequences such shifts might trigger.
Hunting activities


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007

 A portion of the hunting grounds managed by Zhoon-Terek LLC will be submerged due to the formation of the reservoir.
The affected area is located on the left bank of the Naryn River, near the Nichke-Sai region. During a field mission
conducted in March 2025, the Pasture Management Consultants met with a representative of Zhoon-Terek LLC to
discuss the potential impacts. According to the representative, the construction of the K-1 HPP is not expected to
significantly affect the core hunting areas. However, the company expressed concern about a potential increase in
poaching: once the reservoir is filled, previously inaccessible areas may become reachable by boat, potentially providing
easier access for illegal hunting activities.

10.5.1.14.7 Significance of Impacts
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact – High (60): The Project will directly affect approximately 1,024 households—or around
           6,211 individuals—representing about 30% of the total population. Communities living and working within
           the Project’s area of influence are highly dependent on pastureland for their livelihoods and way of life. The
           land acquisition for the Project will result in loss of a significant area of winter pastures, which are the most
           valuable and difficult to replace. In addition, the Project will cause loss of community infrastructures, leading
           the loss of access to pastures.
     •     Extent of Impact – Regional (60): The effects will extend beyond the immediate project footprint, impacting
           three districts and covering areas more than 5 km outside the project site. Given its scale, the Project
           engages regional stakeholders and may trigger broader community concerns, justifying a Regional extent
           classification.
     •     Duration of Impact – Long-term (20): While the primary impact occurs during the construction phase, its
           consequences will persist long-term unless effective mitigation measures are implemented.
     •     Overall: Considering the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse
           impacts of land acquisition are classified as Critical (140) adverse if not adequately mitigated and managed.

10.5.1.15 Phase 3 – Operation Phase
Loss of Access to Pastureland
The Pasture Report indicates that winter pastures located above the inundation zone along the Toluk River, with slopes
of less than 35 degrees, remain suitable for continued use. However, access to these areas will be lost, as detailed in
Section 10.5.5.2, raising concerns among local pasture users. Furthermore, as noted in Section 10.5.5.2.6, the filling of
the reservoir will cut off access to portions of winter pasture in both Sary-Kamysh AA and Toluk AA. This impact is
expected to persist throughout the operation phase.
Restriction on the Use of Pastureland within the Water Protection Zone (WPZ)
Once the reservoir is inundated and in use, there will also be further restriction on land use due to the WPZ. The WPZ
for the construction of the reservoir for the K-1 HPP is set at 500 meters, in accordance with the Regulation "On Water
Protection Zones and Strips of Water Bodies in the Kyrgyz Republic" (July 7, 1995, No. 271). As the reservoir's volume
exceeds 1,000 million cubic meters, the regulation mandates a minimum water protection zone width of 500 meters.
A WPZ is defined as the area adjacent to a water body where a special regime is enforced to prevent pollution, littering,
depletion, and siltation. This regime directly affects pasture users, as it prohibits several activities within WPZ and strips,
including:
     •     The establishment of livestock complexes and farms
     •     Cattle burial grounds and manure storage
     •     Burial sites
     •     Facilities for sheep washing and grazing
Additionally, within the coastal water protection strip, the following are not permitted:
     •     Summer cattle camps
     •     Recreation centers and tent camps



 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                       Page 145
Description of Impacts



      •    Parking of hunters' and tourists' vehicles
Based on the consultations carried out by Pasture Management Consultants with representatives from the Ministry of
Natural Resources, Ecology, and Technical Supervision, district administration, sanitary physician, Chief Physician of the
District Center for Disease Prevention and State Sanitary and Epidemiological Surveillance, all the participants confirmed
that, in the area surrounding the Toktogul reservoir, no restrictions on livestock grazing are currently enforced. In
practice, grazing occurs freely, and in dry years, when water levels recede, local residents cultivate the exposed land for
agricultural use. Therefore, it is anticipated that grazing around the K-1 HPP’s reservoir will not be significantly
restricted.
According to GIS mapping and calculations based on AFRY’s latest project design and data sets provided by Pasture
Management Consultants (May 2025), approximately 9,599 hectares (equivalent to 95.99 km2) of land will be affected
by the WPZ. The area is predominantly public land (including water bodies, shrubs land), covering approximately
4,916.08 hectares (51.21%), followed by pastureland at 4,683.07 hectares (48.79%). There is no other agricultural land
in the WPZ (see Table 10-48).

submergence and the affected structure owners will overlap, given local land use practices. To ensure a more accurate
assessment of the total number of affected households and account for any overlapping losses, a comprehensive census
survey will be conducted during the preparation of the LRRP for Reservoir Area.

Inclusion, Vulnerabilities and Gender
The area of influence has a population that lives in remote villages on mountainous terrain, living and working in
traditional ways. Therefore, vulnerable groups in the Project area will be highly sensitive to a large constructed project
and its induced changes that will take place over a decade.
Certain individuals and groups, such as those based on age, gender, ethnicity, religion, disability, social exclusion, or
economic disadvantage, may be more vulnerable to negative impacts from the Project. They are likely to face the risk
of social exclusion and not equally benefitting from the Project. Vulnerable groups identified include:
     •     Disabled people (those with physical or mental disabilities or chronic illnesses, who will require physical
           assistance)
     •     Female-headed households (as well as women acting as de facto head of households while the head of
           households is absent for labor migration or other reasons)
     •     Elderly people with no younger family members within their household
     •     Poor households receiving monthly allowances
The household survey conducted in the study area identified 16.3% of households (55 out of 337 households) as
vulnerable; these were households with disabled persons and female-headed households. 46 of these households
received social assistance or benefits. The Area of Influence is ethnically and religiously homogenous.
In FGDs conducted with women, they stated that they had positive expectations of the Project however, hoping that
the Project will provide them with positive benefits in employment, education and improved community infrastructure.

10.5.1.17 Construction Phase
To the extent that women are employed in the construction phase of the Project, they will be working in a heavily male
dominiated workforce, which can lead to discrimination, unequal pay, sexual exploitation, abuse and harassments risks
for them in the workplace.




 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3           Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                             SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                          11 August 2025                             Page 148
Description of Impacts



Other vulnerable groups, such as households with disabilities, may be negatively affected if the livelihood restoration
planning does not include specialised livelihood measures and provide them with timely assistance in the face of impacts
and changes caused by the Project.
The gender related impacts have been evaluated based on the FGDs conducted for this ESIA. It is important to note
that risks and impacts will change over time and so need to be continuously monitored throughout the Project lifecycle,
including new and evolving impacts and the effectiveness of mitigation measures.
Potential adverse risks and impacts for women and vulnerable groups include:
Health, safety, and well-being:
     •     Labor influx increases risk of gender-based violence, including SEA/SH as well as risk of communicable and
           sexually transmitted diseases
     •     Inadequate worker accommodation increases risks of safety for women and vulnerable groups
     •     Employment opportunities present health and safety risks for women and vulnerable groups
Employment and Economic Inequality:
     •     Women and vulnerable groups may experience discrimination in hiring processes and unequal pay for equal
           work.
     •     A lack of appropriate workplace policies, procedures, and grievance mechanisms makes it harder for them to
           report unsafe working conditions or sensitive issues.
Land Acqusition, Livelihoods, and Compensation
     •     Women and vulnerable groups may be disproportionally disadvantaged or adversely affected in proving
           ownership or use rights for land, assets, pastures.
     •     Women and vulnerable groups may have more difficulty accessing compensation and financial institutions due
           to systemic barriers or lack of documentation.
     •     Resettlement or restrictions on land access can undermine the livelihoods of vulnerable groups and women
           who rely on informal or subsistence-based activities, thereby impacting food security for those people.
     •     Livelihood restoration and benefit sharing processes may present competition for resources resulting in conflict
           that increase the vulnerability of women
Social Inclusion and Decision-Making
     •     Factors including social and cultural norms, care responsibilities, and limited mobility may result in women and
           vulnerable groups being excluded from participating in community consultations and decision-making spaces.
     •     Even when included, women’s voices may carry less weight, as men typically represent households in
           community meetings.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60) because the VEC (women and vulnerable groups) are assessed as
           highly sensitive to a large construction project.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Local (20) since the effects will mainly occur on the women and vulnerable groups
           within the Project’s directly affected communities.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20): Although the construction phase is expected to last 8.5 years, the
           effects on women and vulnerable groups could persist for 10–20 years or more since disruptions to
           livelihoods, land ownership, economic opportunities, and social structures can have long-term consequences,
           particularly for those who already face systemic disadvantages.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           on women and vulnerable groups are assessed as High (100) adverse if not properly mitigated and
           managed.
On the positive side, the Project will provide direct and indirect employment opportunities for the local economy which
can benefit vulnerable groups in the Project area. In addition, livelihood restoration and benefit sharing measures will


 Draft of Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Report. Volume II. Book 2.3   Client Reference No. 5018007
 Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project                     SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
 Prepared for Ministry of Energy, Kyrgyz Republic                                  11 August 2025                   Page 149
Description of Impacts



include direct investment into local infrastructure and skill development opportunities for affected communities. These
measures will identify differentiated mechanisms to addres the needs of women and vulnerable groups.
Potential positive impacts for women and vulnerable groups include:
     •     Livelihood restoration and benefit sharing mechanisms can present employment and economic empowerment
           opportunities for women and vulnerable groups seeking work or skills development
     •     The Project can improve water scarcity, road infrastructure and electrical infrastructure making access to
           markets, mobility and health outcomes better for women and vulnerable groups
     •     Investments and commitments made in benefit sharing can lead to positive outcomes and shared benefits for
           women and vulnerable groups

10.5.1.18 Operation Phase
As the Project transitions to the operation phase, the construction workforce will decrease significantly from a peak of
7,000 workers during peak construction to approximately 500-1000 workers. This sharp decrease significantly mitigates
the potential adverse risks and impacts on women and vulnerable groups that are associated with large-scale labor
influx. Concerns such as increased pressure on local resources, potential for social disruption, and heightened risks of
gender-based violence, will be substantially lessened during the operational period.
However, the gender-wise impacts stemming from changes in livelihoods, land ownership, economic opportunities, and
social structures, which may have arised during the construction phase, will persist as long-term consequences. If these
impacts were not adequately addressed and mitigated during construction, they will continue to affect women and
vulnerable groups throughout the operational phase. Therefore, it is imperative to ensure that robust mitigation and
management strategies are implemented to address these enduring socio-economic impacts.
Additionally, gender-related concerns in labor management, working conditions, and occupational health and safety
will remain relevant during the operation phase. Despite the smaller workforce, it is crucial to ensure equal employment
opportunities for women, address their workplace needs, and implement policies that promote gender equality and
prevent discrimination. Adequate training, safe working conditions, and tailored occupational health measures are
essential to protect both male and female workers in the hydropower plant environment.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude of Impact is ranked High (60) because the VEC (women and vulnerable groups) are assessed as
           highly sensitive to a large construction project.
     •     Extent of Impact is scored Site-specific (10) as it primarily affects the women workforce within the Project
           footprint.
     •     Duration of Impact is Long-term (20) as it will happen throughout the operation phase which is expected to
           last more than 20 years.
     •     Overall: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential adverse impacts
           of labor influx are assessed as Moderate (90) adverse if not properly mitigated and managed.

A further initial baseline assessment of cultural heritage sites was conducted by National Cultural Heritage Specialist
team through systematic field surveys, community consultations, and archival research in April and May 2025. This
identified a diverse range of archaeological features across the project area as shown in Figure 10-26 including:
     •     Ancient burial mounds (kurgans), often marked by surface stones, which serve as the final resting places of
           ancestors and are respected by local communities as sacred sites.
     •     Ritual and sacrificial sites, where evidence such as animal bones, ash deposits, and charcoal fragments suggest
           ceremonial practices.
     •     Rock art sites, notably the Teke-Tash, Chiyin-Tash, and Ailampa locations, which depict stylized animals,
           hunting scenes, and symbolic motifs.
     •     Remnants of irrigation systems and agricultural features, indicating historical patterns of land use and
           adaptation to the riverine environment.
     •     Traditional settlement areas and dwellings, where fragments of ceramics, tools, and structural remains
           illustrate past community life.

Based on their characteristics, vulnerability, and cultural significance, the identified archaeological sites are documented
in the Cultural Heritage Management Plan (CHMP) and have been grouped into three management categories:
     1.    Sites Requiring Detailed Investigation and Documentation
Certain sites, such as Kokomeren-Naryn Burial Ground (1st millennia BC and 2nd millennia AD 1001 - 2000) (refer to
Figure 10-30), Keng-Tatyr Burial Mounds, and Karagandy-Kolot Complex, require additional archaeological research
before any disturbance or inundation. These sites have not yet been fully documented and must be studied in detail
through stratigraphic.
These investigations will need to be conducted in close collaboration with local communities, Ministry of Culture
representatives, and heritage specialists, prior to any construction or reservoir filling.




                                                Figure 10-30: Kokomeren-Naryn Burial Ground

     2.    Sites Requiring Protection, Registration, and Signage
Highly significant sites, such as Chiyin-Tash Rock Art Complex (refer to Figure 10-31) and Teke-Tash, must be formally
registered as national monuments under Kyrgyz law.
These sites require:
     •     The establishment of formal protection zones and buffer areas.
     •     Clear signage and fencing on the ground.
     •     Legal recognition and inclusion in the national heritage register.
No construction or land use activities are permitted within these designated zones, in accordance with Article 30 of the
Law on the Protection and Use of Historical and Cultural Heritage.

     3.    Sites for Documentation and Monitoring
Other sites, while important, are assessed as less vulnerable to direct impacts but require thorough documentation and
monitoring. These include:
     •     20th-century Kyrgyz cemeteries (refer to Figure 10-32).
     •     Scattered burial mounds in areas such as Sary-Kamysh, Toluk, and Orgo.

These sites will be recorded in the GIS database and regularly monitored during the construction and operation phases.

prior to reservoir filling, to ensure proper documentation, preservation measures, or, where applicable, relocation.

10.5.1.20 Phase 2 - Construction
During the construction there will be significant changes to the land surface resulting in the loss and destruction (due
to submergence) of a significant number of cultural heritage sites including loss of archaeological sites, impact to
protected and affected stites, other sites not yetr protected and changes ot the environmental setting in which these
sites are located.
Loss of archaeological sites

Archaeological sites within the K1 HPP area represent an invaluable and often fragile record of the region’s long and
complex history. These sites are tangible evidence of human settlement, cultural practices, and the relationship
between communities and the landscape over centuries. They reflect the spiritual, social, and economic life of past
generations and hold a deep significance for local communities and the broader cultural heritage of the Kyrgyz Republic.

Impact to protected and affected sites
The K1-HPP also contains several protected sites including:
     •     Kokomeren-Naryn burial ground, the confluence of two rivers (K1-HPP)
     •     The location of the Chiyin-Tash rock paintings (Kambarata 2 hydroelectric power station)
According to Articles 22 and 28 of the Law, the Kokomeren-Naryn and Chiyin-Tash burial grounds shall be included in
the list of monuments of national (republican) significance.
The lists of historical and cultural monuments of national significance are approved by the Government of the Kyrgyz
Republic upon the recommendation of the authorized state body for the protection of monuments. This body is
responsible for compiling a register of historical and cultural heritage sites that are at risk of degradation or loss, and
for taking appropriate measures to preserve them.
These sites of historical and cultural heritage are to be preserved and utilized for the spiritual and cultural development
of the people of the Kyrgyz Republic, as well as for scientific, educational, cultural, tourism, and excursion-related
purposes.
In accordance with Article 30 of the Law, protective zones must be established. These zones shall be designed under
the order of the authorized state body for the protection of monuments, in accordance with the legislation of the Kyrgyz
Republic. Within these protective zones, all types of construction and domestic activities are strictly prohibited.
During the development of master plans, detailed planning projects, infrastructure (e.g., roads and bridges), and
settlement expansion, the approved monument protection zones must be incorporated into such plans.

Impact to Protected Sites (legally recognized or recommended for registration)
Two sites have been formally recognized or recommended for inclusion in the national heritage register under Kyrgyz
legislation, namely:
     •     Kokomeren-Naryn Burial Ground – A large complex of burial mounds dating from the 1st and 2nd millennia
           AD1001 - 2000, located at the confluence of the Kokomeren and Naryn rivers. Although not yet officially
           registered, this site is recommended for protection as a monument of national (republican) significance.
     •     Chiyin-Tash Rock Art Complex – A unique collection of over 200 petroglyphs, located within the Kambarata-2
           HPP area, demonstrating high artistic and scientific value. This site is also recommended for formal protection
           under the Kyrgyz Law on Cultural Heritage.
These sites require the establishment of protective zones, legal registration, and visible demarcation in the field, in
accordance with Articles 22, 28, and 30 of the Law on the Protection and Use of Historical and Cultural Heritage.

Impact to Other Identified Sites (not yet legally protected)
The remaining sites, while not formally registered as legal heritage, are of significant cultural, historical, and scientific
value. These include a wide range of sites identified through systematic field surveys, archival research, and community
consultations.
Specifically, the following categories of sites have been identified as follows:
     •     Burial mound sites (e.g., Kokomeren-Naryn, Ken-Tatyr, Karagandy-Kolot, Bash-Tunduk, Sary-Kamysh, Toluk,
           Toluk Orgo, Koshko, Ailampa).
     •     Ritual and sacrificial areas (e.g., Bash-Tunduk, Ken-Tatyr).
     •     Rock art sites (e.g., Chiyin-Tash, Teke-Tash, Ailampa).
     •     Traditional cultural landscapes and features, such as livestock enclosures (e.g., Yzyryk cattle pen), semi-
           dugouts, and ethnographic structures (e.g., Bash-Tunduk).
While not all these sites are legally protected, they are culturally important, and some are located within the planned
inundation zone of the K1-HPP reservoir. This means they will be permanently submerged because of project activities.
Therefore, these sites must undergo detailed documentation, archaeological analysis, stratigraphic excavation where
feasible, and integration into the project GIS database prior to reservoir filling.
Table 10-55 provides a summary of key cultural heritage sites with K1 HPP project area that have not been registered.

This classification ensures a tailored and proportionate approach to managing cultural heritage impacts in line with
Kyrgyz legislation, World Bank ESS8 requirements, and international best practice. It provides a clear basis for prioritizing
mitigation efforts, aligning technical workstreams, and integrating cultural heritage management into the overall project
framework for Kambarata-1 HPP.

Changes in Environmental setting
The development of the K1 HPP, including the creation of the reservoir, will result in fundamental changes to the
environmental setting of identified cultural heritage sites.These changes include:
     •     The inundation of landscapes previously associated with cultural practices, such as the Teke-Tash site, as well
           as burial mounds and ritual areas located within the planned reservoir area.
     •     Alterations to access routes, viewsheds, and the broader cultural landscape, which may impact the spiritual
           and community value of certain sites.
The assessment confirms that the cultural heritage sites within the K1- HPP area represent a rich and diverse legacy of
human settlement, ritual practices, and traditional knowledge spanning from the Early Iron Age to the present day. Sites
such as the Kokomeren-Naryn Burial Ground and Chiyin-Tash Rock Art Complex are of exceptional significance, requiring
immediate protection and detailed documentation prior to any project activity.
Without appropriate mitigation, there is a risk of irreversible loss of high-value sites, particularly those located in the
planned inundation zone. Moderate vulnerability sites can be managed through careful documentation, monitoring,
and integration into the Cultural Heritage Management Plan. Low vulnerability sites should be respected, recorded, and
included in the project’s GIS database for long-term reference.
Through the implementation of a robust, site-specific management strategy, it is possible to safeguard the cultural
heritage of the Naryn and Djalalabad regions, ensuring its preservation for future generations.
Based on the significance scoring criteria outlined in Chapter 6:
     •     Magnitude High (60): Permanent loss of a range of significant cultural heritage sites that will either required
           relocation or will be destroyed during construction as result of dam submergence.
     •     Extent Regional (60): Many sites are of National significance and will require engagement with regional cultural
           authorities, heritage ministries,
     •     Duration is Long-term (20): Loss of cultural heritage sites in most cases is irreversible.
     •     Overall Impact Significance: Based on the combined scores for magnitude, extent, and duration, the potential
           adverse impacts on cultural heritage are assessed as Critical (140) if not properly managed.

                                         Cultural     The assessment confirms that the cultural heritage sites within the K1- HPP area represent a rich and
                                         Heritage     diverse legacy of human settlement, ritual practices, and traditional knowledge spanning from the Early
                                                      Iron Age to the present day. Sites such as the Kokomeren-Naryn Burial Ground and Chiyin-Tash Rock Art
                                                      Complex are of exceptional significance, requiring immediate protection and detailed documentation
                                                      prior to any project activity.
                                                      Without appropriate mitigation, there is a risk of irreversible loss of high-value sites, particularly
                                                      those located in the planned inundation zone.
